PETITION  FOR  A  WRIT  OF  CERTIORARI
TO  THE  SUPREME  COURT
OF  THE  STATE  OF  M) CHIGAN

Ronald  Harmelin,  petitioner  in pro per and  in forma  i.

respectfully  prays  that  a  Writ  of  Certiorari  issue  from
pauperis,
this  Honorable  United  States  Supreme  Court  to  review  the  decisions
of  the  Michigan  courts  in  this  case  on  constitutional  issues  of

a
5S
MG

major  importance.

QUESTIONS  PRESENTED  FOR  REVIEW

I.  WHETHER  THE  SEARCHES  AND SEIZURES  VIOLATED  THE
FOURTEENTH  AMENDMENT  AND THE TRIAL  COURT ERRED  BY
FAILING  TO SUPPRESS  THEIR FRUITS.

II.  WHETHER  PETITIONER  WAS  DENIED  THE  EFFECTIVE
ASSISTANCE OF COUNSEL  AND THE RIGHT  TO PLEAD  NOT
GUILTY  WHERE  DESPITE  HIS PLEA  OF NOT GUILTY,  HIS
ATTORNEY  FAILED  TO ARGUE  THAT  THE  TRIAL  RECORD
PROVIDED  A  BASIS  FOR FINDING  THE  DEFENDANT  NOT
GUILTY.

III.  WHETHER  THE MANDATORY  SENTENCE  OF LIFE IMPRISONMENT
WITHOUT  POSSIBILITY  OF  PAROLE  CONSTITUTES  CRUEL  AND
UNUSUAL  PUNISHMENT.

IV.  WHETHER  THE  STATE  COURT  OF  APPEALS'  LACK  OF
JURISDICTION  TO REVERSE  THEMSELVES  ON THEIR  OWN
MOTION  VIOLATED  PETITIONER'S  RIGHT  TO DUE PROCESS
OF  LAW.

Defendant-Petitioner  Ronald  Harmelin  was  convicted  of

possession  of  more  than  650  grams  of  cocaine  and  possession  of  a

firearm  in  the  course  of  a  felony,  in  a  bench  trial  in  the  Oakland

County  Circuit  Court,  a  state  court  of  Michigan,  before  the  Hon.

Gene

Schnelz.

On  April

30,

1987,

Judge  Schnelz

imposed  the

mandatory  sentences  required  by  statute:  life  imprisonment  without

possibility  of  parole,  with  2  years  consecutive  on  the  gun  charge

(ST-3,4).

I.  THE  SEARCHES  AND  SEIZURES  VIOLATED  THE  FOURTEENTH  AMENDMENT

AND  THE  TRIAL  COURT  ERRED  BY  FAILING  TO  SUPPRESS  THEIR  FRUITS.

Police  stopped  the  car  of  Defendant  Ronald  Harmelin  for

going  through  a  red  light  at  a  turn-around  without  stopping  (EH-

10).  Ultimately,  police  searched  his  person  and  the  trunk  of  his

car,  and  found  cocaine.  A  motion  to  suppress  the  fruits  of  the

searches  and  seizures  was  denied.

In  the  early  morning  hours  of  May  12,  1986,  two  Oak  Park

police  officers,  Calvin  Rix  and  Lawrence  Blakeney,  were  patrolling

in  a  marked  police  car  in  the  area  of  the  Embassy  Motel  in  the  City

of  Oak  Park,  and  at  2:45  a.m.  drove  into  the  motel's  parking  lot

to  check  for  stolen  vehicles.  The  orficers  observed  the  defendant's

car  exiting  the  parking  lot.  They  were  called  away  on  other  police

business.  They  observed  defendant's  car  entering  the  motel  parking

lot  at  about  4:00  a.m.

and  again  at  5:00  a.m.

In  the  three

sightings  of  the  defendant's  car,

the  officers  noticed  nothing

unusual  or  illegal  in  the  manner  in  which  the  defendant's  car  was

being  driven.  They  could  not  see  who  was  driving.

Shortly  after  5:00  a.m.,  the  officers  saw  defendant's  car

make  a  turn-around  without  making  a  complete  stop  at  a  red  light

(EH1-  8-10).

The  officers  activated  their  overhead  lights  and

defendant  pulled  over  in  a  prompt  fashion.  (EH1-25;  EH2-  7,23)

Officer  Rix  approached  the  driver  side  of  the  defendant's  car,

while  Officer  Blakeney  stayed  back  by  the  passenger  side  of  the

police  car  (EH2-25).  Officer  Rix  asked  the  defendant  for  his  driver

license,  registration  and  proof  of  insurance,  and  defendant  handed

him  the  three  items  (EH1-11).  Defendant  then  exited  the  car.

The  officers  could  not  remember  whether  they  ordered  him

out  of  the  car  (EH1-27),  although  Officer  Rix  testified  that  under
certain  circumstances,  he  would  ask  a  driver  to  exit  the  vehicle

(T-66).  These  circumstances  are  if  the  driver  takes  evasive  action,

movement  while  stopping  the  car,  prior  information  on  problems,  or

if  the driver is extremely nervous  ati, eee  ae

cnet mae 6t these bideanatbants  inks, bhaineann

at  the  time  the  defendant  was  still  in the  car  (T-68).

Defendant  was  not  observed  committing  any  crime,  and did

not threaten  the  officers  by word  or deed  or otherwise  act

suspiciously  (EH1-25,36,37;EH2-25,26,35;T-67,  68).

After  the defendant  was out  of  the car,  officer Rix vent
to  tne  rear  of  the  defendant's  vehicle  (T-69).  The  defendant

followed  him  back  there  (T-69).  As  they  were  walking  to  the  back

of  the  car,  the  defendant  spontanecusly  told  Officer  Rix  that  he

had  a  CCW  permit  and  a  gun  in  an  ankle  holster  (T-71).  That  was  the

first  thing  the  defendant  had  said,  as  far  as  Officer  Rix  could
recall  (T-71).

A.  The  frisk  of  his  person.

The question  at  issue  here  is whether  advising  a

pol

officer  that  one  is  carrying  a  licensed  weapon,  and  presenting t

license  and  inspection  certificate,  entitles  the  officer  to pe

>

y

down or search the individual,  The stop of the vehicle to issue a

traffic  ticket  was  one  intrusion  on  Fourth  Amendment  interests,  a

legally  authorized  intrusion.  The  frisking  and  searching  of

defendant  and  his  car  constitute  separate  and  distinct

on  Fourth  Amendment  rights.

After  the  legitimate  stop  to  issue  a  non-custodial
infraction  citation,  the  officer  committed  a  second  intrusion,  a

1

"stop  and  frisk"  of  the  sort  sometimes  authorized  by Terry v Ohio,

392  U.S.

1;  88  S.Ct.  1868;  20  L.Ed.2d  889  (1968)  under  certain

circumstances.

;

Ordinarily,  the  possession  of  a  concealed  weapon  is  ses

a  crime,  and  an  officer  can  seize  the  contraband  item.  However,  a

lawfully  possessed  weapon  is  not  lawfully  subject  to  seizure,  and

therefcre  cannot  reasonably  be  the  subject  of  a  search.  Pursuant —

to  the  terms  of  the  general  CCW,  any  weapon  found  on  Mr.  Harmelin—
could  not  be  seized,  and  would  have  to  be  returned  to  him  (EHi-
‘
32).

The  "pat-down"  search  for  weapons  constitutes  an

int

on  Four.  Amendment  rights.  Terry vy Ohio,  supra.  “Even  a  Limited

search  of  the  outer  clothing  for  weapons  constitutes  a

re,

though  brief,  intrusion  upon  cherished  personal  security,  and  it

must  surely  be  an  annoying,  frightening,  and  perhaps  humi

1g

experience."  Terry,  supra.  In  this  case,  the  defendant  was

with  his  hands  against  his  car,  with  his  legs  spread,  while

officers  searched  him  (EH1-39,40).

.

fem

Under  Terry,  the  police  officer  is  permitted  to  intrude. on

a  citizen's  Fourth  Amendment

privacy  and  make  a  pet~temn:!  her  a

police  officer  observes  unusual  conduct  which  leads  him
to  conclude  in  light  of  his  experience  that  criminal  activity 4

afoot  an  that  the  persons  with  whom  he  is  dealing  may  be  armed  and

presently  dangerous."  Terry,  30.

Here,  as  in  People

vy Burrell,  417  Mich  439  (1983),

"No

specific  inferences  of  criminal  activity  could  possibly  be  drawn

from  these  facts."  Mr.  Harmelin's  car  approached  a  red  light  at  a

left  lane  turn-around.  The  officer  observed  the  car  "slowed,  hit

it's  brakes,  but  just  kept  going."  (EH1-23).  Other  than  that,  the

defendant's  driving  was

not  unlawful

in  any  way.

He  did  not

attemptto  flee  (EH1-25).  He  produced  a  driver  license,  registration

and  proof  of  insurance.  He  spontaneously  informed  the  office  that

he  had  a  CCW  permit  which  he  produced  and  informed  the  officer  of

the  gun  in  the  ankle  holster.

Both  officers  testified  that  the  defendant  was  nervous;

Officer  Rix  testified  that  other  than  the  defendant  being  nervous

there  was  nothing  else  unusual  about  him  "but  I  would  rather  have

the  elements  of  surprise  with  me  than  with  nim"  (T-79).  Officer

Blakeney  testified  that  the  defendant  was  "  a  little  bit  nervous"

(EH2-29)  specifically,  when  the  officers  "told  him  to  put  his  hands

on  the  car  he  did  so  in  a  jerky  type  manner"  (EH2-29).

Officer  Rix  testified  he  observed  bulges  in  the  defendant's

jacket  pockets  which  he  thought  might  be  more  weapons  (EH2-12).

Officer  Rix  explained  that  "I've  been  in  a  situation  once  before

where  an  man  drew  a  gun  on  me,  pointed  it  at  me  and  I  was  almost

killed.  I'm  not  going  to  go  through  that  again."  (EH1-34).  Oddly

enough,

Rix  testified  that

he  noticed  the  bulges  before  the

denfendant  handed  him  the  CCW  permit  (T-78)  yet  Rix  testified  that

the  defendant

followed

Rix

to

the

back

of

the

car

(T-69).

Therefore,

we  must  conclude  that  Rix  turned  his  back  on  the

defendant  and  the  bulges  that  so  concerned  him.

An  even  more

telling  fact  is  that  the  police  allowed  the  defendant's  gun  itself

to  go  untouched  while  the  officers  searched  his  coat  (EH1-40-43).

The  ofticers  showed  far  more  interest  in  the  contents  of  his

cigarette  case  that  in  his  gun.

Another  telling  fact  is  that  the  biggest  bulge  turned  out

to  be  a  "thin  flat  cigarette  case"  (EH2-31;EH1-78).  The  record

‘em"  by  two  uniformed  officers  and  for  apparently  no  reason

than  making  a  rolling  stop  at  a  stop  sign.

i

Mr.  Harmelin  was  stopped  for  a  civil  infraction,  not @

criminal  offense.  Arrest  was  not  a  possibility  (T-36).  Def

took  no  action  that  caused  the  officer  to  fear  for  his  safety  (EHi~

25,  36-37;  EH2-25,  26,35).

a

These  circumstances  are  very  much  like  People  v Gonzales.

356  Mich  247  (1959),  where  the  Michigan  Supreme  Court  stated:

"The  officers  had  no  reason  to  suspect  any  violation

of  the  law  other  than  the  traffic  offense  ‘or  which  the
summons  was  issued  to  Leal.  Further,  we  feel  the  record
clearly  shows  that  the  officers  had  no  intention  of
incarcerating  Leal  or  detaining  him  any  further.  There
were  no  fruits  of  the  traffic  o offense  to  search  for,  nor
any  need  to  search  for  the  means  by which  it  had  been
committed.  And  since  mo  further  detencion  was  comtemplated
there  was  no  need  to  search  for  weapons  or  other  means
of  possible  escape  from  custody."

:
;
om
‘
|

‘

*
.

The  officers  in  Gonzales  were  in  the  habit  a  “checking  cars  quite

thoroughly  at  that  time"  apparently  referring  to  the  early

of  the  morning.  Officer  Rix  in  the  instant  case  testified  that

searched  Mr.  Harmelin  “because  he  had  a  gun"  (EH1-12).

Harmelin  had  a  legal  right  to  carry  that  gun  just  as  he  had  a

right  to  leave  the  Embassy  Motel  that  night.

Circuit  Court  Judge  Anderson  ruled  that  the  frisk  was

because  "it  appeared  to  someone,  the  police.  that  something

100  percent."  (EH2-60).  This  ruling  is  precisely  what  the

a2

Amendment  was  enacted  to  protect  against--arbitrary

int

“ence

with  basic  human  dignity  in  our  cities.

Judge  Anderson  heard  two  days  of  testimony  by the  off

and  the  appellate  courts  generally  give  deference  to  the

*

4

i  SS  ell

‘

oe

Se,

*

~

Dae

Ay

"

ts

-

court  because  they  are  able  to  judge  the  credibility  of  the

witnesses.

After  two  days  of  testimony,  he  felt  that  the  officers

had  frisked  the  defendant  “because  something  wasn't  100  percent."

This  conclusion  does  not  reach  the  standard  required  by  Terry yv

Qhio.

The  Court  of  Appeals  reversed  the  trial  court  in  their  first

opinion.  The  panel  apparently  felt  that  Mr.  Harmelin  was  searched

for  no  reason.  Their  second  opinion,  which  defendant  concludes  was

the  result  of  ex-parte  communications  (see  issue  IV),  concludes:

"([I)t  was  reasonable  to  conclude  that  criminal

activity  might  have  been  afoot  and  that  the
defendant  might  have  been  armed  and  dangerous."
~-~People

v Harmelin,  176  Mich  App  524,  532  (1989).

What  criminal  activity  might  have  been  afoot?

The  officers

testified  that  the  Embassy  Motel  was  a  drop  off  spot  for  stolen

cars,

but  the  defendant's  car  was  completely  legal.  And  if  the

defendant  was  dangerous,  why  would  he  tell  the  officers  that  he  had

a  gun  and  a  permit?

The  defendant  submits  that  the  trial  court  correctly  summed

up  the  testimony  of  the  police  officers,  that  they  had  no  reason

to  frisk  Mr.  Harmelin.  Of  course  police  officers  would  feel  safer

if  they  were  allowed  to  routinely  frisk  everyone  they  encounter,

but  this  is  precisely  what  the  Fourth  Amendment  prohibits.  The  fact

that  Officer  Rix  had  been  shot  before,  and  he  didn't  like  the  fact

that  the  defendant  had  a  licensed  weapon  in  an  ankle  holster  is  no

justification  for  this  frisk.  The  fact  that  the  biggest  bulge  the

officers  saw  turned  out  to  be  a  "thin,

flat  cigarette  case"

suggests  that  the  real  reason  for  the  search  was  not  because  they

wanted  to  be  sure  he  had  no  other  weapons,  but  that  they  were  just

searching  for  evidence  of  other  crimes.

There  was  no  justifiable  reason  for  the  frisk  in  this  case,  and

therefore  the  evidence  and  its  fruits  should  be  suppressed.  US

Const,  Amend  IV.

Since  all  the  evidence  in  this  case  flowed  from

the  illegal  frisk,

the  evidence  should  be  suppressed  and  the

defendant  discharged.  Wong  Sun  v United  States,  371  U.S.  471;  83

S.Ct.  407;  9  L.Ed.2d  441  (1963).

|
}

B.  The  examination  of  the  cigarette  case.

}  If  a  frisk  were  lawful,  it  must  still  be  confined  to the ©

Cn

cause  for  the  frisk,  i.e.,  to  look  for  dangerous  weapons.
v  Qhie,  supra  at  29.  The  officer  therefore  had  no  right  either  t ©
look  inside  the  "little  flat"  cigarette  case  (EH  1,45)  or  ask

abou

x

its  contents.

-&
eN

Defendant,  at  the  time  of  the  frisk,  was  leaning  with  his

hands  against  the  car,  with  his  legs  spread.  (EH1,39-40).  This  was

not  a  casual  interview  in  the  person's  own  office.

Rather, —

defendant  was  clearly  “deprived  of  his  freedom  of  action  in  ,

significant  way".

Miranda  v  Arizona,  384  U.S.  436,  444;  86  S.ct.

1602;

16.L.Ed  24  694

(1966).

The  questioner  was  looking  for

incriminating  answers,  but  failed  to  give  warnings  required  under

Miranda  v  Arizona.

The  questioning  was  therefore  unlawful,  and  the

subsequent  opening  of  the  cigarette  case,  based  on  the  answer  to

the  questioning,  was  the  fruits,  and  again  must  be  suppressed,

along  with  the  fruits  of  the  marijuana  arrest.  Dunaway  v  New York, ©

vi
in

442  U.S.  200;  99  S.Ct.  2248;  60  L.Ed.2d  824  (1979).

fl ;

C.  The  impoundment  of  the  car.

The  police  indicated  that  they  impounded  Mr.  Harmelin's

;

and  did  a  routine  inventory  search  of  the  vehicle.  The  Court  of <

Appeals,  however,  found  that  the  record  in  this  case

a

that  the  Oak  Park  Police  Department's  standardized  procedures  id
not mandate  searching  and conducting  an inventory  search of

every

container  within  an  impounded  vehicle;  and  that  therefore

inventory  search  done  in  this  case  does  not  fall  within

parameters  of  Colorado
L.Bd.24  739  (1987).  In  Bertine,  the  United  States  Supreme

v Bertine,  479  U.S.  738;  107  S.Ct.  738;  ;
Cour

be he
“es

Fs ta 2

held  that  the  Fourth  Amendment  does  not  prohibit  an

search  by  police  if  the  police  follow  standardized  procedures.

added  in  footnote  6  on  page  742:

A

‘ag

“We  emphasize  that,  ip  this  case,  the  trial

court  found  that  the  police  department's  procedures
mandated  the  ———  of  see a  ~ ll
ys
—  = =  contents  Pay  Ss

nog

3

conducted  according  to  standardized  criteria.  See

Lafayette,462  U.S.,  at  648,  103  S.Ct.  at  2610;  Opperman,

428  U.S.  at  374-5;  96  S.Ct.  at  3099-3100.

Since  the  police  officers  in  this  case  did  not  know  if  there

was  a  specific  departmental  policy  regarding  the  inventory  of

closed  containers  (T-124,130,133,165),  they  could  not  have  been

following  standardized  procedures.

Thus,

the  evidence

in  the

instant  case  was  recovered  in  violation  of  Colorado

v Bertine,  and

should  be  suppressed.

The  Michigan  Court  of  Appeals  opined  that  the  search  in

this  case  falls  within  the  automobile  exception  to  the  warrant

requirement  enunciated  in  United  States  v Ross,  456  U.S.  789;  102

S.Ct.  2157;  72  L.Ed.2d  572  (1982)

In  Ross,

the  police  were  acting  on  information  from  a

reliable

informant

that

there

were

narcotics

in

a

certain

automobile  trunk,  and  that  a  certain  person  would  be  driving  the

automobile.  The  officers  stopped  the  defendant,  opened  the  trunk,

and  discovered  heroin  in  a  closed  container.  The  police  then  drove

the  car  to  the  station,  searched  it  more  thoroughly,  and  discovered

a  zippered  pouch  containing  cash.

At  no  time  did  the  officers

attempt  to  obtain  a  warrant.  The  United  States  Supreme  Court  held

that  police  officers  who  have  legitimately  stopped  an  automobile

and  who  have  probable  cause  to  believe  that  contraband  is  concealed

somewhere  within  may  conduct  a  warrantless  search  of  the  vehicle

as  thorough  as  a  magistrate  could  authorize  in  a  warrant.

The  Ross  case  does  not  apply  to  this  case  for  two  reasons.

The  first  reason  is  that  these  police  officers  testified  that  they

were  conducting  an  inventory  search.

This  search  was  invalid

because  they  did  not  follow  standardized  procedures.  Therefore  the

search  was  not  a  legal  one.

Furthermore,

Ross

and

the

“automobile  exception"  do  not  address  inventory  searches.

The  Michigan  Supreme  Court  addressed  this  latter  issue  in

People

v Long  (On Rem),  419  Mich  636.  In  Long,  police  followed  the

defendant  because  he was speeding.  Long  got  out  of  the  car  and  from

oe

a

his  demeanor,  the  police  formed  the  opinion  he  was  “under  the
influence of something."
Long as he moved back —
The police  followed
toward  the  car,  and  they  saw  a  knife  on  the  floorboard.  Shining  a
light  in  the  interior  of  the  car,  the  officer  saw  something  under  E

the  armrest.  It  was  a  pouch  of  marijuana.  The  police  decided  to  a

impound  the  car.  The  deputy  opened  the  trunk  and  found  75  pounds

ss

of  marijuana.

The  government  argued  the  search  was  a  valid  am

inventory  gearch.  In  footnote  2,  page  648,  the  Michigan  Supreme

Court  said:

"2.  Plaintiff,  in  a  brief  footnote,  concludes

or  developed  in  this  case.  Nor

that  the  search  of  the  trunk  may  have  arguably
been  a  search  incident  to  defendant's  arrest.  We
decline  to  rule  on  that  question  as  the  argument
is  not  relied
does  the  plaintiff  rely  upon  the  “automobile
exception"  to  the  warrant  requirement  in  order  to
justify  the  search  of  the  trunk.  Cf.  United  States
v  Ross,  (456}  U.S.  [(798)};(search  of  closed  container
in  automobile).  Purthermore,  Ross  and  the  automobile
exception  do  not  address  inventory  searches.  Id,
p  809,fn  11.  [Emphasis  added).

The  Michigan  Supreme  Court  was  correct  in  its  careful

reading  of  Ross,  which  does  not  address  inventory  searches  and

should  not  be  read  to  condone  a  constitutionally  invalid  inventory

search.

Secondly,

in  Ross,

the  police  had  been  told  by  an

informant  there  were  drugs  in  the  trunk.  In  Harmelin's  case,  the

:

police  had  no  such  knowledge.  As  a  matter  of  fact,  the  Oak  Park

Police  were  surprised  and  excited  to  discover  money  in  Mr.  Har-

melin's  trunk--so  excited  that  they  hurriedly  close  the  trunk  back

up  and  go  to  get  a  superior  officer.

The true  issue  here  is  whether  or not this  was a  valid  A

inventory  search.  The  testimony  of  the  officers  shows  that  the  Oak

Park  Police  Department's  standardized  procedures  did  not  mandate  |

searching  and  conducting  an  inventory  of  the  contents  of  every

container  within  an  impounded  vehicle  such  as  the  defendant's;  oe

thus,  the  inventory  search  done  in  this  case  was  invalid  under

Colorado

vy Bertine,  supra.

14

a  |

- 7. ™

» alae

sty  Die

D.  The  search  of  containers  and  their  contents.

.

Upon  opening  the  trunk,  the  officers  found  a  shaving  kit

with  other  containers  inside,  and  opened  them  and  searched  then.

The  opening  of  closed  containers,  without  a  warrant  or  probable

cause,  was  unlawful.  United  States  v Chadwick,  433  U.S.  1;97  S.Ct.

2476;  53  L.Ed.2d  538  (1977).

A  car  trunk,  while  not  completely  safe,  is  universally

considered  to  be  a  reasonably  safe  place  to  store  personal  goods.

If  the  defendant  trusts  his  own  car  trunk  as  safe,  the  police  have

no  real  need  to  search  the  trunk  for  the  defendant's  "own  protec-

tion".

A  defendant  can  waive  virtually  any  right,  including  the

right  to  trial  itself.  In  a  society  with  a  Fourth  Amendment,  where

the  right  to  privacy  in  personal  effects  is  supposed  to  be  protect-

ed,  a  citizen  may  choose  to  leave  his  property  locked  in  a  car

trunk  at  minimal  risk,  without  having  it  searched  by  police.

Defendant  concludes  that  the  frisk  of  his  person,  and  all

the  police  actions  thereafter,  were  unlawful,  and  that  the  fruits,

i.e.,  all  items  found  on  Mr.  Harmelin  and  in  his  car,  must  be

suppressed.  U.S.  Const,  Amend  IV.

The  main  difference  between  defense  counsel's  questioning  at  the

evidentiary  hearing  and  the  trial  was  that  at  trial  counsel  added

questioning  designed  to  question  whether  the  officers  really  did

or  did  not  see  bulges  in  the  coat.  (T  76-82).

Clearly,  whether  the

officer  really  did  or  did  not  see  bulges  in  the  coat  was  relevent

only  to  the  legality  of  the  officer's  actions,  and  was  not  relevant

to  the  issues  at  trial:  whether  Defendant  Harmelin  knowingly  and

intentionally  possessed  over  650  grams  of  cocaine  found  in  by  the

officers  in  his  car  trunk.

The  situation  with  the  second  officier  was  similar.

The

officer  was  questioned  about  the  inventory  search,  the  impoundment

and  search  policies  of  the  Oak  Park  Police  Department,  etc.  (T  120-

133).

He  was  also  ask  about  whether  there  was  discussion  about

getting  a  search  warrant,  and  what  made  the  officer  "suspicious."

(T  136-7)  Counsel  established  in  detail  the  steps  taken  by  the

officier  to  discover  the  cocaine  that  was  found  in  the  trunk.  (139-

142).

He  then  returned  to  the  officier's  actions,  thoughts  and

motivations  during  the  search  of  Defendant's  person  (T  145-151).

Counsel  then  recalled  Officer  Rix,  and  had  Defendant  put  on  the

jacket  with  the  items  restored  to  the  pockets,  to  demonstrate  that

the  alleged  bulges  could  not  really  be  seen.  (T  153-158).

He  moved

on  to  the  circumstances  of  the  trunk  search,  in  what  order  things

%

:

Present  counsel  concedes  that  Mr.  Rundell  did  a  competent

and  professional  job  at  rearguing  the  suppression  motion  to  Judge

Schnelz  during  the  course  of  the  trial,  although  it  would  have  been

better  for  him  to  cite  to  People  v  Sacorafas,  76  Mich  App  370

(1977),  expressly  giving  the  successor  judge  power  to  redeternine

admissibility  rulings  made  by  the  first  judge.

=

The  problem  is  that  Mr.  Rundell  did  not  put  on  any  kind  of

a  trial  defense  whatsoever.

He  did  not  give  the  judge  any  cause

to  think  the  Defendant  might  be  innocent,  nor  did  he  argue  that  the

elements  of  the  offense  were  not  adequately  proven.

He  did  not

contest  the  guilt  of  the  Defendant  in  any  way.

a

In  Wiley  v  Sowders,  647  F2d  642,  648-649  (CA  6,  1981),  the

Court  held:

"The  decision  to  pled  ‘guilty’  or  ‘not  guilty'  isa
decision  reserved  soley  for  the  accused  based  on  his
intelligent  and  voluntary  choice...

An  attorney  may  not  stipulate  to  the  functional

:

|

of  his  plea  of  not  guilty’.  CF.
(constitutional  right  not  to

counsel  did  not  urge  a  not  guilty  verdict.

In  other  words,  counsel

argued  to  Judge  Schnelz  in  his  capacity  as  judge  determining

admissibilty  of  evidence,  but  made  no  argument  at  all  to  Judge

Schnelz  in  his  capacity  as  trier  of  fact  in  a  trial.

Defense  counsel  offered  the  trier  of  fact  “virtually  no

choice  but  to  convict  him,  in  spite  of  his  plea  of  ‘not  guilty'".

Martin  v  Rose,  supra.  By  failing  to  present  any  argument  that  the

prosecutor's  case

was

not  proven,

defense  counsel

failed  to

structure  the  trial  around  his  client's  plea  of  not  guilty.

The

so-called  “trial”  was  nothing  more  than  a  suppression  hearing

coupled  with  "the  functional  equivalent  of  a  guilty  plea.”  Wiley

v  Sowders,  supra.

In  een  tis see  also  United  States  v  Cronic,  466  U.S.  648,

659;  104  S.Ct.  2039;  80  L.Ed.2d  657  (1984),  finding  a  denial  of

right  to  counsel  where  counsel  “entirely  fails  to  subject  the

prosecution's  case  to  meaningful  adversarial  testing."  See  also,

People  v  Fisher,  119  Mich.App.  445  (1982);  People v _Carter,  41

I1l.App.  425;  354  N.E.2d  482  (1976).  and  People  v  Hattery,  109  I11.

App.2d  449;  488  N.E.2d  513  (1986),  each  of  which  found  ineffective

assistance  of  counsel  where  counsel  failed  to  advocate  the  client's

position  of  guilty.

Mr.  Harmelin  concedes  that,

if  admissible,  the  evidence

against  him  was  quite  strong.

However,  whether  the  evidence  is

weak  or  strong,  the  Defendant  is  entitled  to  plead  not  guilty,  and

is  entitled  to  have  an  attorney  argue  that  the  prosecutor's  case

has  not  been  proven.

As  an  example,  the  Michigan  statute  requires  that  possession

be  knowing  and  intentional.

MCL  333.7403.

Counsel  could  have

structured  the  case

around

the  theory  that

the  elements  of

knowledge  and  intent  were  not  proven,  because  there  was  no  direct

evidence  that  Defendant  knew  the  drugs  were  in  the  trunk.

Even

when  evidence  is  overwhelming,  a  plea  of  not  guilty  means  the

defense  counsel  must  put  on  a  defense.  As  the  Court  held  in  People

v  Hattery,  supra,  488  NE2d  at  519:

“Although  counsel's  strategy  may  have  been  reasonable
in  light  of  the  evidence--a  point  we  need  not  consider-

674  (1984);  People v Garcia,  398  Mich  250  (1976);  Martin  v  Rose,

supra;  Wiley  v  Sowders,  supra;  United  States  v  Cronic,  supra.

Defendant  was  deprived  of  his  right  to  plead  not  guilty.  Martin  *

Rose  supra;  Wiley  v  Sowders,  supra.

Mich  436  (1973),  an  evidentiary  hearing  should  have  been  ordered

to  determine  this  issue  of  ineffective  assistance  of  counsel.

However,  even  without  further  evidentiary  support,  a  new  trial  is

required  to  remedy  this  Sixth  Amendment  violation.

III.  THE  MANDATORY  SENTENCE  OF  LIFE  IMPRISONMENT

WITHOUT  POSSIBILITY  OF  PAROLE  CONSTITUTES  CRUEL  AND

UNUSUAL  PUNISHMENT.

Defendant  Ronald  Harmelin  was  convicted  of  possession  of

over  650  grams  of  cocaine.

Pursuant  to  statute,  MCL  333.7403,

Judge  Schnelz  imposed  the  mandatory  sentence  of  life  imprisonment

without  possibility  of  parole.

The  U.S.  Constitution  bans  punishments  for  crimes  which

constitute  cruel  and  unusual  punishment.

U.S.  Const,  Amend  VII".

The  federal  and  state  courts  have  long  recognized  the  judicial  duty

to  set  aside  sentences  violating  these  provisions.

Weems  v  United

as Nati

oe ae

If  the substance in Ronnie*s  trunk hed turned aut ©

650  grams of cyanide,  enough to kill hundreds  of  th
innocent  people,  he  would  have  gone  completely

if  the  police  found  with  the  cyanide  a  note  in the

handwriting  outlining  a  plot  to use  the cyanide  to poison

water  supplies,  he  still  could  not  be  punished  as  harshly  as ne bi

been  for  possessing  cocaine.

:

No  other  jurisdiction  in  the  United  States  has

even  coming  close  to  the  harshness  of  Michigan's.  People  v

124  Mich  App  93,  104  (1983)  (partial  concurrences  of  M.  Kelly,

Indeed,  under  federal  law,  if  a  first  offender  possessed  the

amount  of  cocaine  as  did  Mr.  Harmelin,  the  maximum  penalty

be  a  term  of  not  less  than  5  or  more  than  40  years  in  prison.  a
U.S.C.  S@41  (b)(1)(B)(ii).

If  that  same  first  offender

States,  217  U.S.  349;  30  S.Ct.  544;  54  L.Ed.2d  637  (1983).

more  than  5  kilograms  of  cocaine  (eight  times  the  amount  of

The  length  of  sentence  alone  is  a  valid  ground  for  finding

possessed  by  Defendant  Harmelin),  the  maximum  penalty  would  bes

cruel  and  unusual  punishment.

Soler  v  Helm,  463  U.S.

277;  103

S.Ct.3001;  77  L.Ed.2d  637  (1983).

The  Eighth  Amendment  prohibits

“sentences  that  are  disproportionate  to  the  crime  committed."  Solem

v  Helm  supra.

Considering  proportionality  of  the  sentence  to  the  crime,

there  is  no  other  crime  in  Michigan  for  which  the  punishment  is

mandatory  life  without  parole  except  for  first  degree  murder.

A

person  with

a

long  criminal

record  could  commit  the  foulest

atrocities,  such  as  holding  people  capitive  for  years  as  sexual

Slaves,  hacking  off  limbs,

or  poking  out  eyes  of  children,  or

attempting  to  assassinate  public  officials,

without  even

the

possibility  of  receiving  a  sentence  as  high  as  Ronnie  Harmelin.

In  contrast,  Ronnie  Harmelin,  for  possessing  a  substance  one  can

buy  freely  and  legally  on  the  streets  of  Peru,  had  to  receive  a

sentence  of

life  without  parole  regardless  of  how  the  judge

evaluated  his  character.

The  judge  was  compelled  by  statute  to

ignore  the  fact  that  Ronnie  had  absolutely  no  criminal  record,  that

he  harmed  no  one,  that  he  did  not  display  viciousness,  that  he  did

not  demonstrate  an  inability  to  reform.

The  federal  provisions  are  themselves  quite  harsh,  bet

not  even  begin  to  approach  the  unspeakable  cruelty  of  the  fF

ent

Michigan  provisions.  The  judge  cannot  give  a  sentence  of  less  han
life,  and  the  Parole  Board  cannot  issue  a  parole,  ever.

.

in  Solem v Helm,  we look  in  part  to the vast discrepancy

the  Michigan  provisions  and  those  of  other  American  jurisd:

We  look  also  to  the  fact  that  judges  such  as  Judge  Norman
of  Oakland  County  have  refused  to  sentence  people  under this a et é

refusing  to  become  a  party  to  the  barbarity  of  these  provisions  (¢ 3

reported  in  the  newspapers).

former  Judge  Bert  Hensick  of  Livingston  county,  have  reached  1 &

conclusion  that  this  extreme  penalty  is  inconsistent

with :

The recocd reveals that in the early morning hours of May

12,  1986,  two Oak  Park police  officers,  Calvin  Rix  and  Lawrence ©
Blakeney,  were  patrolling  in  a  marked  police  car  in the  area  c
the  Embassy  Motel  in  the  City  of  Oak  Park,  and at 2:45  a.m.  drove |  |
into  the  motel’s  parking  lot  to  check  for  stolen  vehicles.

rare se of Sete tom Oe  i  ee  '

en  ee  Pas  SO

ee

Mumerous  stolen vehicles  had been found in thie let in the past.

At  this  time,  the  officers  observed  defendant’s  car  exiting  the

parking  lot,  and  although  they  were  called  away  on other  police

business  at  various  times  that  morning,  they  saw  defendant’s  car

entering  the  Embassy  Motel  parking  lot  at  about  4:00  a.m.  and

again  at  5:00  a.m.

In  the  three  sightings  of

defendant's  car,

the  officers  noticed  nothing  unusual  or  illegal  in  the  manner  in

which  defendant’s  car  was  being  driven.

Shortly  after  5:00  a.m.,  while  continuing  to  patrol  the

area  around  the  Embassy  Motel,  Officers  Rix  and  Blakeney  saw

defendant's  car  make  a  U-turn  at  the  intersection  of  Eight  Mile

and  Hubbell  Roads,  a  high-crime  area,  without  stopping  for  a  red

light.

When  the  officers,  in  their  patrol  car  with  its  overhead

lights  flashing,

pulled  behind  defendant's  vehicle,  defendant

responded  by  stopping  in  a  reasonably  prompt  fashion.

Officer

Rix  walked  to  the  driver's  side  of  defendant's  car,  while  Officer

Blakeney  positioned  himself  at  the  passenger  side  of  the  car.

Defendant,

who

remained

seated

in

his

car,

complied

in

a

cooperative  manner  upon  being  requested  by  Officer  Rix  to  produce

a  driver’s

license  and  vehicle  registration.

Defendant  then

stepped  out  of  his  car.

Neither  of  the  officers  could  recell

whether  defendant  was  ordered  out  of  his  vehicle  or  whether  he

got

out

of

it

on

his

own

initiative,

although  ‘Officer  Rix

testified  that  if  he

did  in  fact  order  defendant  out

of  the

car,  that  order  would  have  been  prompted  solely  by  the  nervous

behavior

exhibited

by  defendant

--

behavior

described

as

defendant’s  having  “trouble  getting  some  of  the  words  out.”

In

any  event,  after  exiting  his  car,  defendant,  in  4  nervous  manner,

informed  Officer  Rix  that  he  was  carrying  a  pistol  concealed  in

an  ankle  holster.

He  explained  that  he  had  a  permit  for  the

weapon,  however,  and  that  he  did  not  want  the  officers  to  become

alarmed.

He  then  handed  Officer  Rix  a  general  permit  to  carry  a

concealed  weapon  as  well  as  a  safety  inspection  certificate  for

the  .38  caliber,  five-shot  revolver,  which  was  later  found  in  his

ankle  holster.

On  appeal,  defendant  first  argues  that  the  detention  and

pat-down  search  conducted  by  the  police  violated  his  Fourth  ~

Amendment  right  to  be  secure  against  unreasonable  seizures  and

did  not  fall  within  the  stop-and-frisk  exception  to  the  warrant

requirement  first  enunciated

in Terry  v Ohio,  392  US 1;  88 S Ct

1868;  20  L  Ed  2d  6869  (1968),  because  the  detention  and  search

|

could  not  be  jystified  by  any  articulable  suspicion  or  objective  4
meniseovenion  thet he vee éngeget, "es enuh.0h' ll sina
criminal  activity.
frisk  law,  the  United  States  Supreme  Court  created  a  limited  —
exception  to  the  requirement  of  showing
probable  cause  in  the

In  Terry,  the  locus  classicus  of  stop-and-  ‘

soisure of a  person  by ©  police  officer  if  the officer  hed  «
reasonable,  articulable  suspicion  that  the  person  committed,  or

*

was  about  to  commit,  a  crime.

Under  the  Terry  rule,  once  an

officer  has  reasonably  concluded  that  criminal  activity  may  be

afoot,

he  can

conduct

a  pat-down

search

if  he  reasonably

concludes  that  the  person  seized  may  be  armed  and dangerous.  See
People  v  Payton,  166  Mich  App  428,  430-431;  421  NWw2d  191  (1988);
People  v  Peyton,  167  Mich  App  230,  236-237;  421  NW2d  643  (1988).

In  the  present  case,  defendant,  prior  to  trial,  moved  to  supprese

the  narcotics

evidence

against  him  on

the  ground  that

the

detention  and  pat-down  search  to  which  he  was  subjected  violated

his  Fourth  Amendment  rights.

Of  course,  it  is  well  established

that  evidence  derived  from  an  illegal  seizure  must  be  suppressed

as  the  “fruit  of  the  poisonous  tree.”  Wong  Sun  v  United  States,

371  US  471;  83  S  Ct  407;  9  L  Ed  2d  441  (1963);  People  v  Shabaz,

424  Mich  42,  65;  378  NW2d  451  (1985),  cert  dis

US

3;  106

S  Ct  3326;

L  Ed  2d

(1986).

The  trial  court  denied

defendant’s  pretrial  suppression  motion.

This  Court  will  not

disturb  a  trial  court’s  ruling  on  a  suppression  motion  unless

that  ruling  is  clearly  erroneous.
Payton,  supra,  p  430;  Peyton,
supra,  p  235;  People  v  Burrell,  417  Mich  439,  448;  339  Nw2d  403

(1986).

We  will  find  such  a  ruling  clearly  erroneous  if  firmly

convinced  that  4  mistake  has  been  made.

People  v  Daniels,  160

Mich  App  614,  617;  408  NW2d  398  (19867).

In

the

present

case,

defendant,

without

doubt,

was

lawfully  stopped  for  running  a  red

light,  which  is  a  civil

infraction,  MCL  257.611;  MSA  9.2311;  People  v  Laube,  154  Mich  App

400,  407;  397  NW2d  325  (1986),  lv  den  426  M'ch  656  (1987).

It

seems  fairly  clear  that  after  defendant  was  lawfully  stopped  and

produced  his  driver’s  license  and  vehicle  registration,  Officer

Rix  ordered  defendant  to  get  out  of  his

car.

Although  defendant

did  not  testify,  and  thus  did  not  inform  the  court  that  he  was  in

fact  ordered  out  of  his  car,  and  although  Officer  Rix  could  not

remember  whether  he  ordered  defendant  out  of  his  car  or  whether

defendant  exited  the  automobile  of  his  own  accord,

it  seems

this case,  defendant  would  have gotten  out of his car  wil
being
told  to do so.  The  question  thus  becomes  whether
thi
escalation  in  the  intrusiveness of the stop was justified by th
traffic  violation  alone  and,
edditional

if  not,  whether  the

detention  was  supported  by  a  reasonable,  articulable  basis  on  he
part  of  the  police  for  suspecting  criminal  activity.
mindful,
during  our  examination  of  this  matter,  that  “the
intrusiveness  of  the  police  activity  must  be  carefully  limited  to
the  circumstances  justifying  the  detention.”
457.

Burrel],  supra,

We  «

With  respect to the Fourth Amendment of the United States

Constitution,  the  reasonableness  of  requesting  a  driver  to  get
out
of  a  vehicle  following  a  lawful  traffic  stop  has  been
answered  by  the  United  States  Supreme  Court  in  Pennsylvania  v_
Mimms,  434  US  106;  98  S  Ct  330;  54  L  Bd  2d  331  (1977).
In  Minne,
a  majority  of  the  Supreme  court  held  that  a  police  officer’s
order  to  a  driver  to  exit  his  car  after  the  car  was  lawfully
stopped  for  a  traffic  violation  did  not  violate  the  Fourth
Amendment,  even  though  the  officer  had  no  reason  to  suspect  foul
play  at  the  time,  since  the  obvious  interest  in  the  officer’s
safety  outweighed  the  de  minimus  intrusion  involved  in  what  was
described  as  “at  most  a  mere  inconvenience”  to  the  driver.
98  &
Ct  333.
‘The  Mimms  majority  noted  that,  according  to  one  study,
approximately  30%  of  police  shootings  occurred  when  @  police  —
officer  approached  a  suspect  seated  in an automobile
and  that  the
hazard  of  accidental  injury  from  passing  traffic  to  an officer
standing  on  the  driver’s  side  of  a  stopped  vehicle  may  also  be
appreciable  in  some  situations.
once

a  police  officer  has  stopped  a  vehicle  for  a  traffic
violation  he  has  the  authority,  without  more,  to  order  the  driver.
out  of  the  car.

—

which  Justices  Brennan  and  Marshall  joined,  it  is  stated  that  ”
"[tjhe  Court  has  based  its  legal  ruling  on  «  factual  sesumption

5

ws  68

about  police  safety  that  is  dubious  at  best  [and}...  has  created

an  entirely  new  legal  standard  of  justification  for  intrusions  on

the  liberty  of  the  citizen.”

98  S  Ct  337.

majority’s

reliance

in  1959,

which  it  refers.

The  line  between  unfettered  discretion  and

discrimination  is  a  fine  one  indeed.

The  evidence  in  the  present  case  is  clear  that  Officer  —

Rix  had  no  reasonable,  articulable  suspicion  that  defendant  was

engaged  in  criminal  activity  at  the  time  he,  presumably,  was

ordered  out  of  his  car.

At  that  time,  Officer  Rix  knew  that

defendant’s  car  had

frequently  gone  in  and  out  of  a  motel

parking  lot  during  the  early  morning  hours  of  May  12,  1986;  that

numerous  stolen  cars  had  been  found  in  the  parking  lot  in  the

past;  that  defendant  had  illegally  run  a  red  light  in  a  high-

crime  area;  and  that  defendant  complied  in  a  cooperative  manner

upon  being  requested  to  produce  his  driver’s  license  and  vehicle

registration.

We

fail

to

see  how  any  “specific  reasonable

Fe ES Ee Pe

inferences  of  criminal  activity  could  possibly  be  drawn  from

[=

Ale a ee” dg

o 4

rr @

these  facts.”

Burrell,  supra,  p  458.

Either  taken  singly  or  in

toto,  they  suggest  to  us  nothing  particularly  nefarious,  illicit,

improper  or  illegal.

It  is  only  after  defendant  got  out  of  his

car  that  questions  of  safety  earose,

i.e.,  it  was  only  after

defendant

got

out

of  his

car

that  defendant  began  acting

nervously,  displayed  a  bulge  in  a  coat  pocket,  ont produced  a

gun,  for  which  he  possessed  a  facially  valid  general  permit  and

safety  inspection  certificate.

This  latter  evidence,  however,

was

discovered

only  after

the

police  escalated  defendant’s

detention

from

that  of

a  mere  traffic  citation  incident  to

something

more.

Applicable

here

is

the

Burrell]

Court’s

observation  that,  “rather  than  having  a  crime  and  looking  for  the

criminal[}],

the  police,

here,

had  the

‘criminal(}":  and  were

looking  for  the  crime.”

417  Mich  459.

While  the  results  of

such  police  activity  may  --  and  in  this  case,  were  ‘*  very

efficacious,  such  ends,  in  a  constitutional  matter  as  that  now  in

issue,  cannot  justify  the  means  employed.

As  also  noted  by the

10

11

>|

ee.

.,

f.

a

.

URT  OF  APPEA

onus  tht  i  a,  it  is  of  course  painful  to  suppress

otherwise  good  and  highly  incriminating  evidence  that  led  to  a

defendant’s

conviction,

but

the

loss

of  that  evidence

“is

balanced  by  the  fact  that  it  is  only  in  difficult  situations  such

as  those  presented  here  that  we  can  draw  the  fine-lines  between

the  authority  of  the  police  to  protect  us  from  crime  and  the  use

by  the  police  of  governmental  power  to  violate  our  constitutional

rights.”

Id.

;

Accordingly,  having  reviewed  the  entire  record  in  this

case,

we  are  firmly  convinced  that  the  trial  court  erred  in

denying  defendant’s  pretrial  motion  to  suppress  the  narcotics

evidence  used  against  him  at  trial  because  we  are  unable  to  find

specific  facts  which  would  support  a  reasonable  suspicion  of

criminal  activity  sufficient  to  justify  ordering  defendant  out  of

his  car  after  being  stopped  for  a  simple  traffic  violation.

Since  the  incriminating  evidence  in  this  case  was  the  fruit  of  an

illegal  detention  effected  by  the  police,  it  should  have  been

suppressed.  Wong  Sun,  su

;  Shabaz,  supra.

In

light

of

our

decision

to

reverse

defendant’s

convictions  on  the  basis  of  his  illegal  detention,  the  other

issues

he  raises

on  appeal  need  not  be  addressed.

Burrell,

Supra,  p  460,  n  30.

Reversed.

/s/  Myron  H.  Wahls
/s/  Harold  Hood

In  this  case,  defendant  volunteered  the  fact  that  he

was  carrying  a  gun,

for  which  he  had  a  general  permit,

in  a

Seeing  what  he  believed  were

defendant  was  carrying  other  undisclosed  weapons  on  his  person.

This  suspicion  was  enhanced  by  defendant's

extremely  nervous

demeanor  and  their  location  in  a  high  crime  area,  and  Officer  Rix

became  concerned  for  his  own  safety.

not

if  considered  separately,

considered

together

the

search

was

a

justifiable

intrusion  upon  defendant's  Fourth  Amendment  rights.

The  purpose

of  the  pat-down  search  was  not  to  discover  evidence  of  a  crime,

but  to  protect  the  officers  from  harm  by  seizing  suspected  weapon

or  veapons.

(1977);  and  Ybarra  v  Illinois,  444  US  85,  93;  100  S' Ct  338,  343;

62  L  Ed  24  238,  247  (1979).

their  safety.

STATE  OF  MICHIGAN

COURT  OF  APPEALS

PEOPLE  OF THE  STATE  OF  MICITIGAN,

Plaintiff-Appclice,

v

RONALD  ALLEN  HARMELIN,

Defendant-Appcllant.

Before:  Wahis,  PJ.,  Hood  and  NJ.  Kaufman’*,  JJ.

PER  CURIAM.

April  18, 1989

FOR  PUBLICATION

No.  101536

(On Reconsidcration)

On  March  9, 1989,  this  Court,  on  its own  motion,  vacated  its judgment  in this  casc  issucd  on  January
9,  1989,  and  retained  the  maticr  for  rcconsidcration.  Of concern  was  the  cffcct  of a  constitutional  provision
and  its  case-law  precedent,  uncited  by the  partics,  on  the  issuc  of the  propricty  of a  police  officer  ordering  3
driver  out  of his  car  aftcr  having  stopped  the  driver  for  a  traffic  violation.  Two judges  of this  pancl  concluded
that  the  Michigan  Constitution's  scarch-and-scizure  provision  provided  greater  protection  to  drivers  in  such
2  situation  than  that  available  under  the  analogous  provision  of the  fedcral  constitution.  Under  the  latter,  a
police  officer's  order  to  a  driver  to  exit  his  car  after  the  car  has  been  lawfully  stopped  for  a  traffic  violation
does  not  violate  the  Fourth  Amendment  even  though  the  officer  had  no  reason  to  suspect  foul  play  at  the
time.  Pennsylvania  v Mimms,  434  US  106;  98 S Ct 330;  54 L Ed 2d 331  (1977).  The  third judge of this  pancl,
writing  in dissent,  concluded  that  the  record  did  not  support  the assumption  that  defendant  was  ordered  out  of
his  car  by the  police  and,  on  the  contrary,  suggested  that  defendant  had  voluntarity  gotten  out  of his  car  after
having  becn  lawfully  stopped  for a traffic  violation.

On  reconsidcration,  we  hold  that,  even  if defendant  in  this  case  were  ordered  out  of  his  car  by the
police  after  having  been  Icgally  stopped  for a traffic  violation,  the  narcotics  cvidence  revealed  after  subsequent
searches  could  not  have  been  suppresscd  from  evidence  under  the  Michigan  Constitution's  scarch-and-
<izure  provision  because,  undcr  the  circumstanccs  in  this  case,  that  provision  affords  defendant  with  no
greater  protection  than  that  provided  under  the  analogous  fedcral  provision  in  the  Fourth  Amendment.
Regarding  the  remaining  issucs  raised  by defendant  on  appeal  and  not  addresscd  in  the  January  9,  1989,
majority  opinion,  we  adopt  the  rcasoning  and  conclusions  sct  forth  in the dissenting  opinion  of that  date.

The  facts  in this  case  were  recited  ia our  carlicr  opinion:

and  a  mandatory  two-ycar  tcrm  of  imprisonment  for  the  fclony-fircarm  conviction.  On
appeal,  defend:  at  argucs  that  his  convictions  must  be  reversed  because  the  evidence  against
him  was  obtaincd  as  a  rcsult  of  an  unconstitutional  scizurc  and  pat-down  scarch  of  his

because  he was  deprived  of the  cffcctive  assistance  of counscl;  and  that  his  sentence  for  the

Fourth  Amendment's  prohibition  against unrcasonable  searches and scizurcs.  In  doing so, we followcd tt
dissent of Justice  Stevens  in Mimms,  and thus declarcd  that a police officcr, after stopping an automobile  for
traffic violation, could not order the driver to get out of the car, and thus protract the driver's detention, unic:_
the test announced  in Terry v  Ohio, 391 US  1; 88 S Ct 1868;  20 L Ed 2d 889 (1968),  had been  fulfilled,  ic  |
unless  the officer  had a¢casonabic,  articulabic  suspicion  that the driver committed,  or was about to  commit,
crime.  We  opined  that  maintaining  the  Terry  standard  in situations  involving  traffic  stops  by police  officer
would  help to ensure  that  the  right  to be free  from  unrcasonable  scizurcs  would  not  be wholly  croded  i:
traffic  offense  cascs  and,  as  a  corollary,  that  citizens  would  not  lawfully  be  vulncrable  to  unnecessary
intrusive,  and  intimidating  police commands.

such

a

On  reconsidcration,  we  find  that  whether  Justice  Stevens,  in  his  dissent  in  Mimms,  had  the  bettc
argument  on  this  issuc  is not  controlling  becausc  in this  case  we  are  obliged  to follow  the  majority  decision  ir
Mimms.  The  scarch-and-scizurc  provision  in the  Michigan  Constitution  provides:

"The  person,  houses,  papers  and  possessions  of every  person  shall  be  sccure  from
unrcasonable  scarches  aid  scizurcs.  No warrant  to scarch  any  placc  or  to scize  any  person
or  things  shall  issuc  without  describing  them,  nor  without  probable  cause,  supported  by oath
or  affirmation.  The provisions
of this section shall_not  be construed  to bar_from  ev:dence  in
any_criminal_procecding_any_narcotic_ drug, fircarm,  bomb,  explosive  or  anv  other  dangerous
aon scized_hy_a  dibsaa tiilens Aandi  the oadibuen  site  teaibie foaes to _this_state.”
(Emphasis  added.)  Const  1963,  art  1, §11.

The  language  of  this  scction  itsclf  specifics  that  the  protections  granted  under  the  section  shall  not  be
construcd  to bar  from  evidence  any  narcotic  drug scizcd  by a  police
tside  the cartilage  of a house.  In
this  case,  since  the  evidence  was  scizcd  from  defendant  by the  police  on  the  open  highway,  and  therefore
outside  the  curtilage  of a  house,  the  protections  granted  under  the  state's  scarch-and-scizure  provision  arc
not  available  to defendant.  Instead,  defendant  is entitled  only to those  protections  available  to  him  under  the
federal  constitution.  Sce  People  v  Catania,  427  Mich  447, 466,  n  12; 398  NW2d  343  (1986);  Pcoplc  v  Ragland,
149  Mich  App 277,  281;  385  NW2d  772  (1986);  People  v  Alfafara,  140  Mich  App  551, 560-561;  364  NW2d
743  (1985).  And,  as  already  stated,  under  federal  law,  the  police  were  entitled  to  order  defendant  out  of his
car  after  it was  lawfully  stopped  for  a  traffic  violation.  Mimms,  supra.

After  defendant's  automobile  was  lawfully  stopped  for  a  traffic  violation  and  aftcr  defendant  was
awfully  ordered  out  of his car,  it is clear  that  a pat-down  scarch  of his  person  was  warranted  under  the Terry
tandard,  i.c.,  it was  rcasonable  to  conclude  that  criminal  activity  might  have  been  afoot  and  that  defendant
night  have  been  armed  and  dangerous.  Scc  People  v  Payton,  166  Mich  App  428,  430-431;  421  NW2d  191
1988);  People  v  Peyton,  167  Mich  App  230,  236-237;  421  NW2d  643  (1988).  Defendant,  in a  high crime
rca  during  carly  morning  hours,  was  admittcdly  carrying  a  pistol  in  an  ankle  holster,  appearcd  to  be
xtremcly  nervous,  and  had  a bulge  in onc  of his  coat  pockcts  w  rich  Ied  the  arresting  officcr  to  belicve  that
cfendant  could  have  been  carrying  a  sccond  concealed  weapon,  Fearing  for  his  safcty.and  for  tha:  of his
artner,  and  belicving  that,  "[i}f [dcfendant]  had,  in fact, guns  in nis pockcts  where  the bulges were,  thcy could
ave  been  used  on  mc,  and  | wouldn't  have  had  a  chance  to  respond,”  the  officer  conducted  a  pat-down
rarch  on  defendant

mo

-#

In the

present  casc,.defendant,  prior  to ‘rial,  moved  to suppress  the  narcotics  evidence  against  him  on
ie  ground  as oe  detention  and  ama  scarch  to  which  he  was  subjected  violated  his  Fourth
mendment  rights.  It is well  established  that  evidence  derived  from  an  illcgal  scizure  must  be suppresscd  as
i¢  “fruit  of the  poisonous  tree."  Wong Sun  v  United  States, 371  US  471;  83  S Ct  407;  9 L Ed  2d  441
963);  Reople v Shabaz,  424  Mich  42, 65; 378  NW2d  451  (1985),  cert  dis 478  US  1017;  106  S Ct 3326;  92 L
4 2d 733  (1986).  The  trial  court  denicd  defendant's  pretrial  suppression  motion.  This  Court  will  not  disturb
trial  court's  ruling  on  a  suppression  motion  unlcss  that  ruling  is clearly  crroncous.  Payton,  supra,  p 430;
yon,  supra,  p 235; People v Burrell,  417  Mich  439, 448;  339 NW2d  403  (1983).  We will  find  such  a ruling
o}~

Pater

ee

v Daniels,  160  Mich
“clearly crroncous if firmly convinced that a  mistake has been made. People
617; 408 NW2d 398 (1987). We discern no reason to disturb the irial court's ruting in this case, —

REASONS  FOR  DENYING  THE  WRIT

I.

THE  TRIAL  COURT  COMMITTED  NO  ERROR  IN  DENYING  THE >

DEFENDANT'S  MOTION  TC  SUPPRESS.

In  the  trial  court  and  on  appeal,  the  Defendant  has

raised

numerous

challenges

to

the

propriety

of

the

various

searches  and  seizures  conducted  by  the  police  herein.

To  do  so,

the  Defendant  incorrectly  attempts  to  frame  the  Fourth  Amendment

|  issues  of  this  case  within  the  framework  of  a  "Terry"  investi-

gatory  stop,  see  Terry  v  Ohio,  392  US  1;  88  S  Ct  1868;  20  L  Ed  2d

889  (1968).

He  then  initially  arques  that  the  pat-down  search

|  resulting

in  the  discovery  of  drugs  on  his  person  was  invalid

since  the  police  officers  could  not  have  reasonably  suspected

that  criminal  activity  was  afoot.

The  Defendant's  reasoning  in  that  regard  is  factually

flawed  from  the  outset  since  the  stop  in  this  matter  was  a  valid

traffic  stop  for  the  civil  infraction  of  running  a  red  light,  MCL

257.611;  MSA  9.2311.

In  light  of  their  observations,  Officer  Rix

and  Blakeney  were  entirely  justified  in  conducting  the  traffic

stop  herein.

As  such,  the  initial  stop  was  not  within  the  mean-

ing  of  Terry,  supra.

See  also  People  v  Laube,  154  Mich  App  400;

397  NW2da  325  (1986).

person  was  armed  and  dangerous.
(358;  382  NW2d  786  (1985),  Sibron  v  New York,  392  US  40; 88  s_
(1889;  20  L  Ed  2a  917  (1968).

People  v  Parham,  147  tich

ee  wo.

or  vehicle  for  a  civil  infraction,  a  bulge  in  the  person's

search.

387  NW2d  B5¢

(1986),  Pennsylvania  v  Mimms,  434  US  106;  92  S, Ct  330;  54  L  at

Finally,

the  Defendant  attacks

the  legality  of

the

inventory  search  of  his  vehicle  and  of  the  satchel  discovered  in

Clearly,  however,.an  inventory  search  of  a  lawfully

impounded  vehicle  is  proper  as  long  as  it  is  conduct -d  pursuant

to  a  standard  police  department  procedure.

People  v  Long  (On

Remand),  419  Mich  636;  359  NW2d  194  (1984).

In  the  instant  case,

Officer  Rix  testified  that  both  the  vehicle  impoundment  and  the

inventory  search  were  conducted  pursuant  to  established  proce-

dures.

The  inventory  search  of  the  trunk  of  a  vehicle  is  also

permissible  when  reasonably  conducted  to  protect  the  police  or

public  or  to  avoid  claims  ‘against  the  police  for  allegedly  lost

Il.

THE  DEFENDANT  WAS  NUT  DENIED  EFFECTIVE  ASSISTANCE

With  reference  to  the  above  argument,

the  Defendant

alleges  that  his  trial  attorney  was  ineffective  insofar  as  he

e
oe ee
ee eee
ee cee

couched  the  trial  defense  in  terms  of  a  legal  attack  on  the

validity  of  the  ‘searches  herein  as  opposed  to  a  factual  attack  on

the  evidence  probative  of  the  Defendant's  guilt.

Apparently,  the

Defendant  believes  that  his  attorney  should  have  been  more  force-

ful  in  arguing  his  innocence  based  upon  the  facts.

A  review  of

the  record  herein  as  well  as  the  applicable  law  shows  that  the

ee

Defendant's  claim  is  factually  and  legally  unsupported.

In  People  v  Dalessandro,  165  Mich  App  569,

573;

419

NW2d

609

(1988),

the

Court

of  Appeals

adopted

the

federal

standard  for  determining  claims  of  ineffective  assistance  of

counsel  as  follows:

of

of

assistance

ineffective

In  Strickland  v  Washington,  466  US  668;  104
S  Ct  2052;
the  Supreme
80  L  Ed  2d  E74  (1984),
Court  set  forth  the  test  to  be  used  in  reviewing
claims
counsel.
First,  defendant  must  show  that  counsel's  perfor-
mance  was  deficient.
This  requires  showing  that
counsel  made  errors  so  serious  that  counsel  was
not  functioning  as  the  “counsel”  guaranteed  the
The  objec-
defendant  under  the  Sixth  Amendment.
tive  standard  of  reasonableness  must  be  used  i:
this
determining  whether  defense  counsel  meets
threshold.
The
Strickland,  supra,  pp  687-688.
defendant  must  overcome  the  presumption  that  the
challenged  action  might  be  considered  sound  trial
the  deficiency
strategy.
must  be  prejudicial  to  the  defendant.
An  error
by  counsel,  even  if  professionally  unreasonable,
does  not  warrant  setting  aside  the  judgment  of  a
criminal  proceeding  if  the  error  had  no  effect  on
the  judgment.

Id.,  pp  691-692.

Second,

p  689.

Id.,

EVALUATION  AND  PLAN

Ronald  Allen  Harmelin  is a  forty-two  year  old  male  who
claims  to  be  single.  Born  in  Pennsylvania,  he  was  raised
in  New  Jersey,  where  he graduated  from  high school  and
subsequently  entered  the  United  States  Air  Force.  He  has
resided  in the Detroit  area  for the past eight  years.  He has
no  known  legitimate  employment,  and  claims  he is a self-
employed  pool  player  and  courier  of  gambling  money.
Defendant  denied  cocaine  and  heroin  usage,  however,
admitted  to  the  use  of the  controlled  substance  Percodan
for  approximately  five  months  prior  to  his  arrest  in  the
instant  offense.

The  defendant  has  no  previous  criminal  record.  He  was
interviewed  in the Oakland  County  Jail for  this  investiga-
tion,  and  denied,  criminal  culpability,  claiming  he
thought  he  was  transporting  gambling  money.  He  was
unable  to  provide  this  writer  with  family  members’
addresses.  He appeared  to be guarded  in disclosing  infor-
mation  requested  of  him.

Police  investigators  described  the  defendant  as  a  large-
scale  drug  trafficker,  delivering  down  to  mid-level
dealers.

In accordance  with  the  mandatory  statute  associated  with
this  conviction,  incarceration  is  recommended.

INVESTIGATOR’S  DESCRIPTION  OF  THE  OFFENSE:

The  defendant  is  convicted  of  Count  I,  Possession  Of
Controlled  Substance  Over  650  Grams  -  Cocaine  and
Count  II,  Possession  Of  Firearm  While  Committing  Fel-
ony.  Two  additional  counts,  Possession  Of  Controlled
Substance  —  Cocaine  Less  Than  Fifty  Grams  and  Posses-
sion  Of  Firearm  In  Commission  of  Felony  were  dis-
missed.

This  offense  occurred  on  5-12-86  in  the  vicinity  of 8 Mile
and  Coolidge  in Oak  Park  at approximately  5:10  a.m.  On
that  date,  police  on  routine  patrol  pulled  defendant  over
per  a  routine  traffic  violation.  Defendant,  who  was  alone
at  the time,  presented  police  with  his  LD.  in addition  to a
C.C.W.  General  Permit  for  the  .38  five-shot  revolver  that
he  had  on  his  person  in  an  ankle  holster.  At  this  point,
police  observed  bulges  in the defendant's  coat  pocket  and
conducted  a  patdown  of his  person  and  discovered  mari-
juana  cigarettes,  four  brass  cocaine  straws,  and  one
cocaine  spoon,  and  three  vials  containing  a white  powder
and  suspected  cocaine,  twelve  Percodan  tablets,  and
twenty-five  capsules  later  revealed  to  be  Phen-
dimetrazine  Tartrate,  a  controlled  substance;  a  Motorola
beeper,  and  $597.78  in  cash.  At  this  point,  the  defendant
was  arrested  and  taken  into  custody.

The  defendant’s  1977  Ford  LTD  vehicle  was  impounded
and  during  a  police  inventory  of such,  found  in the  trunk
was  a  valise  containing  672.5  grams  of cocaine.  Lab  anal-
ysis of such  revealed  no  dilutants  detected.  Also  found  in
the  valise  with  the  cocaine  was  $2,900  in  cash  and  an
address  book,  as  well  as  a  number  of personal  toiletries.
Police  conducted  a  lab  check  of  various  items  found  in

iti

uy  wa eee a

.

_e  ee  ee

10

.

fo li Oe  Ane  a» |  be do

sre

”~

~~.  ere
ee

Nie  i

=

the  valise  and  linked  such  to  the  defendant  per  finger-
prints.

The  defendant  has  been  held  in  custody  since  the  arrest
date  in  lieu  of  $500,000  bond.

According  to  investigators  involved  in  this  case,  on  the
night  of the  instant  offense,  the  defendant  had  booked  a
room  at  the  Embassy  Hotel  in  Oak  Park,  an  area  known
for  a  high  rate  of  drug  trafficking  and  auto  theft.  The
defendant  is a Detroit  resident,  residing  in the  8 Mile  and
Dequindre  area.  The  defendant  was  also  registered  for  a
room  at  the  Sagamore  Motel  in  Royal  Oak,  since  4-18-86.
Police  investigation  at  the  time  of  the  instant  offense
found  a  seventeen  year  old  female  in  the  room.

Det.  Jankowski  of  the  Oak  Park  Police  Department  indi-
cated  that  although  the  defendant  has  never  held  a  viable
employment  and  described  himself  as  a  self-employed
courier,  receiving  and  delivering,  he  applied  and  was
granted  a  General  C.C.W.  Permit  in  Wayne  County.

N.E.T.  Investigators  and  Oak  Park  Police  described  the
defendant  as  a  large-scale  drug  dealer,  delivering  down
to mid-level  dealers.  N.E.T.  Investigators  indicate  that  the
street  price  of  the  cocaine  confiscated  in  the  instant
offense  from  defendant  is between  $67,000  and  $100,000.

VICTIM  IMPACT:

The  cocaine,  defendant’s  jewelry  and  possessions,  as  well
as  $3,497.78  in  cash,  was  confiscated  by police  under  the
Forfeiture  Act.

OFFENDER’S  DESCRIPTION  OF  THE  OFFENSE:
“I was  stopped  for Moving  Violation.  At  that  time,  I was
subject  to, in my opinion,  an  illegal  search  after  I showed
Officer  that  I had  a  gun  license  &  gun  permit.  Upon
search  of my  car,  Officer  searched  my  person  (reaching
hands  into  my pockets).  He came  out  with  coin  envelope
which  contained  some  packets  of  cocaine  &  place  me
under  arrest.  The  rest  happened  at  police  station  where
they  claimed  to  find  670  grams  of cocaine  in  suitcase
(closed),  inside  of  shaving  kit  (closed),  inside  of  paper
bag  (folded  up).  The  suitcase  (over-night  bag)  was
located  inside  trunk  of  my  car.”

Defendant  is  the  second  of  three  children  born  to  his
parents’  union  and  raised  in  Philadelphia,  Pennsylvania
until  the  age  of six,  at  which  time  he  claimed  the  family
relocated  to  South  Jersey,  Hadden  Heights,  and  subse-
quently  Sommerdale,  New  Jersey  in Camden  County.  The
defendant  graduated  in  June  of  1983  from  Sterling  High
School  in  Sommerdale,  ranking  92  out  of  133.  At  this
time,  his  family  resided  at  8 Glouster,  Sommerdale,  New
Jersey.  After  graduating,  defendant  entered  the  U.S.  Air
Force  and  claims  he was  stationed  in Washington,  D.C.  at
Bolling  A.F.B.,  where  he was  an  Honor  Guard.  In 1967,  he
claims  he was  Honorably  Discharged,  Rank  E-4,  from  the
Air  Force.  Defendant  claims  to  be  single  and  denied
paternity  of children.  He indicated  he relocated  to Detroit
approximately  eight  years  ago.  At  the  time  of the  instant
offense,  he claims  he  was  residing  alone  in  an  apartment
at  20481  Flemming,  Apt.  14,  Detroit.

EMPLOYMENT  &  ECONOMIC  STATUS:

Defendant  has  no  known  legitimate  employment.  He
claims  he  is self-employed  as  a  courier,  delivering  gam-
bling  money.  He  then  classified  himself  as  a  pool  player
and  indicated  that  he relocated  to  Detroit  to  pool-hustle.
He claims  he earns  $500  to $800 a week cash  playing pool.
The  defendant  does  not  file  income  tax.  It  should  be
noted  defendant  was  granted  a  General  C.C.W.  Gun  Per-
mit  in  Wayne  County  in  January  of  1986,  claiming  he
needed  such  for  business,  as  he  was  a  courier.

Defendant  claims  no  assets  or  liabilities.  He  has  retained
his  attorney  for  $15,000,  who  has  been  paid  in  full  by an
associate  of  the  defendant’s,  Ed  White,  according  to
police  investigation.

Upon  arrest  in the instant  offense,  police confiscated  from
defendant  $597.78  cash  which  was  on  his  person  and
$2,900  in cash  which  was  in the overnight  bag in the truck
of  his  vehicle,  where  the  drugs  were  found.  Also  confis-
cated  was  defendant’s  1977  LTD  Ford,  a  Taurus  .38  spe-
cial  revolver  with  ammunition  and  ankle  holster,  two
gold  chains  which  defendant  was  wearing  value  $100,
one  cloverleaf  pendant  and  one  eagle  pendant  valued  at
$100,  one  gold  bracelet  valued  at  $100,  one  gold  cigarette
lighter valued  at $50, one  Motorola  beeper  valued  at $100,
all  on  defendant’s  person.

SUBSTANCE  ABUSE:

Defendant  claims  he  has  never  used  narcotic  drugs  other
than  Percodan,  which  he claims  he began  using  in 2-1986
until  his arrest  in the instant  offense,  5-1986.  He claims  he

13

14

was  using  seven  Percodan  tablets  per  day,  had  no  pre-
scription  for  such,  and  used  due  to  a  toothache  and  his
fear  of dentists.  He claims  he suffered  withdrawal  symp-
toms  when  incarcerated  in  the  instant  offense.  He  also
acknowledged  use  of  marijuana.  The  defendant  denied
drug trafficking;  however,  in addition  to the cocaine  con-
fiscated  from  defendant,  he also  had  on  his  person  upon
arrest  four brass  cocaine  straws,  one  cocaine  spoon,  and a
brass  cigarette  case  containing  six  marijuana  cigarettes
and  25 capsules  of Phendimetrazine  Tartrate,  a controlled
substance.  Also  confiscated  was  $3,497.78  in  U.S.  Cur-
rency.

MENTAL  &  PHYSICAL  HEALTH:
The  defendant  is  in  good  physical  health  and  has  not
been  hospitalized  for  any  illnesses  or  diseases  recently.
