Before:  Wahls,  P.J.,  Hood  and  N.J.  Kaufman",  JJ.

PER  CURIAM

Defendant,  Ronald  Allen  Harmelin,  was  —.
a  bench  trial  of  possession  of 650  or  more  grams  0
pr MCL  333.7403(2)(); MSA  14.15  (7403)(2)(i),  and
felony-firearm,  MCL  750.227b;  MSA  28.424(2),  and was
sentenced  on  April 30, 1987,  by the Oakland  Circuit Court
to  a  mandatory  life  term  of imprisonment  for  the cocaine
conviction  and  a  mandatory  two-year  term  of  imprison-
ment  for  the  felony-firearm  conviction.  On  appeal,  defen-
dant  argues  that  his convictions  must  be reversed  because
the  evidence  against  him  was  obtained  as  a  result of an
unconstitutional  seizure  and  pat-down  search  of his  per-
son  and  an  unconstitutional  search  of  his  car;  that his
convictions  must  be reversed  because  he was  deprived  of
the  effective  assistance  of counsel;  and  that  his  sentence

*  Retired  Court  of  Appeals  judge,  sitting  on  the  Court  of

Appeals  by assignment.

18

for  the  cocaine  conviction  must  be set  aside  and  a  resen-
tencing  occur  because  a  mandatory  life  sentence  without
the  possibility  of  parole  constittutes  [sic]  cruel  and
unusual  punishment.  We  find  that  defendant’s  convic-
tions  must  be reversed  because  the evidence  used  against
him  was  obtained  as  a  result  of an  unconstitutional  sei-
zure  of  his  person.

The  record  reveals  that  in the  early  morning  hours  of
May  12,  1986,  two  Oak  Park  police  officers,  Calvin  Rix
and  Lawrence  Blakeney,  were  patrolling  in  a  marked
police  car  in the  area  of the  Embassy  Motel  in the City of
Oak  Park,  and  at 2:45  a.m.  drove  into  the  motel’s  parking
lot  to check  for stolen  vehicles.  Numerous  stolen  vehicles
had  been  found  in  this  lot  in  the  past.  At  this  time,  the
officers  observed  defendant's  car  exiting  the  parking  lot,
and  although  they  were  called  away  on  other  police
business  at  various  times  that  morning,  they  saw  defen-
dant’s  car  entering  the  Embassy  Motel  parking  lot  at
about  4:00  a.m.  and  again  at  5:00  a.m.  In  the  three  sight-
ings  of  defendant’s  car,  the  officers  noticed  nothing
unusual  or  illegal  in the  manner  in which  defendant’s  car
was  being  driven.

Shortly  after  5:00  a.m.,  while  continuing  to  patrol  the
area  around  the  Embassy  Motel,  Officers  Rix  and
Blakeney  saw  defendant's  car  make  a U-turn  at  the  inter-
section  of  Eight  Mile  and  Hubbell  Roads,  a  high-crime
area,  without  stopping  for  a  red  light.  When  the  officers,
in  their  patrol  car  with  its  overhead  lights  flashing,  pul-
led  behind  defendant's  vehicle,  defendant  responded  by
stopping  in  a  reasonably  prompt  fashion.  Officer  Rix
walked  to  the  driver’s  side  of  defendant’s  car,  while
Officer  Blakeney  positioned  himself  at  the  passenger  side

19

20

ee  ee ee ,

.

of  the  car.  Defendant,  who  remained  seated  in  his  car,
complied  in  a  cooperative  manner  upon  being  requested
by Officer  Rix  to  produce  a  driver's  license  and  vehicle
registration.  Defendant  then  stepped  out  of his  car.  Nei-
ther  of  the  officers  could  recall  whether  defendant  was
ordered  out  of his  vehicle  or  whether  he  got out  of it on
his  own  initiative,  although  Officer  Rix  testified  that  if he
did  in  fact  order  defendant  out  of  the  car,  that  order
would  have  been  prompted  solely  by the  nervous  behav-
ior  exhibited  by defendant  —  behavior  described  as  defen-
dant’s  having  “trouble  getting  some  of the  words  out.”  In
any  event,  after  exiting  his  car,  defendant,  in  a  nervous
manner,  informed  Officer  Rix  that  he  was  carrying  a
pistol  concealed  in an  ankle  holster.  He  explained  that  he
had  a  permit  for  the  weapon,  however,  and  that  he  did
not  want  the officers  to become  alarmed.  He  then  handed
Officer  Rix  a  general  permit  to carry  a  concealed  weapon
as  well  as  a safety  inspection  certificate  for the  .38 caliber,
five-shot  revolver,  which  was  later  found  in  his  ankle
holster.

After  looking  at  the  firearm  documents  proffered  by
defendant,  Officer  Rix  observed  nothing  which  suggested
that  the  papers  were  possibly  invalid.  Nonetheless,
because  defendant  appeared  to  be  nervous,  because  he
was  armed,  albeit  legally,  and  because  he  had  a  bulge  in
one  of his  coat  pockets,  Officer  Rix, fearing  for  his  own
safety  and  sensing  that  “[t]here  was  something  wrong,”
decided  to  conduct  a  pat-down  search  of  defendant.  In
doing  so,  some  marijuana  was  discovered,  and  defendant
was  placed  under  arrest.

In  searching  defendant  after  his  arrest,  the  officers
found  other  incriminating  evidence  in  defendant's  pos-
session,  including  assorted  pills and  capsules,  three  vials
of  white  powder,  ten  baggies  of  white  powder,  drug
paraphernalia  and  a  telephone  beeper.  Later,  pursuant  to
the  standard  policy  of  the  Oak  Park  police  department
defendant's  car  was  impounded  for  a  possible  forfeiture
action, and  an  inventory  search  was  conducted.  Durin
this  search,  a  satchel  containing  a  shaving-kit  bag  i
discovered  in  the  trunk.  The  kit  contained  $2900  in  cash
and  two  bags  of white  powder  subsequently  determined
to  be  672.5  grams  of  cocaine.  A  fingerprint  expert  testi-
fied  that  defendant's  fingerprints  were  found  on  books
located  inside  the  satchel  and  next  to  the  bags of cocaine.

Defendant  neither  testified  in  his  own  behalf  nor

presented  any  witnesses.

On  appeal,  defendant  first  argues  that  the  detention
and  pat-down  search  conducted  by the police  violated  his
Fourth  Amendment  right  to  be  secure  against  unreason-
able  seizures  and  did  not  fall  within  the  stop-and-frisk
exception  to  the  warrant  requirement  first  enunciated  in
Terry v Ohio,  392  US 1; 88 S Ct 1868;  20 L Ed 2d 889  (1968)
because  the detention  and  search  could  not  be justified  b
any  articulable  suspicion  or  objective  manifestation  at
he ‘was  engaged,  or  about  to  be  engaged,  in  criminal
activity.  In  Terry,  the  locus  classicus  of stop-and-frisk  law
the  United  States  Supreme  Court  created  a  limited  exce  ‘
tion  to  the  requirement  of showing  probable  cause  in  he
seizure  of a  person  by a police  officer  if the  officer  had  a
reasonable,  articulable  suspicion  that  the  person  commit-
ted,  or  was  about  to  commit,  a  crime.  Under  the  Terry

21

rule,  once  an  officer  has  reasonably  concluded  that  crimi-
nal  activity  may  be  afoot,  he  can  conduct  a  pat-down
search  if he  reasonably  concludes  that  the  person  seized
may  be  armed  and  dangerous.  See  People  v  Payton,  166
Mich  App  428,  430-431;  421  NW2d  191  (1988);  People  v
Peyton,  167  Mich  App 230,  236-237;  421  NW2d  643  (1988).
In  the  present  case,  defendant,  prior  to  trial,  moved  to
suppress  the  narcotics  evidence  against  him  on  the
ground  that  the  detention  and  pat-down  search  to  which
he  was  subjected  violated  his  Fourth  Amendment  rights.
Of  course,  it  is  well  established  that  evidence  derived
from  an  illegal seizure  must  be suppressed  as  the  “fruit  of
the  poisonous  tree.”  Wong Sun  v  United  States,  371  US 471;
83  S Ct  407;  9 L  Ed  2d  441  (1963);  People  v.  Shabaz,  424
Mich  42, 65; 378  NW2d  451  (1985),  cert  dis___  US ___;  106
S Ct  3326;  __  L Ed  2d  ___  (1986).  The  trial  court  denied
defendant's  pretrial  suppression  motion.  This  Court  will
not  disturb  a  trial  court’s  ruling  on  a  suppression  motion
unless  that  ruling  is  clearly  erroneous.  Payton,  supra,  p
430;  Peyton,  supra,  p 235;  People  v  Burrell,  417  Mich  439,
448;  339  NW2d  403  (1986).  We  will  find  such  a  ruling
clearly  erroneous  if firmly  convinced  that  a  mistake  has
been  made.  People  v  Daniels,  160  Mich  App  614,  617;  408
NW2d  398  (1987).

In  the  present  case,  defendant,  without  doubt,  was
lawfully  stopped  for  running a red  light,  which  is a  civil
infraction,  MCL  257.611;  MSA  9.2311;  People  v  Laube,  154
Mich  App 400, 407;  397 NW2d  325  (1986),  lv den  428  Mich
856  (1987).  It seems  fairly  clear  that  after  defendant  was
lawfully  stopped  and  produced  his  driver’s  license  and
vehicle  registration,  Officer  Rix  ordered  defendant  to  get
out  of  his  car.  Although  defendant  did  not  testify,  and

>

22

thus  did  not  inform  the  court  that  he  was  in  fact  ordered
out  of his car,  and  although  Officer  Rix  could  not  remem-
ber  whether  he  ordered  defendant  out  of  his  car  or
whether  defendant  exited  the  automobile  of  his  own
accord,  it  seems  highly  unlikely  to  us  that,  under  the
circumstances  presented  in  this  case,  defendant  would
have  gotten  out  of  his  car  without  being  told  to  do  so.
The  question  thus  becomes  whether  this  escalation  in the
intrusiveness  of the stop  was  justified  by the traffic  viola-
tion  alone  and,  if not,  whether  the  additional  detention
was  supported  by a  reasonable,  articulable  basis  on  the
part  of the  police  for suspecting  criminal  activity.  We  are
mindful,  during  our  examination  of this  matter,  that  “the
intrusiveness  of the  police  activity  must  be carefully  lim-
ited  to  the  circumstances  justifying  the  detention.”  Bur-
rell,  supra,  457.

With  respect  to  the  Fourth  Amendment  of the  United
States  Constitution,  the  reasonableness  of  requesting  a
driver  to  get  out  of  a  vehicle  following  a  lawful  traffic
stop  has  been  answered  by  the  United  States  Supreme
Court  in  Pennsylvania  v Mimms,  434  US  106;  98  S Ct  330;
54  L  Ed  2d  331  (1977).  In  Mimms,  a  majority  of  the
Supreme  court  held  that  a  police  officer’s  order  to  a
driver  to  exit  his  car  after  the  car  was  lawfully  stopped
for  a  traffic  violation  did  not  violate  the  Fourth  Amend-
ment,  even  though  the  officer  had  no  reason  to  suspect
foul  play  at  the  time,  since  the  obvious  interest  in  the
officer’s  safety  outweighed  the  de  minimus  intrusion
involved  in what  was  described  as  “at  most  a mere  incon-
venience”  to  the  driver.  98 S Ct 333.  The  Mimms  majority
noted  that,  according  to  one  study,  approximately  30%  of
police  shootings  occurred  when  a  police  officer

23

24

approached  a  suspect  seated  in  an  automobile  and  that
the  hazard  of accidental  injury  from  passing  traffic  to  an
officer  standing  on  the  driver’s  side  of a  stopped  vehicle
may  also  be  appreciable  in  some  situations.  Accordingly,
under  federal  law,  once  a  police  officer  has  stopped  a
vehicle  for a  traffic  violation  he has  the authority,  without
more,  to  order  the  driver  out  of  the  car.

.

In  the  dissent  in  Mimms  written  by Justice  Stevens
and  in  which  Justices  Brennan  and  Marshall  joined,  it  is
stated  that  “[t]he  Court  has  based  its  legal  ruling  on  a
factual  assumption  about  police  safety  that  is dubious  at
best  [and]  .
.  has  created  an  entirely  new  legal  standard
of justification  for  intrusions  on  the  liberty  of the citizen.”
98  S  Ct  337.  Regarding  the  majority’s  reliance  on  one
study’s  statistical  finding  that  “approximately  30%  of all
police  shootings  occurred  when  the  police  officer
approached  a  suspect  seated  in  an  automobile,”  98  S Ct
333,  Justice  Stevens  stated:

“That  statement  does  not  fairly  characterize  the
study  to  which  it  refers.  Moreover,  the  study  does  not
indicate  that  police  officers  can  minimize  the risk  of being
shot  by ordering  drivers  stopped  for  routine  traffic  viola-
tions  out  of  their  cars.  The  study  reviewed  110  selected
police shootings  that  occurred  in  1959,  1960,  and  1961.7  In
35 of those  cases,  ‘officers  were  attempting  to investigate,
control,  or  pursue  suspects  who  were  in  automobiles.’®
Within  the  group  of  35  cases,  there  were  examples  of
officers  who  ‘were  shot  through  the  windshield  or  car
body  while  their  vehicle  was  moving’;  examples  in  which
from  his  vehicle

or  while  approaching  the suspect[’]s  vehicle’;  and,  appar-
ently,  instances  in  which  the  officer  was  shot  by a  pas-
senger  in  the  vehicle.  Bristow,  [Police  Officer  Shootings  -
A Tactical  Evaluation,  54 J. Crim.  L.C.  & PS.  93  (1963)],  n.
7, at  93.

Payton
“Conflicting  advice  is found  in an  earlier  work,  G.  yton,
Poses Sea 298  (4th  ed.  1971).  It  is  worth  noting  that
these  authorities  suggest  that  any  danger  to  the  officer  oo
passing  traffic  may  be  greatly  reduced  by  the  simple  an

unintrusive  expedient  of  parking  the  police  car  behind,  and
two  or  three  feet  to  the  left  of,  the  offender’s  vehicle.  Folley,
supra,  at  93;  Payton,  supra,  at  301;  Yount,  supra  at  2.”  98  S Ct
337-338.

The  dissent  by justice  Stevens  also  notes  that a state  is
free  to  provide  its citizens  with  greater  protection  in  this
matter  than  that  afforded  under  the  Fourth  Amendment
of  the  United  States  Constitution.  98  S Ct  336.  It is  well
established  that  state  constitutions  can  provide  greater
protections  than  those  afforded  by the  federal  constitu-
tion.  People  v  Thompson,  424  Mich  118,  125;  379  NW2d  49
(1985).

We  agree  with  the  dissent  of  Justice  Stevens  in
Mimms  and  therefore  hold  that  a police  officer,  after  stop-
ping an  automobile  for a traffic  violation,  may  not  order
the driver  to  exit  the car,  and  thus  protract  the detention,
unless  the  test  announced  in  Terry has  been  fulfilled,  i.e.,
unless  the  officer  had  a  reasonable,  articulable  suspicion
that  the  driver  committed,  or  was  about  to  commit,  a
crime.  Maintaining  the  Terry  standard  in  situations
involving  traffic  stops  by  police  officers  will  help  to
ensure  that  the  right  to  be  free  from  unreasonable  sei-
zures  will  not  wholly  be  eroded  in  traffic  offense  cases
and,  as  a  corollary,  that  citizens  will  not  lawfully  be
vulnerable  to  unnecessary,  intrusive  and  intimidating
police  commands.  As  observed  by Justice  Stevens,  the
risk  to a police  officer  in the context  of a  legitimate  traffic
stop  cannot  be  so  universal  as  always  to  constitute  a
reasonable  justification  for  ordering a citizen  out  of  his
Car.

27

28

“The  commuter  on  his  way  home  to  dinner,  the  par-
ent  driving  children  to  school,  the  tourist  circling  the
Capitol,  or  the  family  on  a  Sunday  afternoon  outing
hardly  pose  the  same  threat  as  a  driver  curbed  after  a
high-speed  chase  through  a  high-crime  area  late  at  night.
Nor  is  it  universally  true  that  the  driver’s  interest  in
remaining  in  the  car  is  negligible.
A woman  stopped  at
night may  fear  for her own  safety;  a person  in poor  health
may  object  to  standing  in  the  cold  or  rain;  another  who
left  home  in haste  to drive  children  or  spouse  to school  or
to  the  train  may  not  be  fully  dressed;  an  elderly  driver
who  presents  no  possible  threat  of  violence  may  regard
the  police  command  as  nothing  more  than  an  arrogant
and  unnecessary  display  of  authority.  Whether  viewed
from  the  standpoint  of  the  officer’s  interest  in  his  own
safety,  or  of the  citizen’s  interest  in  not  being  required  to
obey  an  arbitrary  command,  it  is  perfectly  obvious  that
the  millions  of traffic  stops  that  occur  every  year  are  not
fungible.”  98  S Ct  338.

In addition,  maintaining  the  Terry standard  in this  context
will  help  to  avoid  the  arbitrary  exercise  of  the  power  to
force  a  citizen  out  of his  car  in  those  many,  many  circum-
stances  in  which  there  exist  no  suspicions  for  fearing  the
driver  but,  for  instance,  because  of the  political  message
of a  bumper  sticker  on  the car  or  the  color  of the  driver’s
skin,  the  driver  is  nevertheless  legally  obliged  to  obey
submissively  the  instruction  of  a  police  officer.  The  line
between  unfettered  discretion  and  discrimination  is a  fine
one  indeed.

We are  aware  that  the  majority  decision  in Mimms  has
been  cited  by  this  Court  with  apparent  approbation.  In
People  v  Laube,  154  Mich  App  400,  408-410;  397  NW2d  325

(1986),  lv den 428 Mich  856 (1987),  for example,  a panel of
this  court,  citing  Mimms,  rejected  an  argument  by  the
pri no a  stop  for  the  civil  infraction  of walking
on
the  shoulder  of a  road  with,  and  not  agains  ’
MCL  257.655,  MSA  9.2355,  did  not eidiaiibier the dite
deputies  to  extend  his  detention  or  conduct  a  frisk.  In
Laube,  however,  the defendant  himself  “consented  to, and
indeed,  appears  to  have  initiated,  an  extended  contact
with  the  officers,”  and  “repeatedly  placed  his  hands  in
his  pockets  while  inching  towards  the  rear  of  the  patrol
car.”  154  Mich  App  410.  These  circumstances  created  a
legitimate  concern  on  behalf  of  the  officers  regarding
their  safety.  Moreover,  in  People  v  Blackburne,  150  Mich
App  156,  164-165;  387  NW2d  850  (1986),  lv den  425  Mich
869 (1986),  a panel of this Court,  citing Mimms,  found  that
the  detaining,  and  pat-down  searches,  of  the  defendant
and  three  other  men  were  not  unreasonable  after  the
driver  of the  car  in  which  the  men  were  travelling  com-
mitted a traffic  violation  by driving  on  the  wrong  side  of
the  center  line  for a distance  of about  1/8th  of a  mile.  In
Blackburne,  however  the police  had  reasonable,  articulable
suspicions  that  criminal  activity  was  afoot  even  before
stopping  the  car  for  the  traffic  violation.  Before  stopping
the car  and  subjecting  its driver  and  passengers  to detain-
ment,  “[t]he  police  officers  reasonably  suspected  that  the
four  men  in  the  car  were  engaged  in  drug  shipment
activity.”  150  Mich  App  164.  Thus,  while  both  Laube  and
Blackburne  cite  Mimms,  neither  case,  to  support  its  result
relies  exclusively  on  the  holding  in  Mimms.
|
We  find  added  support  in  Peo ple v  Burrell,  417  Mich
439;  339  NW2d  403  (1983),  for  our  conclusion  that  the
Michigan  constitutional  Provision  regarding  freedom

29

from  unreasonable  searches  and  seizures,  Const  1963,  art
1, § 11, affords  more  protection  to  individuals  such  as  the
present  defendant  than  that  provided  under  federal  con-
stitutional  law.  In  Burrell,  the  defendant’s  vehicle  was
legitimately  stopped  by police  for  violation  of  a  statute
prohibiting  excessively  noisy  exhaust  systems,  MCL
257.707;  MSA  9.2407.  This  legitimate  stop  revealed  that
the  driver  had  no  driver’s  license  or  vehicle  registration.
Thus,  the  police  reasonably  suspected  that  the  vehicle
was  stolen.  The  Supreme  Court,  stressing  that  the  intru-
siveness  of police  activity  must  be carefully  limited  to  the
circumstances  justifying  the  detention,  found  that  the
police  were  able  to  detain  the  defendants  only  so  long  as
to  write  citations  for  the  noisy  exhaust  system  and  -  after
determining  by way  of a  LEIN  check  that  the  driver  was
licensed  and  that  the  car  was  not  reported  as  stolen  —  the
driver’s  failure  to  have  in  his  possession  a  valid  driver’s
license.  The  Court  stated:

“Analytically,  given  this  state  of  facts,  it  is  clear  that
more  than  one  roadside  detention  occurred.  Initially,
driver  Brown  was  properly  stopped  for  an  equipment
violation.  A detention  following  a  stop  for  such  a  minor
violation  would  be  justified  only  for  the  length  of  time
necessary  to  write  a  citation.  Here,  however,  the  stop
immediately  revealed  a new  set  of circumstances,  a driver
without  either  a  operator’s  license  or  a  vehicle  registra-
tion.  We  agree  that  it  was  reasonable  to  suspect  that  the
vehicle  was  stolen,  and  we  also  agree  that  detention  was
justified  long  enough  to  resolve  the  suspicion  raised.
When  the  LEIN  check  verified  that  Brown  was  licensed
and  that  the  car  was  not  reported  stolen,  that  suspicion
expired.  By 7:15  p.m.,  detention,  again,  could  be  justified

only for the length  of time  required  for Blackport  to write
a  citation  for  Brown’s  failure  to  have  a  valid  driver’s
license  in  his  possession  while  driving.”
Although  this  statement  concerns  only  the  duration  of a
detention,  it is clearly implied  that  the manner  of a deten-
rm should  be  no  more  intrusive  than  that  necessary  to
pes the  issue  or  issues  justifying  the detention  in the
st  place.  In  this  :egard,  the  Supreme  Court  made  the
following  observations  and  Statements:

“In  [United  States  v]  Brignoni-Ponce,  [4

881-882;  95 S Ct 2574;  45  L Ed  2d 607 NOVEM, tha Vaan
Supreme  Court  applied  the Terry ‘stop and  frisk’  doctrine
to  investigatory  Stops  of automobiles  by a  roving  border
patrol.  The  Court  condemned  a  random  stop  of a  vehicle
made  because  its  three  passengers  appeared  to  be  of
Mexican  descent.  The  Court  concluded  that  appearance
alone  was  an  insufficient  basis  to  allow  a  random  sto
and  brief  questioning.  This  result  flowed  naturally
the  Terry  requirement  that  more  than  ‘inchoate  and
unparticularized  Suspicion  or  ‘hunch’  ’  was  necessa
to
Justify  a  frisk  of  a  Suspicious  individual.

*

*

*

“In  Delaware  v Prouse,  [440  US 648;  99 S Ct  1391;  59  L
Ed  2d  660  (1979)],  the Court  required  that  a police  officer
who S‘-ps  and  detains  a  motorist  to  check  for  a  valid
driver’s  license  and  registration  have  at least  an  articula-
ble  and  reasonable  Suspicion  that  the  motorist  is
unlicensed  or  that  the  vehicle  is not  registered.  The  offi-
cer  in the case  testified  that he had  not observed traffic  or
equipment  violations  or  any  suspicious  activity  prior  to

making  the stop.  The  Court  ruled  that  his stop  was  unrea-
sonable.

“In  the  instant  case,  we  do  not deal  with  64
improper  stop.  Therefore,  neither  Brignoni-Ponce  nor  =~
aware  v  Prouse  is  dispositive.  However,  since  a  ied
almost  inevitably  leads  to  a  brief  detention  for  =~ “a

conclude  that  a  brief  detention  for questioning is perm
sible  if based  on  a  reasonable  and  articulable  suspicion
criminal  activity.

“Since  fourth  Amendment  rights  are  involved  ra a

detention,  however  brief,  the  intrusiveness  of the  po  “a
activity  must  be  carefully  limited  to  the  circumstance
justifying  the  detention.”  (Footnotes  omitted;  emphasis  in
original.)  417  Mich  455-457.

The  evidence  in  the  present  case  is clear that  smn
Rix  had  no  reasonable,  articulable  suspicion  that de ra
dant  was  engaged  in  criminal  activity  at  the  ongen
presumably,  was  ordered  out  of  his  car.  At  that  ti  - ,
Officer  Rix  knew  that  defendant’s  car  had frequen  7
gone  in  and  out  of a  motel  parking  lot  during  the  osttnd
morning  hours  of  May  12,  1986;  that  numerous  —
cars  had  been  found  in  the  parking  lot  in the  past;  =
defendant  had  illegally  run  a  red  light  in  a  —
area;  and  that  defendant  complied  in  a nag ots ol
ner  upon  being  requested  to  produce  his  driver  . ao
and  vehicle  registration.  We  fail  to  see  how  any  “speci

reasonable  inferences  of criminal  activity  could  possibly
be  drawn  from  these  facts.”  Burrell,  supra,  p 458.  Either
taken  singly  or  in toto,  they suggest  to us  nothing  partic-
ularly nefarious,  illicit,  improper  or  illegal.  It is only after
defendant  got out  of his car  that questions  of safety arose,
i.e.,  it  was  only  after  defendant  got  out  of  his  car  that
defendant  began acting  nervously,  displayed  a bulge in a
coat  pocket,  and  produced  a gun,  for which  he possessed
a  facially  valid  general  permit  and  safety  inspection  cer-
tificate.  This  latter  evidence,  however,  was  discovered
only after  the police  escalated  defendant's  detention  from
that  of a  mere  traffic  citation  incident  to something  more.
Applicable  here  is  the  Burrell  Court’s  observation  that,
“rather  than  having  a  crime  and  looking  for  the  crimi-
nal[],  the  police,  here,  had  the  ‘criminal[]’  and  were  look-
ing tor the crime.”  417 Mich  459.  While  the results  of such
police  activity  may  -  and  in  this  case,  were  —  very  effi-
cacious,  such  ends,  in a constitutional  matter  as  that  now
in  issue,  cannot  justify  the  means  employed.  As  also
noted  by  the  Supreme  Court  in  Burrell,  it  is  of  course
painful  to  suppress  otherwise  good  and  highly  incrimi-
nating  evidence  that  led  to  a  defendant's  conviction,  but
the  loss  of that  evidence  “is balanced  by the fact  that  it is
only  in  difficult  situations  such  as  those  presented  here
that  we  can  draw  the  fine  lines  between  the  authority  of
the  police  to  protect  us  from  crime  and  the  use  by the
police  of  governmental  power  to  violate  our  constitu-
tional  rights.”  Id.

Accordingly,  having  reviewed  the  entire  record  in
this  case,  we  are  firmly  convinced  that  the  trial  court
erred  in denying  defendant's  pretrial  motion  to  suppress
the  narcotics  evidence  used  against  him  at  trial  because
we  are  unable  to  find  specific  facts  which  would  support

—-_

33

34

a  reasonable  suspicion  of  criminal  activity  sufficient  to
justify  ordering  defendant  out  of  his  car  after  being
stopped  for  a  simple  traffic  violation.  Since  the  incrimi-
nating  evidence  in  this  case  was  the  fruit  of  an  illegal
detention  effected  by  the  police,  it  should  have  been
suppressed.  Wong  Sun,  supra;  Shabez,  supra.

In light of our  decision  to reverse  defendant’s  convic-
tions  on  the basis  of his  illegal  detention,  the  other  issues
he  raises  on  appeal  need  not  be addressed.  Burrell,  supra,
p 460,  n  30.

Reversed.

/s/  Myron  H.  Wahls
/s/  Harold  Hood

STATE  OF  MICHIGAN
COURT  OF  APPEALS

PEOPLE  OF  THE  STATE
OF  MICHIGAN,

Plaintiff-Appellee,

Vv
RONALD  ALLEN  HARMELIN,
Defendant-Appellant.

No.  101536

Before:  Wahls,  P.J.,  and  Hood  and  N.J.  Kaufman,”  JJ.

N.J.  Kaufman,  J. (dissenting)

I  vigorously  disagree  with  the  majority's  hinging
review  of  this  case  on  the  assumption  that  Officers  Rix
and  Blakeney  ordered  defendant  out  of  his  car.  The
record  reflects  that  neither  officer  could  recall  whether
defendant  was  ordered  out  of his  car  or  whether  defen-
dant  simply  got  out  on  his  own  initiative,  and  defendant
did  not  testify.

Unlike  the  majority,  I  do  not  find  it  in  the  least
unlikely  that  defendant  would  have  exited  his  car  with-
out  being  told  to  do  so.  To  the  contrary,  given  the  contra-
band  contained  in  the  trunk  of defendant's  car,  I find  it
very  understandable  that  defendant  would  get out  and  as
far away  from  his car  as  he could.  The  majority’s  assump-
tion  concerning  defendant’s  actions  is justified  by neither
the  record  nor  defendant’s  brief  on  appeal,  the  later  of
which  is  void  of any  reference  to  the  theory  adopted  by
the  majority.

;

Clearly,  defendant  was  lawfully  stopped  for  running
a  red  light,  a  civi!  infraction,  MCL  257.611;  MSA  9.2311.
People  v  Burrell,  417  Mich  439,  450;  339  NW2d  403  (1983);
People  v  Laube,  154  Mich  App  400,  407;  397  NW2d  325
(1986),  lv den  428  Mich  856  (1987).  The  police  officers,  in
conducting  the  pat-down  search,  must  have  concluded
that  defendant  was  armed  and  dangerous  based  on  a
specific,  articulable,  reasonable  inference  drawn  from  the
facts  for  the  frisk  to  have  been  a  reasonable  intrusion
upon  defendant’s  Fourth  Amendment  rights.  Terry v Ohio,

*  Former  Court  of  Appeals  judge,  sitting  on  the  Court  of
Appeals  by assignment.

35

36

392 US 1, 32; 88 S Ct 1868;  20 L Ed 2d 889  (1968);  Michigan
v  Summers,  452  US  692;  101  S Ct  2587;  69  L  Ed  “ ne
(1981);  People  v  Backburne,  150  Mich  App  156,  164;  3
NW2d  850  (1986),  lv  den  425  Mich  869  (1986).

In  this  case,  defendant  volunteered  the  fact  that  he
was  carrying  a gun,  for which  he had  a general  permit, o
a  holster  strapped  to  his  ankle.  Seeing  what  he  believ
were  several  bulges  in  defendant's  coat,  Officer  Rix  sus-
pected  that  defendant  was  carrying  other  a
weapons  on  his  person.  This  suspicion  was  enhanced  by
defendant’s  extremely  nervous  deineanor  and  their  loca-
tion  in  a  high  crime  area,  and  Officer  Rix  became  con-
cerned  for  his  own  safety.  Although  these  facts  may  not
justify  a  pat-down  search  if considered  separately,  im
considered  together  the  pat-down  search  was  a justifiable
intrusion  upon  defendant’s  Fourth  Amendment  rights.
The  purpose  of the  pat-down  search  was  not  to  discover
evidence  of a crime,  but  to  protect  the  way sig * gm

izi

spected  weapon  or  weapons.  /erry,

m4 yr »  Witlions, 407 US 143,  146;  92S  ct 1921,  1923;
32 L Ed  2d  612,  617  (1972);  Pennsylvania  v Mimms,  434 _
106,  110;  98 S Ct  330,  333;  54  L Ed  2d  331,  336-337  nasi
and  Ybarra  v Illinois,  444  US  85, 93;  100  S Ct 338,  343;  62  L
Ed  2d  238,  247  (1979).

There  is  no  need  to  strain  for  reversal  by  following
the  dissent  in  Mimms,  as  the  majority  in  this  case  has
done,  because  the  Terry  test  was  met:  the  officers  had  a
reasonable,  articulable  suspicion  that  defendant  was
armed  and  a  danger  to  their  safety.

Nor  do  any  of  defendant’s  four  other  claims  on

appeal  require  reversal.

First,  defendant  argues  that  his  right  not  to  be com-
pelled  to  testify  against  himself  was  violated  when  Offi-
cer  Rix  asked  him  about  the  hard  object  felt  in
defendant’s  chest  pocket.  This  conversation  took  place
prior  to  defendant's  arrest.  Officer  Rix  believed  the  hard
object  was  another  gun,  and  asked  defendant  what  it was.
Defendant  replied  that  it was  his  marijuana.  The  limited
constraint  conferred  upon  defendant’s  freedom  by  the
officers  conducting  the  pat-down  search  does  not  rise  to
the  level  of  a  custodial  interrogation,  and  precludes  a
finding  that  defendant’s  admission  was  compelled  by
police  action.  People  v  Hill,  429  Mich  382,  397;  415  NW2d
193  (1987).

Second,  the  warrantless  search  of  defendant’s  car
was  not  an  unjustifiable  intrusion  upon  defendant's
Fourth  Amendment  rights  such  that  the  fruits  of  this
search  should  have  been  suppressed.  Defendant's  car  was
properly  impounded  under  the  forfeiture  provisions  on
the  Controlled  Substance  Act,  MCL  333.7522;  MSA
14.15(7522).  However,  because  the  officers’  testimony
established  that  the  Oak  Park  Police  Department's  stan-
dardized  procedure  did  not  mandate  searching  and  con-
ducting  an  inventory  of  the  contents  of  every  container
within  the  vehicle,  the  inventory  search  done  in  this  case
does  not  fall  within  the  parameters  of Colorado  v  Bertine,
479  US  __;  107  S Ct  738;  93  L Ed  2d  739  (1987):  People  v
Krezen,  427  Mich  681;  397  NW2d  803  (1986).

I would  find  that  the  search  in  this  case  falls  under
the  automobile  exception  to  the  warrant  requirement
enunciated  in  United  States  v  Ross,  456  US  789;  102  S Ct
2157;  72  L Ed  2d  572  (1982),  and  adopted  by this  Court  in
People  v  Cruz,  161  Mich  App  238,  241-242;  409  NW2d  797

FE  ae  ee  ee  ee  eT  a

37

38

(1987),  lv den  430  Mich  854  (1988).  Ten  clear  plastic  bags
filled  with  white  powder,  three  small  vials  containing
white  powder,  assorted  pills and  capsules,  paraphernalia
of the type used  to ingest  cocaine,  and a telephone  paging
device  were  retrieved  from  defendant’s  person  after  his
arrest,  and  a gun  was  strapped  to defendant’s  ankle  when
the  officers  stopped  him.  These  facts  would  lead  a  rea-
sonably  prudent  person,  thus  police  had  a  probable
cause,  to  believe  that  defendant’s  car  contained  more
controlled  substances.  People  v  Goins,  164  Mich  App  559,
560;  417  NW2d  499  (1987).  Although  the  trial  court’s
denial  of  the  suppression  motion  was  not  based  on  the
automobile  exception  to  the  warrant  requirement,  re-
versal  is  not  mandated  where  the  trial  court  reaches  the
right  result  for  the  wrong  reasons.  People  v  Beckley,  161
Mich  App  120,  131;  409 NW2d  759  (1987),  lv gtd on  other
grds  430  Mich  857  (1988).

Third,  under  either  the  Michigan  or  federal  stan-
dard,’  defendant’s  claims  that  he  was  deprive  of  the
effective  assistance  of counsel  and  the  right  to  plead  not
guilty  because  (1) his  trial  counsel  offered  no  evidence  in
response  to  the  prosecutor’s  case-in-chief,  but  solely  con-
fined  the  defense  to  attacking  the  admissibility  of  evi-
dence  found  on  defendant’s  person  and  in his car,  and  (2)
counsel  did  not  argue  that  defendant  was  not  guilty  of
knowingly  possessing  the  cocaine  found  in  his  truck,  are
both  without  merit.  The  evidence  against  defendant  was
overwhelming,  and  counsel’s  strategy  in  concentrating
the  defense  on  the  admissibility  of the  disputed  evidence
focused  upon  defendant's  best  and  only chance  of acquit-
tal.  This  choice  of strategy  was  not  the  functional  equiva-
lent  of a  guilty  plea  proscribed  in People  v Schulz,  85 Mich

App  527;  271  NW2d  305  (1978),  and  defendant  was
afforded his constitutional  right to plead  not  guilty.  People
v  Fisher,  119  Mich  App  445;  326  NW2d  537  (1982).

Defendant's  last claim  is that  the mandatory  sentence
of  life  imprisonment  is entirely  out  of  proportion  to  the
seriousness  of  his  crime  and  constitutes  a  cruel  and
unusual  punishment  proscribed  by US  Const,  Am  VIIL,
and  Const  1963,  art  1,  § 16.  I  disagree.  See  People  v
Harding,  163  Mich  App  298,  329;  413  NW2d  777  (1987)
vacated  on  other  grds  430  Mich  858-859  (1988).
:
I would  affirm  defendant’s  convictions  and  sentence.

/s/  Nathan  J. Kaufman

STATE  OF  MICHIGAN

COURT  OF  APPEALS

(Caption  Omitted  In  Printing)
April  18,  1989
FOR  PUBLICATION
No.  101536
(On  Reconsideration)

Before:  Wahls,  P.J.,  Hood  and  N.J.  Kaufman”,  JJ.

PER  CURIAM.

On  March  9,  1989,  this  Court,  on  its  own  motion,
vacated  its  judgment  in  this  case  issued  on  January  9,
1989,  and  retained  the  matter  for  reconsideration.  Of
concern  was  the  effect  of a  constitutional  provision  and
its  case-law  precedent,  uncited  by  the  parties,  on  the
issue  of the  propriety  of a  police  officer  ordering  a driver
out  of his  car  after  having  stopped  the driver  for a  traffic
violation.  Two  judges  of  this  panel  concluded  that  the
Michigan  Constitution’s  search-and-seizure  provison  pro-
vided  greater  protection  to  drivers  in  such  a  situation
than  that  available  under  the  analogous  provision  of the
federal  constitution.  Under  the  latter,  a  police  officer's
order  to  a  driver  to  exit  his  car  after  the  car  has  been
lawfully  stopped  for  a  traffic  violation  does  not  violate
the  Fourth  Amendment  even  though  the  officer  had  no
reason  to  suspect  foul  play  at  the  time.  Pennsylvania  v
Mimms,  434  US  106;  98  S Ct  330;  54  L Ed  2d  331  (1977).

"Retired  Court  of  Appeals  judge,  sitting  on  the  Court  of
Appeals  by assignment.

41

42

a  re

eS

catalan

=,

‘

The  third  judge  of  this  panel,  writing  in  dissent,  con-
cluded  that  the  record  did  not  support  the  assumption
that  defendant  was  ordered  out  of  his  car  by the  police
and,  on  the  contrary,  suggested  that  defendant  had  vol-
untarily  gotten  out  of his  car  after  having  been  lawfully
stopped  for  a  traffic  violation.

On  reconsideration,  we  hold  that,  even  if defendant
in this  case  were  ordered  out  of his car  by the  police after
having  been  legally  stopped  for  a  traffic  violation,  the
narcotics  evidence  revealed  after  subsequent  searches
could  not  have  been  suppressed  from  evidence  under the
Michigan  Constitution’s  search-and-seizure  provision
because,  under  the  circumstances  in  this  case,  the  provi-
sion  affords  defendant  with  no  greater  protection  than
that  provided  under  the  analogous  federal  provision  in
the  Fourth  Amendment.  Regarding  the  remaining  issues
raised  by defendant  on  appeal  and  not  addressed  in  the
January  9, 1989,  majority  opinion,  we  adopt  the  reasoning
and  conclusions  set  forth  in the dissenting  opinion  of that
date.

The  facts  in  this  case  were  recited  in  our  earlier

opinion:

posse

“The  record  reveals  that  in  the  early  morn-
ing  hours  of May  12,  1986,  two  Oak  Park  police
officers,  Calvin  Rix  and  Lawrence  Blakeney,
were  patrolling  in  a  marked  police  car  in  the
area  of  the  Embassy  Motel  in  the  City  of  Oak
Park,  and  at  2:45  a.m.  drove  into  the  motel’s
era
lot  to  check  for  stolen  vehicles.
umerous  stolen  vehicles  had  been  found  in
this  lot  in  the
past.  At  this  time,  the  officers
observed  defendant's  car  exiting  the parking  lot,
and  although  they  were  called
away  on  other
police  business  at  various  times  that  morning,
they  saw  defendant’s  car  entering  the  Embassy
Motel  parking  lot at about  4:00  a.m.  and  again at
5:00  a.m.  In  the  three  sightings  of  defendant’s
car,  the  officers  noticed  nothing  unusual  or  ille-
gal in  the  manner  in  which  defendant's  car  was
being  driven.

“Shortly  after  5:00  a.m.,  while  continuing  to
patrol  the  area  around  the  Embassy  Motel,  Offi-
cers  Rix and  Blakeney  saw  defendant's  car  make
a  U-turn  at  the  intersection  of  Eight  Mile  and
Hubbell  Roads,  a  high-crime  area,  without  stop-
ping  for  a  red  light.  When  the  officers,  in  their
patrol  car  with  its  overhead  lights  flashing,
pulled  behind  defendant’s  vehicle,  defendant
responded  by stopping  in  a  reasonably  prompt
fashion.  Officer  Rix  walked  to  the  driver’s  side
of defendant’s  car,  while  Officer  Blakeney  posi-
tioned  himself  at  the  passenger  side  of  the  car.
Defendant,  who  remained  seated  in  his  car,

for  the  .38  caliber,  five-shot  revolver,  which  was

In our  earlier  opinion,  we  recognized  that  i

;

supra,  a  majority  of the United  eesant oi bbe held
that  a  police  officer’s  order  to  a  driver  to  get  out  of  his
car after  the  car  was  lawfully  stopped  for a traffic  viola-
tion  did  not  violate  the  Fourth  Amendment,  even  though
the  officer  had  no  reason  to suspect  foul  play at  the  ihe
and that  other  panels  of  this  Court  have  cited  Mimms
with  approbation,  People  v.  Laube,  154  Mich  App  400
408-410;  397  NW2d  325  (1986),  lv den  428  Mich  856  (1987);
People  v.  Blackburne,  150  Mich  App  156,  164-165;  387
NW2d  850  (1986),  lv den  425  Mich  869  (1986).  Neverthe-
less,  we  decided  in  our  earlier  opinion  that,  under  the
Michigan  Constitution’s  search-and-seizure  provision,  a
driver  such  as  defendant  in  the  present  case  should  be
afforded  more  protection  from  the  police  than  is afforded
under  the  Fourth  Amendment’s  prohibition  against
unreasonable  searches  and  seizures.  In  doing  so,  we  fol-
lowed  the  dissent  of Justice  Stevens  in  Mimms,  ond thus
declared  that  a  police  officer,  after  stopping  an  auto-
mobile  for a traffic  violation,  could  not  order  the driver  to

45

46

eT  ae  eee a

5

get  out  of  the  car,  and  thus  protract  the  driver’s  deten-
tion,  unless  the  test  announced  in Terry v.  Ohic,  391  US 1;
88 S Ct 1868;  20 L Ed 2d 889  (1968),  had been fulfilled, Le.,
unless  the  officer  had  a  reasonable,  articulable  suspicion
that  the  driver  committed,  or  was  about  to  commit,  he
crime.  We  opined  the  maintaining  the  Terry  standard  in
situations  involving  traffic  stops  by police  officers  would
help to  ensure  that  the  right to be free  from  unreasonable
seizures  would  not  be  wholly  eroded  in  traffic  offense
cases  and,  as  a corollary,  that  citizens  would  not  lawfully
be  vulnerable  to unnecessary,  intrusive,  and  intimidating
police  commands.

On  reconsideration,  we  find  that  whether  Justice
Stevens,  in his dissent  in Mimms,  had  the better  argument
on  this  issue  is not  controlling  because  in this  case we  are
obliged  to  follow  the  majority  decision  in  Mimms.  The
search-and-seizure  provision  in  the  Michigan  Constitu-
tion  provides:

“The  person,  houses,  papers  and  posses-
sions  of  every  person  shall  be  secure  from
unreasonable  searches  and  seizures.  No  warrant
to  search  any  place  or  to  seize any  person  or
things  shall  issue  without  describing  them,  nor
without  probable  cause,  supported  by oath  or
affirmation.  The  provisions  of  this  section  shall not
be  construed  to  bar  from  evidence  in  any  criminal
proceeding  any  narcotic  drug, firearm,  bomb, explo-
sive  or  any  other  dangerous  weapon,  seized  by  a
peace  officer  outside  the  curtilage  of any  dwelling
house  in  this  state.”  (Emphasis  added.)  Const
1963,  art  1, §11.

The  language  of this  section  itself  specifies  that  the  pro-
tections  granted  under  the  section  shall  not  be construed
to bar  from  evidence  any  narcotic  drug  seized  by a  police

officer  outside  the  cartilage  of a  house.  In this  case,  since
the  evidence  was  seized  from  defendant  by the  police  on
the cpen  highway,  and  therefore  outside  the curtilage  of a
house,  the  protections  granted  under  the  state’s  search-
and-seizure  provision  are  not  available  to  defendant.
Instead,  defendant  is  entitled  only  to  those  protections
available  to  him  under  the  federal  constitution.  See  People
v.  Catania,  427  Mich  447,  466,  n  12; 398  NW2d  343  (1986);
People  v.  Ragland,  149  Mich  App  277,  281;  3.:  NW2d  772
(1986);  People  v.  Alfafara,  140  Mich  App  551,  560-561;  364
NW2d  743  (1985).  And,  as  already  stated,  under  federal
law,  the  police  were  entitled  to order  defendant  out  of his
car  after  it  was  lawfully  stopped  for  a  traffic  violation.
Mimms,  supra.

After  defendant’s  automobile  was  lawfully  stopped
for  a  traffic  violation  and  after  defendant  was  lawfully
ordered  out  of his car,  it is clear  that  a  pat-down  search  of
his  person  was  warranted  under  the  Terry standard,  i.e.,  it
was  reasonable  to  conclude  that  criminal  activity  might
have  been  afoot  and  that  defendant  might  have  been
armed  and  dangerous.  See People  v.  Payton,  166  Mich  App
428,  430-431;  421  NW2d  191  (1988);  People  v.  Peyton,  167
Mich  App 230,  236-237;  421  NW2d  643  (1988).  Defendant,
in  a  high  crime  area  during  early  morning  hours,  was
admittedly  carrying a pistol  in an  ankle  holster,  appeared
to  be  extremely  nervous,  and  had  a  bulge  in  one  of  his
coat  pockets  which  led  the arresting  officer  to believe  that
defendant  could  have  been  carrying  a  second  concealed
weapon.  Fearing  for his safety  and  for  that  of his  partner,
and  believing  that,  “[i]f  [defendant]  had,  in  fact,  guns  in
his  pockets  where  the  bulges  were,  they could  have  been
used  on  me,  and  I  wouldn’t  have  had  a  chance  to

3
|

47

48

or

a  “2

=  2

-  Fee  Ss oe

*.

iS

c

7

respond,”  the  officer  conducted  a  pat-down  search  on
defendant.

In  the  present  case,  defendant,  prior  to  trial,  moved
to  suppress  the  narcotics  evidence  against  him  on  the
ground  that  the  detention  ard  pat-down  search  to  which
he  was  subjected  violated  his  Fourth  Amendment  rights.
It is well  estabiished  that  evidence  derived  from  an  illegal
seizure  mvist  be suppressed as the  “fruit  of the  poisonous
tree.”  Wong Sun  v.  United  States,  371  US 471;  83 S Ct 407;  9
L Ed  2d  441  (1963);  People  v.  Shabaz,  424  Mich  42,  65;  378
NW2d  451  (1985),  cert  dis 478  US  1017;  106  S Ct 3326;  92 L
Ed  2d  733  (1986).  The  trial  court  denied  defendant's  pre-
trial  suppression  motion.  This  Court  will  not  disturb  a
trial  court’s  ruling  on  a  suppression  motion  unless  the
ruling  is  clearly  erroneous.  Payton,  supra,  p 430;  Peyton,
supra,  p 235; People v Burrell,  417  Mich  439,  448; 339  NW2d
403  (1983).  We  will  find  siich  a ruling  clearly  erroneous  :f
firmly  convinced  that  a  mistake  has  been  made.  People  v
Daniels,  160  Mich  App 614,  617;  408  NW2d  398  (1987).  We
discern  no  reason  to disturb  the  trial  court's  ruling  in  this
case.

Regarding  the  remaining  issues  raised  on  appeal  by
defendant,  we  agree  with  the  reasoning  and  conclusions
set  forth  in  the  dissenting  opinion  filed  with  our  January
9,  1989,  decision.  We  will  review  very  briefly  each  of
those  issues.

First,  defendant  argues  that  his  right  not  to  be  com-
pelled  to  testify  against  himself  was  violated  when  Offi-
cer  Rix  asked  him  about  the  hard  object  felt  in
defendant’s  chest  pocket.  This  conversation  took  place
prior  to  defendant's  arrest.  Officer  Rix  believed  that  the

hard  object  was  another  gun  and  asked  defendant  what  it
was.  Defendant  replied  that it was  marijuana.  The litnited
constraint  placed  upon  defendant's  freedom  by the  offi-
cer  conducting  the  pat-down  search  does  not  rise  to  the
level  of a custodial  interrogation  and  precludes  a  finding
that  defendant’s  admission  was  compelled  by  police
action.  People  v.  Hill,  429  Mich  382,  397;  415  NW2d  193
(1987).

Second,  the  warrantless  search  of  defendant’s  car
was  not  an  unjustifiable  intrusion  upon  his  Fourth
Amendment  rights  such  that  the  fruits  of  this  search
should  have  been  suppressed.  Defendant’s  car  was  prop-
erly  impounded  under  the  forfeiture  provisions  of  the
Controlled  Substances  Act,  MCL  333.7522;  MSA  14.15
(7522).  However,  because  the  officers’  testimony  in  the
present  case  established  that  the  Oak  Park  Poiice  Depart-
ment’s  standardized  procedures  did  not  mandate  search-
ing and  conducting  an  inventory  of the  contents  of every
container  within  an  impounded  vehicle  such  as  defen-
dant’s,  the inventory  search  done  in this case  does  not  fall
within  the  parameters  of  Colorado  v  Bertine,  479  US  __;
107  S Ct  738;  93  L Ed  2d  739  (1987);  People  v.  Krezen,  427
Mich  681;  397  NW2d  803  (1986).

Nevertheless,  we  find  that  the search  in this  case  falls
within  the  automobile  exception  to  the  warrant  require-
ment  enunciated  in United  States  v Ross,  456  US 789;  102S
Ct 2157;  72 L Ed 2d 572  (1982);  and  adopted  by this  Court
in  People  v  Cruz.,  161  Mich  App  238,  241-242;  409  NW2d
797  (1987),  lv  den  430  Mich  854  (1988).  Ten  clear  plastic
bags  filled  with  white  powder,  three  small  vials  contain-
ing white  powder,  assorted  pills  and  capsules,  parapher-
nalia  of  the  type  used  to  facilitate  the  ingestion  of

49

50

cocaine,  aud  a  telephone  paging  device  were  retrieved
from  defendant’s  person  after  his arrest.  Moreover,  a gun
was  strapped  to  defendant's  ankle  when  he was  stopped
by  the  officers.  These  facts  establish  probable  cause  to
believe  that  defendant’s  car  contained  more  controlled
substances.  Probable  cause  exists  when  the  facts  and  cir-
cumstances  warrant  a  person  of reasonable  prudence  to
believe  that  the  evidence  of a crime  or  contraband  sought
is in a stated  place.  People v.  Goins,  164 Mich  App 559, 560;
417  NW2d  499  (1987).  Altnough  the  trial  court’s  denial  of
the  suppression  motion  was  not  based  on  the  automobile
exception  to  the  warrant  requirement,  reversal  is  not
mandated  where  the  trial  court  reaches  the  right  result
for  the  wrong  reason.  People  v  Beckley,  161  Mich  App  120,
131;  409  NW2d  759  (1987),  lv  gtd on  other  grds 430  Mich
857  (1988).

Third,  under  either  the  Michigan  or  federal  stan-
dard,2  defendant’s  claims  that  he  was  deprived  of  the
effective  assistance  of counse!  and  the  right  to  plead  not
guilty  because  (1) his  trial  counsel  offered  no  evidence  in
response  to  the  prosecutor’s  case-in-chief,  but  solely  con-
fined  the  defense  to  attacking  the  admissibility  of  evi-
dence  found  on  defendant's  person  and  in his  car,  and  (2)
counsel  did  not  argue  that  defendant  was  not  guilty  of
knowingly  possessing  the  cocaine  found  in  his  trunk,  are
both  without  merit.  The  evidence  against  defendant  was
overwhelming,  the counsel's  strategy  in concentrating  the
defense  on  the  admissibility  of  the  disputed  evidence
focused  upon  defendant's  best  and  only change  of acquit-
tal.  This  chvice  of strategy  was  not  the  functional  equiva-
lent  of  a  guilty  plea,  and  defendant  was  afforded  his
constitutional  right to  plead  not  guilty.  People  v.  Schulz,  85

Mich  App 527;  271  NW2d  305  (197
Mich  App  445;  326  NW2d  537  (1982).

;

8); People  v.  Fisher,  119

Defendant's  last claim  is that  the mandatory  sentence
of  life  imprisonment  is entirely  out  of  proportion  to  the
seriousness  of  his  crime  and  constitutes  a  cruel  and
unusual  punishment  proscribed  by US  Const,  Am  VIII
and  Const  1963,  art  1,  §16.  We  disagree.  See  People  .
Harding,  163  Mich  App  298,  329;  412  NW2d  777  (1987)
vacated  on  other  grounds  430  Mich  859  (1988).
|

Affirmed.

OPINIONS  BELOW

The  order  of  the  Michigan  Supreme  Court  denying
leave  to appeal  is published  at 434  Mich.  863  (January  29,
1990).  The  opinion  of the  Michigan  Court  of  Appeais  on
reconsideration  sua  sponte  is published  at  176  Mich.  App.
524;  440  N.W.2d  75  (1989).  The  original  opinion  of  the
Michigan  Court  of Appeals  reversing  petitioner’s  convic-
tion,  dated  January  9, 1989,  was  unpublished.  The  judg-
ment  of  sentence  in  this  case  was  dated  April  30,  1987.
Copies  of  these  opinions  are  reproduced  in  the  Joint
Appendix.

JURISDICTION

The  judgment  of the  Michigan  Court  of Appeals  was
entered  on  April  18,  1989,  affirming  petitioner’s  convic-
tion  and  sentence  dated  April  30,  1987.  On  January  20,
1990,  the  Michigan  Supreme  Court  denied  petitioner’s
application  for  leave  to  appeal.  On  April  2,  1990,  peti-
tioner  filed  his  Petition  for  Writ  of  Certiorari  with  this
Court.  The  petition  was  granted  on  May  29,  1990.  Peti-
tioner  then  filed  a  request  for  court-appointed  appellate
counsel.  This  Court  appointed  counsel.  The  jurisdiction  of
this  Court  is invoked  under  the  Eighth  Amendment  of the
Constitution  of  the  United  States.

+

CONSTITUTIONAL  PROVISIONS  INVOLVED

This  case  involves  a  violation  of  the  Eighth  Amend-

ment  to  the  United  States  Constitution.

°

1

STATEMENT  OF  THE  CASE

Petitioner  Ronald  Allen  Harmelin  was  arrested  in
Oak  Park,  Michigan,  with  672  grams  of  cocaine  in  the
trunk  of  his  car.  He  was  convicted  after  a  bench  trial  of
possession  6f  650  or  more  grams  of  cocaine,  MCL
333.7403(1)(i);  MSA  14.15(7403)(2)(a)(i),  (Pet.  App.  —  stat-
ute)  and  felony  firearm,  MCL  750.227b;  MSA  28.424(2).
On  April  30,  1987,  he  was  sentenced  by  the  Oakland
County  Circuit  Court  to  a  mandatory  life  term  of
imprisonment  with  no  parole,  and  a  two-year  term  for  the
felony-firearm  conviction.

In  the  early  morning  hours  of  May  12,  1986,  two
police  officers  stopped  the  petitioner,  Ronald  Harmelin,
for  failure  to  make  a  complete  stop  at  a  red  light.  The
petitioner  complied  in  a  cooperative  manner  upon  being
requested  to  produce  a  driver’s  license  and  vehicle  regis-
tration.  Mr.  Harmelin  voluntarily  informed  one  of  the
officers  that  he  was  carrying a pistol  in  an  ankle  hoister
(Evidentiary  Hearing  of  November  12,  1986,  hereafter
referred  to  as  EH  1,  p.  11).  Petitioner  then  handed  the
officer  a  valid  permit  to  carry  a  concealed  weapon.  Ulti-
mately,  Mr.  Harmelin’s  person  was  searched  and  the  offi-
cers  discovered  in  his  pockets  some  marijuana  cigarettes,
assorted  pills,  ten  small  packets  of  cocaine,  (one  such
packet  was  analyzed  and  found  to contain  0.14  grams  of a
mixture  containing  cocaine)(J.A.  —  Trial  Exhibit  #16,  the
chemist’s  lab  sheet,  p. 2),  three  little  vials  of cocaine,  and
a  telephone  pager  (EH  1,  pp.  1-14).  Later,  the  police
impounded  the  1977  Ford  and  searched  the  trunk.  The
search  revealed  a  closed  travel  bag.  Inside  the  travel  bag
was  a  Closed  shaving  kit  bag  containing  $2900  and  two
bags  containing  672.5  grams  of cocaine  (EH  2, p-  41).  Mr.

Harmelin’s  fingerprints  were  found  on  a  book  inside  the
travel  bag (Trial  Transcript  of February  26,  1987,  hereafter
referred  to  as  TT,  p.  98).  His  fingerprints  were  not  found
inside  the  shaving  kit  which  contained  the  money  and  the
cocaine  (TT,  p.  106).

Defendant  neither  testified  in  his  own  behalf  nor
presented  any  witnesses.  He  was  convicted  as  charged
and  sentenced  to  mandatory  life  imprisonment  without
possibility  of  parole.

The  presentence  report  reveals  that  Mr.  Harmelin  had
no  prior  contacts  with  the  criminal  justice  system  (J.A.
Presentence  report,  p.  1).  He  graduated  from  Sterling
High  School  in  Sommerdale,  New  Jersey  in  1983.  Mr.
Harmelin  entered  the  US  Air  Force  and  was  stationed  at
Bolling  AFB  near  Washington,  D.C.,  where  he  was  in  the
Honor  Guard  for  four  years.  He  was  honorably  dis-
charged  in  1967.

The  presentence  report  revealed  that  petitioner
admitted  he was  a drug  addict.  He  denied  an  addiction  to
cocaine  which  was  contradicted  by  the  fact  that  he  had
four  brass  cocaine  straws  and  one  cocaine  spoon  in  his
pocket,  as  well  as  a  small  amount  of cocaine.  (J.A.  presen-
tence,  p.  4)

ee

Petitioner  was  unemployed  at  the  time  of the  offense.
Petitioner  owned  the  9 year  old  Ford  automobile  and  had
$597.00  in  his  pocket.  He  had  no  other  assets.  He  resided
at  an  apartment  in  Detroit.  (J.A.  presentence  report,  p.  6)

The  presentence  report  described  the  petitioner  as  a
largescale  drug dealer,  estimating  the  value  of the cocaine

Ue ee

he  was  carrying  between  $67,000  and  $100,000.  They
offered  no  proof  of  his  role  in  the  drug  trafficking  hier-
archy,  and  nothing  that  would  dispute  the  fact  that  he
was  a  “mule  of  transport”.  The  contents  of  the  presen-
tence  report  were  not  challenged,  indeed  they  were  not
mentioned  at  the  sentencing  hearing,  because  of the  man-
datory  nature  of  the  sentencing  statute.

On  appeal,  petitioner  argued  that  his  convictions
must  be  reversed  because  the  evidence  against  him  was
obtained  as a result  of an  unconstitutional  search;  that  he
had  been  deprived  of  the  effective  assistance  of counsel;
and  that  his  sentence  must  be  set  aside  and  a  resentenc-
ing  occur  because  a  mandatory  life  sentence  without  the
possibility  of  parole  constitutes  cruel  and  unusual  pun-
ishment.  The  Michigan  Court  of  Appeals  reversed  his
conviction  in  an  opinion  dated  January  9,  1989,  stating
that  the  search  was  unconstitutional  (J.A.).

In  an  order  dated  March  9, 1989,  the  Michigan  Court

of  Appeals  vacated  that  judgment  (J.A.).

ee

The  Michigan  Court  of  Appeals  denied  a  Motion  for
Rehearing  on  April  18,  1989  (J.A.).  In  an  opinion  (on
reconsideration)  dated  April  18,  1989,  they  affirmed  his
conviction  (J.A.).

The  Michigan  Supreme  Court  denied  leave  to  appeal

The  petitioner  filed  a  petition  for  writ  of certiorari  on
April  2, 1990.  The  petition  was  granted  on  May  29,  1990.
