not  conain  cocaine  or  ecgonine.  The  substances  include
cocaine,  its  salts,  stereoisomers,  and  salts  of stereoisomers
when  the  existence  of the  sz!ts,  stereoisomers,  and  salts  of
stereoisomers  is possible  within  the  specific  chemical  des-
ignation.

App.  5

LAW  LIBRARY

HOUSE  BILL  4190  (as  passed  by  the  House)
Sponsor:  Rep.  Paul  Rosenbaum
House  Committee:  Judiciary
Senate  Committee:  Judiciary

THE  APPARENT  PROBLEM:

»

Despite  the  efforts  of  law  enforcement  and  drug  educa-
tion  personnel,  there  has  been  a  steady  .ncrease  in  nar-
cotics  traffic  and  drug  abuse  in  Michigan,  with  a
corresponding  increase  in  drug-related  crime.  Some  per-
sons  claim  that  the  state  has  failed  to  stem  drug  traffic
because  the  penalties  for  drug  dealing  are  not  severe
enough,  and  law  enforcement  tools  are  inadequate.  They
contend  that  the  potential  for  profit  in  drug  dealing  is so
great  that  Michigan’s  present  penalties  pose  little  or  no
deterrent  to  would  be  violators,  with  lenient  probation
and  parole  policies  weakening  the  threat  of imprisonment
still  further.  Police  officers  involved  in  narcotics  investi-
gation  also  complain  that  they  are  arresting  the  same
persons  over  and  over  because  convicted  drug  dealers  are
quickly  returned  to  the  illicit  drug  scene  through  proba-
tion  and  parole.  Further,  it  is  claimed  that  the  top  drug
dealers  are  so  far  removed  from  the  “street  scene”  that
only  increased  investigatory  powers  including  wiretap-
ping  can  result  in  their  apprehension  and  conviction.

THE  CONTENT  OF  THE  BILL:

The  bill  would  change  the  penalties  in  the  Controlled
Substance  Act  of  1971  for  the  illegal  possession  or  sale
and  delivery  of  Schedule  1  or  2  narcotic  drugs  (opium

¥v

App.  6

App.  7

and  its  derivatives,  including  heroin)  and  cocaine,  as
follows:

ILLEGAL  MANUFACTURE,  DELIVERY,  OR  POSSES-
SION  OVER  50  GRAMS  (13/4  ounces)  OF  ANY  MIXTURE
CONTAINING  THESE  SUBSTANCES

650  grams  or  more  (23 oz.  or  more)  -  mandatory
life  imprisonment

225  to  649.99  grams  (8 to  23  oz.)  -  mandatory  20
to  30  years  imprisonment

50  to  224.99  grams  (13/4  to  8 oz.)  —  either  manda-
tory  10  to  20  years  imprisonment,  or  probation
for  life

These  prison  terms  could  not  be suspended  and  could  not
run  concurrently  with  a  prison  sentence  for  another  fel-
ony.  Except  for  the  optional  sentence  of probation  for  life
for  the  50 -  224.99  gram  offense,  there  would  be no  parole
or  probation  possibility.  Second  or  subsequent  convic-
tions  would  carry  a  mandatory  life  imprisonment  penalty.

Present  Penalties:

Manufacture  or  delivery  of  schedule  1 or  2 nar-
cotic  drugs:  imprisonment  up  to  20  years  and/
or  a  fine  up  to  $25,000  at  the  court’s  discretion.

Manufacture  or  delivery  of  cocaine:  imprison-
ment  up  to  7  years  and/or  a  $5,000  fine.

Possession  of  schedule  1  or  2  narcotic  drugs:
imprisonment  up  to  4  years  and/or  a  $2,000
fine.

Possession  of  cocaine:  imprisonment  up  to  2
years  and/or  a  $2,000  fine.

ILLEGAL  MANUFACTURE  OR  DELIVERY  LESS  THAN
50  GRAMS

Imprisonment  for up  to  20  years  and/or  a  $25,000  fine,  at
the  court’s  discretion.  (Same  as  present  penalty.)

ILLEGAL  POSSESSION  LESS  THAN  50  GRAMS

Imprisonment  for  up  to  4  years  and/or  a  fine  up  to
$2,000.  (Same  as  present  penalty.)

ILLEGAL  USE  OF  A  CONTROLLED  SUBSTANCE

Penalties  for  the  illegal  use  of  a  controlled  substance
would  remain  the  same,  except  for  the  fine  for  cocaine
use,  which  would  be  increased  from  $1,000  to  $2,000.

SALE  TO  MINORS

Presently,  an  adult  who  gives  or  sells  a  schedule  1  or  2
narcotic  drug  to  a  person  under  18  and  at  least  5 years
younger  than  the  adult  may  be  fined  up  to  $25,000  and/
or  imprisoned  up  to  40  years  -  double  the  prison  term
otherwise  authorized  for  manufacture  or  delivery.  The
bill  would  include  the  distribution  of  cocaine  in  this
“double  maximum”  provision.

FIRST  OFFENSE

Presently,  in  the  case  of a  first  violation  for  possession  of
any  controlled  substance,  the  court  has  the  option  of
deferring  proceedings  and  placing  the  violator  on  proba-
tion.  If the  person  then  meets  the  conditions  of probation,
the  case  is  dismissed  without  an  adjudication  of  guilt.
The  bill  would  exempt  first  violations  for  possession  of
schedule  1  or  2  narcotic  drugs  and  cocaine  in  amounts
over  225  grams  from  this  option.

App.  8

App.  9

PLEA-BARGAINING

Once  a  defendant  had  been  arraigned  on  a  warrant,
indictment,  or  information  for  one  of the  mandatory  sen-
tence  offenses  (225  or  more  grams  of  schedule  1  or  2
narcotics  or  cocaine),  or  conspiracy  to  commit  such  an
offense,  dismissal  upon  the  motion  of  the  prosecuting
attorney  could  only  be  “with  prejudice”  that  is,  a  dis-
missal  which  precluded  any  further  action  on  the  same
charge.  Further,  the  prosecuting  attorney  could  not
reduce  the  charge  if at  the  end  of the  preliminary  exam-
ination  it appeared  to  the  examining  magistrate  that  1 or
more  of  the  offenses  had  been  committed  and  there  was
probable  cause  to  charge  the  defendant.  The  bill  would
also  prohibit  the  court  from  accepting  a  plea  of  guilty,
guilty  but  mentally  ill,  or  nolo  contendere  to  any  lesser
offense  without  such  a  plea  to  1  of  the  mandatory  sen-
tence  offenses  (50  or  more  grams).

AUTHORIZED  DISTRIBUTION  OF  CONTROLLED  SUB-
STANCES

Under  the  present  law,  certain  persons  such  as  pharma-
cists  may  be  registered  to  legitimately  dispense,  pre-
scribe,  manufacture,  or  study  controlled  substances.  This
registration  may  be  denied,  suspended,  or  revoked  if the
person  has  filed  a  fraudulent  application,  violates  any
state  or  federal  drug  law,  loses  his/her  federal  registra-
tion,  or  promotes  any  controlled  substance  to  the  general
public.  The  bill  would  specifically  state  that  the  registra-
tion  authorizes  only  the  manufacturing,  prescribing,  or
dispensing  which  is  for  legitimate  medical  or  therapeutic
purposes  and  in  the course  of the  registrant’s  professional
practice.  Unauthorized  manufacturing,  prescribing,  or
dispensing  could  result  in  loss  of  registration,  as  could

violation  of any  provision  of, or  rule  promulgated  under,
the  Contolled  [sic]  Substance  Act  of  1971.  In  addition,  a
registered  practitioner  who  illegally  dispensed,  pre-
scribed,  or  administered  a  controlled  substance  would  be
subject  to  prosecution  for  illegal  manufacture,  delivery,  or
possession  of  a  controlled  substance.

The  bill  is tie-barred  to  House  Bills  4191,  4192,  4193,  4194
and  4195.

The  effective  date  of  the  bill  is specified  as  September  1,
1978.

FISCAL  IMPLICATIONS:

The  House  Fiscal  Agency  has  recommended  that  the  bill
be  referred  to  the  Appropriations  Committee  on  the  basis
of the  fiscal  effect  for  offenders  caught  with  possession  of
more  than  50  grams  of  narcotics  or  cocaine.  They  state,
“according  to  the  Department  of  Corrections  annual
report,  there  were  15  offenders  in  prison  for  sale  of  nar-
cotics  with  a maximum  of life  imprisonment  at  the  end  of
1974.  If  this  bill  were  in  effect,  their  sentences  would  be
mandatory  life.  There  were  also  129  offenders  in  prison
for  unlawful  sale  and  distribution  of  narcotics  which
carries  a  present  maximum  of 20 years.  It is impossible  to
tell  which  category  these  offenders  would  fall  into,  as
there  is  no  way  to  determine  the  quantity  of  narcotics
with  which  they  were  caught;  however,  with  an  estimated
annual  cost  of  $5,000  per  prisoner  year,  the  long-range
costs  of  this  bill  would  be  enormous  and  the  prison
population  would  greatly  increase.”

App.  10

App.  11

The  Department  of Attorney  General  notes  that  w  .enever
legislation  of  this  nature  is  passed,  the  dc.  artment
charged  with  enforcement  looks  to  the  Attorney  General
to  provide  legal  advice  and  counsel,  increasing  the  over-
all  demand  for  legal  services  and  necessitating  increased
funding  for  the  Department  of Attorney  General.  In addi-
tion,  legislation  such  as  this  often  leads  to  more  litigation
which  also  requires  enlargement  of  the  Attorney  Gen-
eral’s  staff.

The  Department  of Public  Health  believes  that,  if the  bill
proved  effective,  the  number  of  addicted  inmates  in
prison  would  increase,  and  that  unless  treatment  pro-
grams  including  methadone  maintenance  in  prison  were
significantly  expanded,  control  problems  and  substance
abuse  among  inmates  would  increase  me“surably.  In  this
event,  the  department  would  expect  the  Department  of
Corrections  to  request  a  dramatic  expansion  of  its  subs-
tance  abuse  contractual  relationship  with  the  Office  of
Substance  Abuse  Services.  The  department  also  points
out  that  the  bill  could  increase  court  costs  significantly,
since  the  severe  proposed  penalties  would  lead  many
defendants  to  request  jury  trials.  In  addition,  at  the  local
level,  the  staffs  of  judges,  prosecutors,  and  enforcement
agencies  would  probably  be  expanded.

BACKGROUND  INFORMATION:

In  1973,  the  state  of New  York  enacted  the  nation’s  tough-
est  drug  laws,  with  mandatory  minimum  sentences  of  15
years  to  life  imprisonment  for  possession  of  2 ounces  or
more,  or  sale  of  1 ounce  or  more  of a  narcotic  drug.  The
question  of  the  success  or  failure  of  these  laws  is  still

highly  controversial.  Those  in  favor  of the  law  claim  that
it has  helped  stem  an  epidemic  rise  in  narcotic  addiction.
Critics  of  the  law  insist  that  it  is  a  false  solution  which
has  accomplished  nothing  while  doing  great  harm  to
enlightened  criminal  justice.  Statistics  from  New  York
now  indicate  that  despite  substantially  increased  enforce-
ment  efforts  and  expenditures,  the  stiff  New  York  drug
laws  have  not  reduced  the  state’s  drug  problem.  Oppo-
nents  of House  Bills  4190  -  4195  maintain  that  this  appar-
ent  failure  is  due  to  flows  inherent  in  any  legislation
which  attempts  to  use  a  tough,  mandatory  sentencing
approach  to  solve  the  heroin  problem.  Supporters  of  the
bills,  however,  deny  that  the  New  York  laws  have  clear
applicability,  stating  that  House  Bills  4190  -  4195  are
significantly  different  in  several  areas.  In  particular,  they
point  out  that  the  bills  are  specifically  aimed  at  a  higher
echelon  of drug dealing,  and  that  the  prohibition  on  plea
bargaining  is drafted  to avoid  the problems  that  the rigid-
ity of the  New  York  laws  has  caused.  Many  opponents  of
the  New  York  law  favor  the  British  method  of  dealing
with  the  narcotic  problem,  which  allows  physicians  to
legally  prescribe  morphine  or  heroin  for  addicts.  As  with
the  New  York  laws,  the  success  of  the  British  method  is
open  to  great  controversy.  Its  proponents  claim  that  it has
eliminated  black-market  drug  dealing  and  related  crime,
while  offering  a  greater  opportunity  for  addicts  to  lead
useful  lives.  Its critics  claim  that  legalized  addiction  does
not  truly  rehabilitate  addicts,  and  that  governmental
sanction  of  drug  abuse  is  unhealthy,  unethical,  and
unwise.

App.  12

App.  13

ARGUMENTS  ADVANCED  FOR  AND  AGAINST  THE
BILL:

For:

All  of society  is a  victim  of the  illicit  drug  scene.  Not  only
does  widespread  narcotic  addiction  cause  a  serious  loss
of  human  resources,  but  black-market  drug  dealing  cre-
ates  a  spiral  of drug-related  crime.  The  magnitude  of  the
narcotics  problem  in  Michigan  necessitates  tougher  law
enforcement  policies.  The penalties  imposed  by  the  bill
for  major  drug  dealing  are  severe,  but  they  are  not  inap-
propriate  in  terms  of the  harm  drug  dealers  do  to  society.

For:

The  severe  penalties  imposed  by  the  bill  would  have  an
important  deterrent  effect  on  illicit  drug dealing.  With  the
present  drug  penalties  and  parole  possibilities,  dealers
feel  that  if  caught  they  will  spend  little  or  no  time  in
prison.  The  risk  to  them  seems  relatively  small,  and  the
potential  for  profit  great.  Consequently,  only  more  severe
penalties  and  the  certainty  of  punishment  will  serve  to
deter  would-be  violators.  The  bill  also  contains  an  impor-
tant  exception  to  the  mandatory  prison  sentence  provi-
sions  by  allowing  a  life  probation  option  in  the  50  to
224.99  gram  offense  category.  This  could  be  used  as  an
incentive  for  lower-echelon  drug  dealers  to  give  valuable
information  on  major  drug  dealers.

For:

The  bill  would  ensure  that  persons  convicted  of  drug
dealing  spend  substantial  periods  of  time  in  prison.  By
keeping  drug  dealers  off  the  streets  for  longer  periods  of

time,  it  will  help  reduce  illicit  drug  activity.  Further,  it
will  help  improve  police  morale.

Against:

Instead  of  ensuring  that  the  state’s  major  drug  dealers
spend  substantial  periods  of  time  in  jail,  the  bill  could
backfire.  Convictions  on  drug charges  could  become  more
difficult  to  obtain,  because  juries  might  be  more  apt  to
acquit  in  reaction  to  the  severe  penalties.  In  addition,  it
has  been  suggested  that  one  possible  negative  conse-
quence  of the  bill  would  be  that  major  drug  dealers  who
are  the  intended  target  of  the  legislation  might  use
addicts  to  transport  large  quantities  of drugs  rather  than
take  the  risk  of  facing  long  mandatory  prison  sentences
themselves.  If this  happened,  the  drug  traffic  would  con-
tinue  and  only  addicts,  who  are  already  “victims”  of  the
drug  scene,  would  be  faced  with  long  prison  sentences.

Against:

The  bill  would  provide  stiff,  mandatory  penalties  for  the
illegal  sale  of  cocaine,  identical  to  the  penalties  for  nar-
cotics.  This  is unwarranted.  Cocaine  is not  a  narcotic  and
the  physiological  effects  of  cocaine  are  substantially  dif-
ferent  from  those  of  heroin  and  other  opiates.  Further-
more,  quantities  of cocaine  and  heroin  are  not  equivalent
in  terms  of  potency  or  market  value.  Consequently,  the
effect  of  the  bill  is  to  provide  stiffer  penalties  for  cocaine
than  for  heroin.

|

App.  14

App.  15

For:

9

Against:

While  cocaine  does  not  have  the  addictive  effect  of  her-
oin,  the  bill’s  grouping  of cocaine  with  heroin  is justified.
Because  the  profit  margin  for  cocaine  is nearly  as  high  as
for  heroin,  with  fewer  risks,  cocaine  dealing  is  a  fast-
growing  phenomenon,  often  involving  people  who  also
deal  heroin.  It is further  claimed  that  because  of the  high
profits,  cocaine  dealing  now  generates  related  criminal
activity.

Against:

Severe,  mandatory  prison  sentences  are  a  simplistic
approach  to  the  complex  problem  of  illicit  drug  activity.
There  is  no  real  evidence  that  they  will  serve  as  a  deter-
rent  to  crime.  A  deterrent  effect  could  be  reasonably
hoped  fo®aonly  if  the  state  could  ensure  certainty  of
apprehension  as  well  as  certainty  of  punishment.

Given  size  of  the  state’s  addict  population  and  the  poten-
tial  for  profit,  new  dealers  would  quickly  take  the  places
of  those  sentenced  to  mandatory  jail  terms.  Meanwhile,
the  problems  which  provoke  and  foster  drug  addiction
remain  unaddressed.  In  fact,  many  critics  of the  strict  law
enforcement  approach  to  drugs  believe  that  it is not  only
a  futile  method,  but  one  that  ultimately  worsens  the
problem.  When  one  major  heroin  channel  is  broken  and
the  supply  decreases,  the  price  of  heroin  increases  pro-
portionately,  and  profitability  soars.  This  can  create  a
type  of  “crime  inflation”,  since  the  price  of  heroin
appears  to  have  a  direct  effect  on  street  crime.

Mandatory  minimum  sentences  undermine  the  modern
concept  of criminal  justice,  which  endeavors  to  treat  indi-
vidual  defendants  separately,  fairly,  and  with  due  process
of  law.  The  bill  stresses  punishment  and  thwarts  reha-
bilitation.  If  prisons  are  to  be  “correctional”  instead  of
punitive,  prisoners  must  have  incentives  to  participate  in
prison  rehabilitation  programs.  By  requiring  a  fixed
prison  term,  with  no  possibility  of  parole,  the  bill
destroys  that  incentive.

Further,  the  bill  could  impose  an  intolerable  burden  on
the  state’s  courts  and  penal  institutions,  because  criminal
defendants  faced  with  severe  mandatory  sentences  would
insist  upon  jury  trials,  and  long  prison  terms  with  no
possibility  of  parole  would  quickly  overload  the  already
overcrowded  prisons.

Against:

The  power  to  sentence  should  belong  to  judges;  the  bill
would,  in  effect,  shift  that  power  from  judges  to  prosecu-
tors.  Sentencing  judges  must  have  unrestricted  power  to
choose  an  appropriate  sentence  according  to  the  condi-
tion  of the  defendant,  the  record  of prior criminal  activity,
and  the  requirement  of  public  protection.  Discretion  in
sentencing  is  particularly  important  on  drug  offenses,
becattse  so  many  violators  are  victims  of their  own  addic-
tion  and  poverty.

It  has  also  been  suggested  that  so  severely  restricting
prosecutorial  discretion  in  post-arraignment  proceedings
may  be  violative  of  the  separation  of  powers  doctrine.

App.  16

QUESTION  PRESENTED

1.  WHETHER  A  MANDATORY  SENTENCE  OF  LIFE
IMPRISONMENT  WITHOUT  PAROLE  FOR  POSSESSION
OF  OVER  650  GRAMS  OF  PURE  COCAINE  VIOLATES
THE  CRUEL  AND  UNUSUAL  PUNISHMENT  CLAUSE
OF  THE  EIGHTH  AMENDMENT?

STATEMENT  OF  THE  CASE

For  the  most  part,  Respondent  accepts  Petitioner’s
Statement  of the  Case.  It should  be added,  however,  that
the  672.5  grams  of cocaine  involved  were  pure  with  no
dilutants.  (J.A.,  p.  2, Chemist’s  Laboratory  Report)

As  to Petitioner’s  professional  status,  the  presentence
report  indicated  that  the  Oakland  County  Narcotics
Enforcement  Team  (N.E.T.)  and  Oak  Park  Police
described  Petitioner  “as a large-scale  drug dealer,  deliver-
ing  down  to  mid-level  dealers.”  The  street  price  of  the
confiscated  cocaine  was  estimated  as  between  $67,000
and  $100,000.  (J.A.,  p.  9)  Petitioner  states  there  was  no
offer  of  proof  as  to  his  alleged  drug-trafficking.  (Peti-
tioner’s  brief,  p.  4)  However,  as  Petitioner  admits,  the
contents  of the  presentence  report  was  not  challenged  at
the  time  of  sentencing.  Petitioner’s,  trial  counsel  indi-
cated:  “I  have  had  an  opportunity  td. go  through  the
presentence  report.  As  to  the  factual  allegations  only,  I
have  no  difficulty  with  them.  I believe  them  to  be  accu-
rate.”  (J.A.,  pp.  14-15)

= _—
~ RR are

In  the  presentence  report,  Petitioner  denied  “drug
trafficking.”  He  claimed  he  earned  $500  to  $800  a  week

playing  pool.  Petitioner  retained  his attorney  for $15,000,
paid  for  by  an  associate  of  Petitioner’s,  according  to
police  investigation.  (J.A.,  pp.  12-13)

+

SUMMARY  OF  THE  ARGUMENT

Petitioner  makes  the  emotional  argument  that  Michi-
gan’s  statutorily-mandated  sentence  of  life  in  prison
without  parole  constitutes  cruel  and  unusual  punishment
in  violation  of  the  US  Const,  Amend  VIII.  Respondent
submits  that  given  that  Petitioner  was  convicted  of  pos-
sessing  672.5  grams  of  pure  cocaine  worth  between
$67,000  to  $100,000,  Petitioner  cannot  complain  of  the
harshness  of the  sentence  he  received.  Drug-trafficking  is
one  of the  greatest  and  gravest  dangers  facing  the  United
States.  Michigan’s  mandatory  penalty  for those  dealing  in
large  quantities  of  drugs  is  not  grossly  disproportionate
as  a  weapon  in  the  national  war  on  drugs.  That  Michi-
gan’s  penalty  may  be  one  of the  harshest  in  the  nation  is
beside  the  point  under  the  doctrine  of  federalism.

I.  A  MANDATORY  SENTENCE  OF  LIFE  IMPRISON-
MENT  WITHOUT  PAROLE  FOR  POSSESSION  OF
OVER  650  GRAMS  OF  PURE  COCAINE  DOES  NOT
VIOLATE  THE  CRUEL  AND  UNUSUAL  PUNISH-
MENT  CLAUSE  OF  THE  EIGHTH  AMENDMENT.

A.  Michigan’s  State  Interest  In  Fighting  Drugs

On  February  26,  1987,  Petitioner  was  found  guilty  by
the  trial  judge  of  possession  of  over  650  grams  of  a
substance  containing  cocaine,  in  violation  of  MCL
333.7403(2)(i)  and  of  possession  of  a  firearm  during  the

commission  of a  felony.  in  violation  of MCL  750.227b.  On
April  30,  1987,  he  was  sentenced  to  a  mandatory  term  of
imprisonment  of  two  years  on  the  felony-firearm  charge
and  to  a  consecutive  mandatory  life  term  on  the  cocaine
charge.

The  crimes  in  question  occurred  on  May  12,  1986.
Until  amendments  which  became  effective  March  30,
1988,  MCL  333.7403  provided:

Sec.  7403.  (1)  A person  shall  not  knowingly
or  intentionally  possess  a  controlled  substance
unless  the substance  was  obtained  directly  from,
or  pursuant  to, a  valid  prescription  or  order  of a
practitioner  while  acting  in  the  course  of  the
practitioner’s  professional  practice,  or  except  as
otherwise  authorized  by  this  article.

(2)  A  person  who  violates  this  section  as

to:

(a)  A  controlled  substance  classified  in
schedule  1 or  2 which  is  either  a  narcotic  drug
or  described  in  section  7214(a)(iv),  and;

(i)  Which  is  in  an  amount  of 650  grams  or
more  of any  mixture  containing  that  substance  is
guilty  of  a  felony  and  shall  be  imprisoned  for
life.

(ii)  Which  is in an  amount  of 225  grams  or
mixture
more,  but  less  than  650  grams,  of  any
containing  that  substance  is  guilty  of  a  felony
and  shall  be  imprisoned  for  not  less  than  20
years  nor  more  than  30  years.

(iii)  Which  is in  an  amount  of 50  g-ams  or
more,  but  less  than  225  grams,  of  any
ruxture
containing  that  substance  is gully  of  a  felony
for  not  less  than
and  shall  be  either  imprisoned
10  teen nor  more  than  20  years  or  placed  on
probation  for  life.

4

(iv)  Which  is  in  an  amount  of less  than  50
grams  of any  mixture  containing
that  substance
is  guilty  of  a  felony,  punishable  by  imprison-
ment  for  not  more  than  4 years,  or  by a fine  of
not  more  than  $2,000.00,  or  both.

(b)  A  controlled  substance  classified  in
schedule  1, 2, 3, or  4, except  a  controlled  subs-
tance  classified  in  schedule  1  for  which  a  pen-
alty is prescribed  in subdivision  (a),  (c), or  (d),  is
guilty  of  a  felony,  punishable  by imprisonment
for  not  more  than 2 years,  or  a  fine  of  not  more
than  $2,000.00,  or  both.

(c)  Lysergic  Acid  Diethylamide,  peyote,
mescaline,  dimethyltryptamine,  psilocyn,
psilocybin,  or  a  controlled  substance  classified
in  schedule  5, is  guilty  of  a misdemeanor,  pun-
ishable  by  imprisonment  for  not  more  than  1
year,  or  a  fine  of  not  more  than  $1,000.00,  or
both.

punishable  by imprisonment  for  not  more  than  1
year,  or  a  fine  of  not  more  than  $1,000.00,  or
both.

MCL  333.7403  codified  section  7403  of  Mich  Public  Act
368  of  1978  which  became  effective  September  30,  1978.
MCL  333.7214(a)(iv)  codified  section  7214(a)(iv)  of  Act
368  and  specifically  encompassed  cocaine.

Also  part  of  Mich  Public  Act  368  was  section  7401
which  prohibited  the  manufacture,  delivery,  or  posses-
sion  with  intent  to  manufacture  or  deliver  a  controlled
substance.  The  penalties  were  the  same  as  for  possessing
schedule  1  and  2  controlled  substances  in  amounts
greater  than  50  grams.  This  section  became  codified  as
MCL  333.7401  and  included  in  section  7401(3)  a  provision

for  no  parole  during  the  mandatory  terms.  MCL  333.7401
provided:

Sec.  7401.  (1)  Except  as  authorized  by  this
article,  a  person  siiall  not  manufacture,  deliver,
or  possess  with  intent  to  manufacture  or  deliver,
a  controlled  substance.  A  practitioner  licensed
by the  administrator  under  this  article  shall  not
dispense,  prescribe,  or  administer  a  controlled
substance  for  other  than  legitimate  and  profes-
sionally  recognized  therapeutic  or  scientific  pur-
poses  or  outside  the  scope  of  practice  of  the
practitioner,  licensee,  or  applicant.

(2)  A  person  who  violates  this  section  as

to:

(a)  A  controlled  substance  classified  in
schedule  1  or  2 which  is  either  a  narcotic  drug
or  described  in  section  7214(a)(iv)  and:

(i)  Which  is  in  an  amount  of 650  grams  or
more  of any  mixture  containing  that  substance  is
guilty of  a  felony  and  shall  be  imprisoned  for
ife.

(ii)  Which  is in  an  amount  of 225  grams  or
more,  but  less  than  650  grams,  of  any  mixture
containing  that  substance  is  guilty  of  a  felony
and  shall  be  imprisoned  for  not  less  than  20
years  nor  more  than  30  years.

(iii)  Which  is in  an  amount  of 50  grams  or
more,  but  less  than  225  grams,  of  any  mixture
containing  that  substance  is  guilty  of  a  felony
and  shall  be  either  imprisoned  for  not  less  than
10  years  nor  more  than  20  years  or  placed  on
probation  for  life.

(iv)  Which  is  in  an  amount  less  than  50
grams  of any  mixture  containing  that  substance
is  guilty  of a  felony  and  may  be  imprisoned  for
not  more  than  20  years,  or  fined  not  more  than
$25,000.00,  or  both.

(b)  Any  other  controlled  substance  classi-
fied  in  schedule  1, 2, or  3, except  marihuana,  is
uilty  of  a  felony,  punishable  by imprisonment
or  not  more  than  7 years,  or  a  fine  of not  more
than  $5,000.00,  or  both.

(c)  A substance  classified  in  schedule  4 or
marihuana,  is  guilty  of a  felony,  punishable  by
imprisonment  for  not  more  than  4  years,  or  a
fine  of  not  more  than  $2,000.00,  or  both.

(d)  A  substance  classified  in  schedule  5, is
uilty  of  a  felony,  punishable  by  imprisonment
or  not  more  than  2 years,  or  a  fine  of  not  more
than  $2,000.00,  or  both.

(3)  A  term  of  imprisonment  imposed  pur-
suant  to  subsection  (2)(a)(i),  (ii),  or  (iii)  or  sec-
tion  7403(2)(a)(i),  (ii),  or  (iii)  shall  be imposed  to
run  consecutively  with  any  term  of  imprison-

felony.  An  individual  subject  to  a  mandatory
term  of imprisonment  under  subsection  (2)(a)(i),
(ii),  or  (iii)  or  section  ~  13(2)(a)(i),  (ii),  or  (iii)
shall  not  be eligible  for  prcbation,  suspension  of
that  sentence,  or  parole  during  that  mandatory
term,  except  and  only  to  the  extent  that  those
provisions  permit  probation  for  life.

Mich  Public  Act  368  of 1978  began as Mich  House  Bill
4190  of  1977.  The  House  Legislative  Analysis  of  the  bill
‘as enrolled),  dated  May  17,  1978,?  explained  the  problem
the  bill  was  intended  to  address:

2  The  legislative  analysis  in  Brief  of  Petitioner,  Appendix

in  the  House.  The  final  bill  dropped  wiretapping  as  part  of  its
package.

THE  APPARENT  PROBLEM:

Despite  the  efforts  of law  enforcement  and  drug
education  personnel,  there  has  been  a  steady
increase  in  narcotics  traffic  and  drug  abuse  in
Michigan,  with  a  corresponding  increase  in
drug-related  crime.  Some  persons  claim  that  the
state  has  failed  to  stem  drug  traffic  because  the
penalties  for  drug  dealing  are  not  severe
enough,  and  law  enforcement  tools  are  inade-
quate.  They  contend  that  the  potential  for  profit
in  drug  dealing  is  so  great  that  Michigan’s  pre-
sent  penalties  pose  little  or  no  deterrent  to
would  be  vielators,  with  lenient  probation  and
parole  policies  weakening  the  threat  of
imprisonment  still  further.  Police  officers
involved  in  narcotics  investigation  also  com-
plain  that  they  are  arresting  the  same  persons
over  and  over  because  convicted  drug  dealers
are  quickly  returned  to  the  illicit  drug  scene
through  probation  and  parole.

Since  1978,  the  Michigan  Legislature  has  twice
amended  some  of  the  penalties  of  MCL  333.7401  and
MCL  333.7403.  Mich  Public  Act  275  of  1987  and  Mich
Public  Act  47  of  1988  took  effect  March  30,  1988  and
changed  some  of  the  penalties.  Notably,  the  mandatory
minimum  sentences  tor  an  amount  of 225  grams  or  more,
but  less  than  650  grams,  was  lowered  to  ten  years  from
twenty.  The  mandatory  minimum  for  an  amount  of  50
grams  or  more,  but  less  than  225,  was  lowered  to  five
years  from  ten.  More  significantly,  the  statutes  were
amended  to  allow  the  sentencing  court  to  depart  from  the
minimum  terms  specified  for  all  the  amounts  except  650
grams  or  more  “if  the  court  finds  on  the  record  that  there
are  substantial  and  compelling  reasons  to  do  so.”  MCL
333.7401(4);  MCL  333.7403(3).

8

9

;

;

Finally,  effective  September  28,  1989, Mich Public  Act
143  of  1989  changed  the-«mandatory  minimums  for  over
225  grams  and  over  50  grams  back  to  20  years  and  10
years  respectively.  MCL  333.7401(2)(a)(ii)  and  (iii);  MCL
333.7403(2)(a)ii)  and  (iii)  (West  Supp.  1990)3

,

,

Over the  years,  the  Michigan  Court of Appeals  has,
as  in  the  case  at  bar,  sustained  the  imposition  of  the
mandatory  life  imprisonment  penalty  for  the  manufac-
ture,  delivery  or  possession  of 650  grams  or  more  of the
specified  controlled  substances.  In  People  v  Romano,  181
Mich  App  204,  219;  448  NW  2d  795,  802  (1989),  the  Court
summarized  its  repeated  holdings:

This  court  has  upheld  the  constitutionality  of
M.C.L.  § 333.7403;  M.S.A.  § 14.15  (7403),  as  well
as  the  similar  statute  M.C.L.  § 333.7401;  M.S.A.
§ 14.15(7401),  and  repeatedly  found  that  they
did  not  constitute  cruel  and  unusual  punish-
ment.  See,  e.g.,  [People  v Hamp,  170  Mich  App 24,
37  (1988)]  428  N.W.2d  16;  People  v  Leighty,  161
Mich.App.  565,  583,  411  N.W.2d  778  (1987),  lv.
den.  430  Mich  895  (1988);  People  v  Acosta,  153
Mich.App.  504,  515,  396  N.W.2d  463  (1986),  lv.
den.  428  Mich  865  (1987);  People  v  Matthews,  143

3  The  reason  for  this  amendment  was  provided  in  the
House  Legislative  Analysis  of  the  enactment,  Mich  House  Bill
4698  (as  enrolled),  dated  July  28,  1989:

The  acts  (Public  Act  275  of 1987  and  Public  Act  47 of
1988)  reducing  specified  minimum  prison  terms  for
illegal  manufacture,  delivery,  or  possession  of larger
quantities  of  narcotics  and  cocaine  dangerously
weakened  the  law  governing  controlled  substances
offenses.  The  bill  would  deter  drug  trafficking  by
increasing  those  mandatory  minimum  prison  terms
back  up  to  their  original  levels.

|

,

Mich.App.  45,  64,  371  N.W.2d  887  (1985);  People
v  Campbell,  115  Mich.App.  369,  376-381,  320
N.W.2d  381  (1982),  lv.  den.  417  Mich  879  (1983).
This  Court  has  specifically  considered  o
rejected  the argument  that  life  imprisonment  for
a  conviction  under  M.C.L.  § 333.7403(2)(a)(i);
M.S.A.  §  14.15(7403)(2)(a)(i)  constitutes  cruel
and  unusual  punishment.  People  v  McCarty,  113
Mich.App.  464,  317  N.W.2d  659  (1982),  lv.  den.
414  Mich  958  (1982).

In  People  v  McCarty,  113  Mich  App  464,  467-472;  317  NW
2d  659,  660-662  (1982),  the  Court  reinstated  the  charge  of
possession  of cocaine  of 650  grams  or  more  dismissed  by
the  trial  court:

The  United  States  Constitution  prohibits
cruel  and  unusual  punishment.  The  Michigan
punish-
Constitution  prohibits  cruel  or  unusual
ment.  [Mich  Const  1963,  art  1,  § 16]  The
trial
prescribed  by  the
court  found  that  the  penalty
statute  constituted  cruel  and/or  unusual  punish-
ment.  [emphasis  in  original]

The  dominant  test  for  determining  if  a
given  punishment  constitutes  cruel  and/or
unusual  punishment  was  stated  in  People  v
Lorentzen,  387  Mich  167,  176,  194  N.W.2d  827
(1972),  as  whether  “the  punishment  is  in  excess
of  any  that  would  be  suitable  to fit the  crime.”

At  first  blush,  we  are  inclined  to  agree  with
the  trial  court,  but  after  closely  examining  the
nature  of the  act,  gleaning  the  overall  legislative
scheme  from  recent  decisions  and  legislative
comments,  and  after  reviewing  the  penalties
prescribed  by  other  jurisdictions,  we  reach  the
following  conclusions.

The  questioned  statute  is an  integral  part  of an
overall  legislative  scheme  designed  to  stamp  out

10

11

illicit  sale,
possession,  and  use  of  certain  dan-
gerous  and/or  addictive  substances  by providing
graduated  punishment  for  offenses  involving  -
greater  amounts  of  mixtures  containing  con-
trolled  substances.  In finding  that  the ——
provisions  of  the  statute  are  not  viola-
penalty
equal  pro-
tive  of  the  title-object  clause  and  the
tection  and  due  process  clauses,  the
Court,  in
People  v  Lemble,  103  Mich  App  220,  222,  303
N.W.2d  191  (1981),  stated:

“The  statutory  scheme  of  the  controlled
substances  portion  of  the  health  code  pun-
ishes  those  found  to  be  in  possession  of
greater  amounts  of mixtures  containing  con-
trolled  substances  with  more  severe  penal-
ties.  We  find  that  the  legislative  policies
underlying  criminal  penalties  -  rehabilita-
tion  of  the  offender,  society’s  need  to  deter
the  behavior  in  others,  the  prevention  of the
offender  from  causing  injury  to  others  —  are
achieved  by this  statute’s  graduated  punish-
ment.  People  v  Lorentzen,  387  Mich  167,  194
N.W.2d  827  (1972).  The  penalties  that  may
be  imposed  under  this  statute  do  not  shock
the  judicial  conscience  in  light  of the  gravity
of  the  offenses.”

*

*

*

We  point  out  that  before  legislation  is
enacted  various  committees  seek  community
input.  The  information  obtained  is  examined
closely  to  determine  what  laws  need  to  be
enacted  to  assist  the  community  in  its  continu-
ing battle  against  the  illegal  sale,  possession  and
use  of  narcotics.  The  Legislature,  because  of the
information  it  obtains  and  the  studies  it  con-
ducts,  is better  equipped  than  we,  the  courts,  to
draft  laws  to  combat  the  serious  problems  of
society.

It  is  our  opinion  that  the  mandatory  life
sentence  provision  for  possession  of 650  or  more
grams  of  a  controlled  substance  was  well-
thought  out  by  the  Legislature,  and  premised
upon  two  of  the  legislative  policies  underlying
criminal  penalties  mentioned  in  Lemble  which
were:  (1)  the  prevention  of  the  offender  from
causing  injury  to  others;  and  (2)  society’s  need
to  deter  the  behavior  in  others.

The  amount  specified  in  the  statute,  650  or
more  grams,  is  a  large  quantity  that  suggests  an
amount  involving  major  drug  dealers.  Considering
that  the  street  value  of  650  or  more  grams  of
cocaine  is  close  to  one  million  dollars,  the
number  of  lives  affected  by the  possession,  sale
or  use  of  this  drug  would  be  substantial.  Cer-
tainly,  in  establishing  the  lifetime  penalty  provi-
sion  for  this  offense,  the  Legislature  sought  to
prevent  an  offender  from  causing  further  injury
to  others  which  would  necessarily  result  from
involvement  with  a  large  quantity  of  a  con-
trolled  substance.

Moreover,  it  is  apparent  that  deterrence  of
this  conduct  was  a  major  factor  in  prescribing  a
mandatory  life  sentence  for  this  offense.  Soci-
ety’s  need  to  deter  this  behavior  in  others  is  war-
ranted  by the  magnitude  of problems  involving  drugs
that  society  faces.

After  reviewing  the  statutes  in  other  juris-
dictions,  comparatively  the  Michigan  statute
imposes  the  most  severe  penalty  for  possession
of  a  controlled  substance  of  the  quantity
involved  in  this  case.  However,  the  Michigan
statute  is also  the  most  progressive.  It provides  grad-
uated  penalties  depending  upon  the  amount  pos-
sessed,  ranging  from  probation  for  possession  of

12

13

a  small  amount  to  the  lifetime  imprisonment
penalty  for  possession  of  an  amount  of  650  or
more  grams.  Most  other  jurisdictions  provide
one  penalty  for  all  offenders.

On  the  bases  of  the  gravity  of  the  offense,
the  potential  injury  to  others,  and  society’s  need
to  deter  this  behavior  in  others,  as  well  as  other
reasons  stated  within  this  opinion,  we  must  con-
clude  that  the  information  gathered  by the  Leg-
islature  required  a  severe  penalty  for  this  crime,
and  we  at  this  juncture  have  not  been  able  to
determine  that  this  legislation  was  unwise  nor
the  penalty  imposed  cruel  and/or  unusual.

(footnotes  omitted;  emphasis  added)

B.  Proportionality  In  Non-Capital  Cases

Petitioner  relies  upon  this  Court’s  proportionality
analysis  in  Solem  v  Helm,  463  US  277  (1983)  to  argue  that
his  sentence  of mandatory  life  imprisonment  is cruel  and
unusual  under  the  Eighth  Amendment.  However,  any
analysis  involving  Solem  must  also  take  into  consider-
ation  this  Court’s  decisions  in  Rummel  v  Estelle,  445  US
263  (1980)4  and  Hutto  v  Davis,  454  US  370  (1982).

In  Rummel,  445  US  at  274,  this  Court  held  that a life
sentence  imposed  under  Texas’s  recidivist  law  upon  a

4  In  Solem,  463  US  at  288,  n.  13  and  463  US  at  303,  n.  32,
this  Court  emphasized  that  it  was  not  overruling  Rummel but
was,  in  fact,  being  consistent  with  it.  The  facts,  however,  were
distinguishable  because  Rummel  was  eligible  for  reasonably
early  parole  whereas  Helm,  at  age  36,  was  sentenced  to  life
with  no  possibility  of  parole.

defendant  thrice  convicted  of  property-related  felonies
did  not  constitute  cruel  and  unusual  punishment.  The
Court  stated  that  as  to  its  prior  decisions  applying  the
prohibition  of  cruel  and  unusual  punishments:

.

.  one  could  argue  without  fear  of  contradic-
tion  by any  decision  of this  Court  that  for crimes
concededly  classified  and  classifiable  as  felo-
nies,  that  is,  as  punishable  by significant  terms
of  imprisonment  in  a  state  penitentiary,  the
length  of  the  sentence  actually  imposed  is
purely  a  matter  of  legislative  prerogative."

1!  This  is not  to say  that  a proportionality  princi-
ple would  not  come  into  play in the  extreme  example
mentioned  by the  dissent,  post,  at  288,  if a legislature
made  overtime  parking  a  felony  punishable  by  life
imprisonment.

Whereas  a  “bright  line”  can  be  drawn  between  the  pun-
ishment  of  death  and  “the  various  other  permutations
and  commutations  of  punishment  short  of  that  ultimate
sanction,”  the  line-drawing  would  be much  more  difficult
for  “any  constitutional  distinction”  between  different
terms  of years.  Id.  at  275.  The  Court  pointed  out  that  such
basic  line-drawing  “is  pre-eminently  the  province  of  the
legislature”  and  reiterated  Justice  White’s  caution  in
Coker  v  Georgia,  433  US  584,  592  (1977)  that  the  Court's
Eighth  Amendment  judgments  should  neither  be,  nor
appear  to  be,  merely  the  subjective  views  of the  individ-
ual  Justices.  Id.  at  274  -—  275.

In  Rummel  at  276,  this  Court  recognized  the  societal

interest  involved  in  the  punishment  of  recidivists:

14

15

Thus  the  interest  of the State  of Texas  here  is not
simply  that  of  making  criminal  the  unlawful
acquisition  of another  person’s  property;  it is  in
addition  the  interest,  expressed  in  all  recidivist
statutes,  in  dealing  in  a  harsher  manner  with
those  who  by repeated  criminal  acts  show  that
they  are  simply  incapable  of conforming  to  the
norms  of  society  as  established  by  its  criminal
law.

Further,  the  Court  stated:

The  purpose  of  a  recidivist  statute  such  as  that
involved
here  is not  to  simplify  the  task  of pros-
ecutors,  judges,  or  juries.  Its  primary  goals  are
to  deter  repeat  offenders  and,  at  some  point  in
the  life  of one  who  repeatedly  commits  criminal
offenses  serious  enough  to  be  punished  as  felo-
nies,  to  segregate  that  person  from  the  rest  of
society  for  an  extended  period  of  time.  This
segregation  and  its  duration  are  based  not
merely  on  that  person’s  most  recent  offense  but
also  on  the  propensities  he  has  demonstrated
over  a  period  of time  during  which  he  has  been
convicted  of  and  sentenced  for  other  crimes.

Like  the  line  dividing  felony  theft  from
petty  larceny,  the  point  at  which a recidivist  will
be  deemed  to  have  demonstrated  the  necessary
propensities  and  the  amount  of  time  that  the
recidivist  will  be  isolated  from  society  are  mat-
ters  largely  within  the  discretion  of  the  punish-
ing  jurisdiction.  Id.  at  284-285.

The  Court  warned  that  under  federalism  there  will
always  be  one  state  that  will  have  the  distinction  of
treating  particular  defendants  more  severely  than  any
other  state,  445  US  at  281  -  282:

Even  were  we  to  assume  that  the  statute
employed  against  Rummel  was  the  most  strin-
gent  found  in  the  50  States,  that  severity  hardly

would  render  Rummel’s
punishment  “grossly
disproportionate”  to  his  offenses  or  to  the  pun-
ishment  he  would  have  received  in  the  other
States.

*

*

*

Absent  a  constitutionally  imposed  unifor-
mity  inimical  to  traditional  notions  of  federal-
ism,  some  State  will  always  bear  the  distinction
particular  offenders  more  severely
of  treating
than  any  other  State.?7

27  The  dissent  draws  some  support  for  its  belief
that  Rummel’s  sentence  is  unconstitutional  by com-
paring  it  with  punishments  imposed  by  Texas  for
crimes  other  than  those  committed  by  Rummel.
Other  crimes,  of course,  implicate  other  societal  interests,
making  any  such  comparison  inherently  speculative.
Embezzlement,  dealing  in  “hard”  drugs,  and  forgery,
to  name  only  three  offenses,  could  be  denominated
“property  related”  offenses,  and  yet  each  can  be
viewed  as  an  assault  on  a  unique  set  of  societal
values  as  defined  by  the  political  process.  The
notions  embodied  in  the  dissent  that  if  the  crime
involved  “violence,”  see  post,  295-296,  n  12,  a  more
severe  penalty  is  warranted  under  objective  stan-
dards  simply  will  not  wash,  whether  it be  taken  as  a
matter  of  morals,  history,  or  law.  Caesar’s  death  at
the  hands  of  Brutus  and  his  fellow  conspirators  was
undoubtedly  violent;  the  death  of Hamlet's  father  at
the  hands  of  his  brother,  Claudius,  by  poison  was
not.  Yet  there  are  few,  if  any,  States  which  do  not
punish  just  as  severely  murder  by  poison  (or
attempted  murder  by poison)  as  they  do  murder  or
attempted  murder  by  stabbing.  The  highly  placed
executive  who  embezzles  huge  sums  from a state
savings  and  loan  association,  causing  many  share-
holders  of  limited  means  to  lose  substantial  parts  of
their  savings,  has  committed  a  crime  very  different

16

17

from  a  man  who  takes  a  smaller  amount  of. money
from  the  same  savings  and  loan  at the point of a gun.
Yet  rational  people  could  disagree  as  to which  crimi-
nal  merits  harsher  punishment.  By the  same  token,  a
State  cannot  be  required  to  treat  persons  who  have
committed  three  “minor”  offenses  less  severely  than
persons  who  have  committed  one  or  two  “more
serious”  offenses.  If  nothing  else,  the  three-time
offender’s  conduct  supports  inferences  about  his
ability  to  conform  with  social  norms  that  are  quite
different  from  possible  inferences  about  first-  or  sec-
ond-time  offenders.

In  short,  the  “seriousness”  of  an  offense  or  a
pattern  of  offenses  in  modern  society  is  not  a  line,
but  a  plane.  Once  the  death  penalty  and  other  pun-
ishments  different  in kind  from  fine  or  imprisonment
have  been  put  to  one  side,  there  remains  little  in  the
way  of  objective  standards  for  judging  whether  or
not  a  life  sentence  imposed  under  a  recidivist  statute
for  several  separate  felony  convictions  not  involving
“violence”  violates  the  cruel-and-unusual-punish-
ment  prohibition  of  the  Eighth  Amendment.  As  Mr.
Justice  Frankfurter  noted  for  the  Court  in  Gore  v
United  States,  357  US  386,  393  (1958),  “[w]hatever
views  may  be entertained  regarding  severity  of pun-
ishment,  whether  one  believes  in  its  efficacy  or  its
futility,
...  these  are  peculiarly  questions  of legisla-
tive  policy.”  (emphasis  added)

Justice  Powell  in  his  dissent  agreed  that  the  States
cannot  be  required  to  provide  for  uniform  sentences,  445
US  at  299,  n.  19:

A  State’s  choice  of  a  sentence  will,  of  course,
never  be  unconstitutional  simply  because  the
penalty  is harsher  than  the  sentence  ~  Ay by
other  States  for  the  same  crime.  Such
a  rule
would  be inconsistent  with  principles  of federal-
ism.  The  Eighth  Amendment  prohibits  grossly
disproportionate  punishment,  but  it  does  not

uire  local  sentencing  decisions  to  be  con-

os led  by majority  vote  of  the  States.

In  Hutto  v  Davis,  this  Court  followed  P-«mmel  and
upheld  a  sentence  of  two  consecutive  20-year  prison
terms  and  two  separate  fines  of $10,000.00  for convictions
of possession  with  intent  to  distribute  and  distribution  of
approximately  nine  ounces  of  marihuana  with  a street
value  of  about  $200.00.

Justice  Powell  in  concurring  in  the  judgment  “reluc-
tantly”  concluded  Rummel  was  controlling.  454  US at  375.
However,  Justice  Powell  did  concede  that  “under  our
system  the  limits  of  a  prison  sentence  normally  are  a
matter  of  legislative  prerogative.”  Id.  at  377.

Justice  Brennan,  with  whom  Justice  Marshall  and
Justice  Stevens  joined,  dissented  and  argued  that  Rum-
mel’s  holding  was  rather  narrow

.  in  the  context  of  Texas’  habitual  offender
statute,  the  imposition  of  a  life  sentence  on
Rummel  served  the  legitimate  state  interests  of
deterring  recidivism  and  of Tecan habitual
offenders  “from  the  rest
for  an
extended  period  of  time.”  [445  US]  at 284.

society

Hutto  v  Davis,  454  US  382.

Finally,  in  Solem,  this  Court  for  the  first  time  found  a
sentence  for  a  term  of years  to  be  grossly  disproportion-
ate  to  the  crime  and  thus  prohibited  by  the  Eighth
Amendment.  Justice  Powell,  joined  by  the  other  dis-
senters  in  Rummel,5  found  that  a  life  sentence  without  the

5  Justices  Brennan,  Marshall  and  Stevens.

possibility  of  parole  for  a  seventh  nonviolent  felony®  of

summarized  at  463  US  290:

In  sum,  we  hold  as  a  matter  of  principle  that  a
criminal  sentence  must  be  proportionate  to  the
crime  for  which  the  defendant  has  been  convicted.
Reviewing  courts,  of course,  should  grant  substan-
tial  deference  to  the  broad  authority  that  legisla-
tures  necessarily  possess  in  determining  the  types
and  limits  of punishments  for crimes,  as  well  as  to
the discretion  that  trial  courts  possess  in sentencing
convicted  criminals.

(footnote  omitted)

In  footnote  16  following  this  passage,  Justice  Powell
stated  in  part:

In  view  of the  substantial  deference  that  must  be
accorded  legislatures  and  sentencing  courts,  a
reviewing  court  rarely  will  be  required  to
engage  in  extended  analysis  to  determine  that  a
sentence  is  not  constitutionally  disproportion-
ate.

When  reviewing  sentences,  Justice  Powell  instructed
that  the  courts  should  be  guided  by  “objective  factors”
including:  (a) the  gravity  of the  offense  and  the  harshness
of the  penalty;  (b)  the  sentences  imposed  for  other  crimes
in  the  same  jurisdiction;  and  (c)  the  sentences  imposed
for  commission  of  the  same  crime  in  other  jurisdictions.
Id.  at  290-292.  Justice  Powell  assumed  that  courts  were
competent  to  judge  the  gravity  of an  offense  “at  least  on  a

6  Alcohol  was  a  contributing  factor  in each  of the  offenses.

463  US  at  280.

relative  scale.”  Comparisons  could  be made  in light  of the
harm  caused  or  threatened  to  the  victim  or  society,  and
the  culpability  of  the  offender.  Id.  at  292.

C.  Solem  Analysis

Respondent  submits  that  in  the  case  at  bar  Peti-
tioner’s  life  sentence  is not  so  grossly  disproportionate  as
to  be  cruel  and  unusual  under  the  Eighth  Amendment.
Unlike  in  Rummel  where  the  Court  focused  on  and
deferred  to  society’s  interest  in  punishing  recidivists,  in
Solem  the  Court  focused  on  the  principal  felony  “one  of
the  most  passive  felonies  a  person  could  commit.”  463  US
at  296  quoting  State  v  Helm,  287  NW  2d  497,  501  (S.D.,
1980)  (Henderson,  J., dissenting).  See  also:  463  US  296,  n.
21.7  Whether  the  focus  is on  Michigan’s  paramount  inter-
est  in  stamping  out  illegal  drug  trafficking  or  in  examin-
ing  the  gravity  of  Petitioner’s  crime,  the  punishment  of
mandatory  life  imprisonment  cannot  be  called  grossly  or
significantly  disproportionate.

The  Michigan  Legislature  in  1978  enacted  the  manda-
tory  life  term  for  possession  of  650  grams  or  more  of
cocaine  because  of an  ever-increasing  spiral  of drug  traf-
fic,  drug  abuse  and  drug-related  crime.  Today,  the  situa-
tion  appears  to  be  even  worse.  According  to  President
George  Bush,  the  top  priority  for  the  United  States  is the

7  In  Solem,  Justice  Powell  did  not  concern  himself  with  the
legislature’s  prerogative  apparently  because  South  Dakota’s
recidivist  statute  made  the  imposition  of  life  imprisonment
discretionary.  Id.,  at  299,  n.  26.  Once  a  sentence  of  life
imprisonment  was  meted  out,  however,  the  person  so  sen-
tenced  was  not  eligible  for  parole.  Id.  at  282.

20

<a a ney

21

“war”  on  illegal  drugs.  In  that  fight,  the  federal  govern-
ment  will  spend  $10.6  billion  annually.  Deaths  from
cocaine  and  heroin  abuse  are  increasing.  In  urban  areas
such  as  Detroit  and  Washington,  D.C.,  the  pathologies
related  to  drug  abuse  -  violent  crimes  and  property
crimes,  AIDS,  crack  babies  -  are  most  pronounced.  Urban
murders,  many  of  them  drug-related,  are  likely  to  reach
record  numbers  this  year.  In  Michigan,  an  estimated
67,000  persons  -  7.3  of  every  1,000  state  residents  —  use
cocaine  at  least  once  a  week.®  In  Detroit,  one  of the  areas
of the  United  States  under  heavy  siege  from  crack  cocaine
since  1985,  the  first  large  group  of crack-exposed  5-year-
olds  started  kindergarten  this  fail.  They  suffer  from  an
array  of  neurological,  emotional  and  learning  problems.’

In  Young  v  Miller,  883  F2d  1276,  1285  (6th  Cir.,  1989),
the  Sixth  Circuit  Court  of  Appeals  concluded  under  a
Solem  proportionality  analysis  that Michigan’s  mandatory
term  of  life  imprisonment  for  a  first  offender  for  posses-
sion  with  intent  to  deliver  of 650  grams  or  more  of heroin
was  not  cruel  and  unusual:

[E]ven  assuming  Michigan  has  the  toughest
drug law  of all  the  states,  the question,  as  we  see
it,  is  whether  the  Michigan  statutory  scheme  is
totally  out  of  line  with  these  other  states.
Clearly,  most  of the  states  listed  above  prescribe
heavy  penalties  for  drug  trafficking,  albeit  with

8  “War  On  Drugs”,  Detroit  Free  Press,  September  8, 1990,  at

6A.

9  “Crack  Casts  Pall  Over  Kindergarten”,  Detroit  Free  Press,

August  8,  1990  at  1A.

much  more  consideration  for  the  criminal  back-
ground  and  possibility  of  rehabilitation  of  the
offender.  In  many  of these  states,  the  sentencing
judge  has  the  discretion  to  impose  a life  sen-
tence  or  a  significant  term  of  years  for  drug
traffickers.  Additionally,  given  the  present  polit-
ical  climate,  it  may  well  be  that  many  of  these
states  will  change  their  penal  codes  to  provide
for  even  stiffer  sentences  for  drug  offenders  in
the  future.  Thus,  notwithstanding  its  harshness,
we  believe  Michigan’s  drug  law  is  in  the  range
of  the  drug  laws  of  other  states.

Further,  the  Court  stated:

Considering  the  severity  of the  crime,  we  refuse
to  interfere  with  Michigan’s  efforts  to  punish
major  drug  traffickers  to  the  fullest  extent  of the
law,  even  those  who  are  first  offenders.  Id.

In  the  course  of its  Solem  analysis,  the  Court  remarked  on
the  seriousness  of  the  defendant’s  crime:

We  begin  our  Solem  analysis  with  the  first  and
primary  factor  —  the  gravity  of  the  offense  and
the  harshness  of  the  penalty.  Petitioner  Young
was  convicted  of  possession  with  intent  to
deliver  650  grams  or  more  of  heroin.  We  have
little  doubt  that  her  crime  is one  of the  gravest  a
person  can  commit  today.  Young  possessed  with
intent  to  distribute  approximately  1300  grams  of
heroin  (almost  twice  the amount  upon  which  the
penalty  could  be  predicated),  an  extremely
addictive  narcotic.  The  ripple  effect  on  Society  of
such  a  large  quantity  of heroin  is staggering  to
contemplate.  Indeed,  as  one  court  has  aptly
observed:  “Except  in  rare  —_ the  murderer's  red
hand  falls  on  one  victim  only,  however  grim  the
blow;  but  the foul hand  of the  rug dealer  blights  life
after life and,  like  the  vampire  of fable,  creates  others
in  its  owner's  evil  image  —  others  who  create  others
still,  across  our  land  and  down  our  generations,

22

23

sparing  not  even  the  unborn.”  Terrebonne  v  Butler,
848  F.2d  500,  504  (Sth  Cir.  1988)  (en  banc),  cert.
denied,  __  US  __,  109  S.  Ct.  1140  (1989).  We
thus  consider  the  crime  that  Young  committed
to  be  very  serious.  (emphasis  added)

In  his  dissent  in  Rummel,  445  US  at  295,  n.  12, Justice
Powell  had  acknowledged  that  drug  cases  might  not  be
easily  compartmentalized  under  his  proportional  anal-
ysis:

I do  not  suggest  that  all  criminal  acts  may  be
separated  into  precisely  identifiable  compart-
ments.  A  professional  seller  of  addictive  drugs
may  inflict  greater  bodily  harm  upon  members
of society  than  the  person  who  commits  a  single
assault.  But  the  difficulties  of  line-drawing  that
might  be  presented  in  other  cases  need  not
obscure  our  vision  here.

The  point  is that  in  Michigan  by making  the  sentence
mandatory  in  1978  the  Legislature  made  clear  the  gravity
with  which  it  viewed  the  dealing  in  large  quantities  of
drugs.  Subsequently,  in  1987  and  1989  when  it eased  and
then  tightened  the  mandatory  penalties  for  less  than  650
grams  of  schedule  1  and  2 controlled  substances,  it  did
not  change  the  penalty  for  650  grams  or  more.  Thus,
unlike  in  Solem  where  life  imprisonment  was  discretion-
ary  with  the  sentencing  court,  the  Michigan  Legislature
made  its  interest  crystal  clear.  As  such,  this  Court  should
as  in  Rummel,  which  after  all  is  still  viable,  defer  to  the
legislature’s  definitive  statement  of  the  public  interest.1°
Michigan’s  controlled  substance  statutes  have  a  rational

10  Note,  Solem  v  Helm:  Extension  of Eighth  Amendment  Pro-
portionality  Review  to  Noncapital  Punishment,  69  lowa  L R  775,
782-783,  793-794  (1984).

basis  to  Michigan’s  interests  in  eliminating  the  scourge  of
large-scale  drug  trafficking.'1  See:  Carmona  v  Ward,  576
F2d  405,  410-412  (1978)  cert  den  439  US  1091  (1979).

Petitioner  cites  eleven  other  states  that  provide  for
possible  life  sentences,  but  with  parole,  for  similar
offenses.  Some,  however,  do  have  mandatory  minimums
ranging  from  ten  to  thirty  years.  (Brief  of  Petitioner,  pp
42-43).  Petitioner  received  his  sentence  at  age  42.  Under
Standard  Mortality  Tables,  his  life  expectancy  would  be
30.41  years.  MCL  500834  (West  Supp.  1990,  p.  37).
Although  the  sentence  herein  may  be considered  harsh,  it
is  not  out  of  line  with  the  range  available.  In  fact,  it  is
similar  to  the  40-year  sentence  upheld  in  Hutto  v  Davis
which  involved  nine  ounces  of  marihuana.  As  noted  by
Justice  Powell  in  his  dissent  in  Rummel,  445  US  299,  n.19,
the  principle  of  federalism  would  not  allow  the  striking
down  of  a  sentence  just  because  it  is  the  most  severe
among  the  states.  The  question  has  to  be  whether  it  is

11  Built-in  to  Michigan’s  statute  prohibiting  possession  is a
clear  legislative  presumption  that  he  who  possesses  a  large
amount  of narcotics  should  be dealt  with  in  the  same  way  as  a
seller.  Cf.,  People  v  Broadie,  37  NY2d  100;  371  NYS2d  471;  332
NE  2d  338,  343  (1975)  cert.  den.  423  US  950  (1975).  Such  is
reasonable  especially  in  Petitioner’s  case  where  the  672.5
grams  of  cocaine  he  was  transporting  was  pure  and  worth
between  $67,000.00  and  $100,000.00.  Petitioner  was  arrested
with  the  standard  tools  of the  drug dealer:  ten  small  packets  of
cocaine,  three  little  vials  of cocaine,  a  beeper,  a  coded  address
book,  some  marihuana  cigarettes,  assorted  pills  including  Per-
codan  and  approximately  $3,500  in  cash.

24

25

grossly  disproportionate.  Respondent  submits  that  given
the  evil  being  addressed,  it  is  not.

Under  the  second  Solem  factor  of  examining  sen-
tences  for  other  crimes  in  the  same  jurisdiction,  in  Young
v  Miller,  883  F2d  at  1283,  the  Sixth  Circuit  found  no  gross
disproportion:

Large-scale  drug  traffickers  (defined  by  the
Michigan  legislature  as  those  persons  possess-
ing  650  grams  of  Level  I narcotics  or  more)  are
treated  the  same  as  first-degree  murderers
(Mich.  Comp.  Laws  §  750.316).  Both  receive
mandatory  life  imprisonment  without  parole,
without  regard  to  the  criminal  record  or  reha-
bilitative  potential  of the  offender.  The  Michigan
Penal  Code  also  provides  for  a  mandatory  life
sentence  with  possibility  of  parole  for  treason
(§750.544).  It  authorizes  discretionary  life  sen-
tences  with  possibility  of  parole  for  various
other  crimes  such  as  second-degree  murder
(§750.317),  attempted  murder  (§750.91),  causing
death  by  explosives  (§750.327),  armed  robbery
(§750.529),  first-degree  criminal  sexual  conduct
(§750.520b(2)),  and  kidnapping  (§750.349).  In  its
sentencing  scheme,  the  Michigan  legislature  has  rec-
ognized  the  devastating  consequences  of large-scale
drug  trafficking  by treating  that  crime  as  being  more
serious  than  many  of the  violent  crimes.  It  has
chosen  to  take  the  drug  problem  in  its  state  “by
the  horns”  by  going  after  the  major  sources  of
illegal  drugs.  By imposing  such  a  harsh  penalty,
it obviously  hopes  to  provide  a  strong  deterrent
to  those  who  would  deal  illegal  drugs  and  pos-
sibly  prevent  some  of  the  violent  crimes  often
associated  with  drug  trafficking.  While  one
might  question  the  trial  court’s  lack  of discretion
in  the  sentencing  process,  we  certainly  do  not

aS

-

believe  that  the  punishment  imposed  for  large-
scale  drug
traffickers  is  in  all  cases  inappropri-
ate.  (emphasis  added)!2

Also,  as  pointed  out  in McCarty,  317  NW2d  at 467-472,  the
drug  statutes  provide  for  graduated  punishment.

D.  Individualized  Sentencing

Petitioner  attempts  to  analogize  to  this  Court’s  capi-
tal  punishment  cases  mandating  individualized  sentenc-
ing.  Such  an  analogy  is  misplaced,  however.  Justice
Powell  in  his  dissent  in  Rummel,  445  US  at  301,  allowed
that,  “Of course,  the  Constitution  does  not  bar  mandatory
sentences.”

In  Lockett  v  Ohio,  438  US  586,  604-605  (1978)  the
plurality  opinion  makes  clear  the  profound  difference
between  the  imposition  of death  and  all  other  noncapital
sentences:

We  recognize  that,  in  noncapital  cases,  the
established  practice  of  individualized  sentences
rests  not  on  constitutional  commands,  but  on
public  policy  enacted  into  statutes.  The  consid-
erations  that  account  for  the  wide  acceptance  of
individualization  of  sentences  in  noncapital
cases  surely  cannot  be  thought  less  important  in
capital  cases.  Given  that  the  imposition  of death
by  public  authority  is  so  profoundly  different
from  all  other  penalties,  we  cannot  avoid  the

12  Although  a  prisoner  might  technically  be  eligible  for
parole  after  ten  years  he cannot  be paroled  if there  is a  written
objection  by  the  sentencing  judge,  or  his  successor  in  office.
MCL  791.234(4)(b).  Also,  for  the  mandatory  life  sentences,
there  is  the  possibility  of  a  governor’s  reprieve,  commutation
or  pardon.  MCL  791.244.

26

naam

27

conclusion  that  an  individualized  decision  is
essential  in  capital  cases.  The  need  for  treating
each  defendant  in a capital  case  with  that  degree
of  respect  due  the  uniqueness  of  the  individual
is  far  more  important  than  in  noncapital  cases.
A  variety  of
probation,
flexible  techniques  -
few  -  and
parole,  work  furloughs,  to  name  a
various  postconviction  remedies,  may  be  avail-
able  to  modify  an  initial  sentence  of  confine-
ment  in  noncapital  cases.  The  nonavailability  of
corrective  or  modifying  mechanisms  with
respect  to  an  executed  capital  sentence  under-
scores  the  need  for  individualized  consideration
as  a  constitutional  requirement  in  imposing  the
death  sentence.'?

13  Sentencing  in  noncapital  cases  presents  no
comparable  problems.  We  emphasize  that  in  dealing
with  standards  for  imposition  of  the  death  sentence
we  intimate  no  view  regarding  the  authority  of  a
State  or  of  the  Congress  to  fix  mandatory,  minimum

.  sentences  for  noncapital  crimes.

This  Court’s  recent  decision  in  Mistretta  v  United
States,  488  US  ___;  109  S Ct 647  (1989)  upheld  the  limiting
by  Congress  of  judicial  discretion  as  to  sentencing.  In
Walton  v  Arizona,  497  US  __;  110  S Ct  3047,  3076  (1990),
Justice  Blackmun’s  dissent  (joined  in by Justices  Brennan,
Marshall  and  Stevens),  after  reiterating  the  “qualitative
difference”  between  death  and  all  other  penalties,
pointed  out  the  practical  distinction  for  noncapital  sen-
tences:

In  noncapital  cases,  of  course,  the  States  are
given  broad  latitude  to  sacrifice  precision  for
predictability  by  imposing  determinate  sen-
tences  and  restricting  the  defendant’s  ability  to
present  evidence  in  mitigation  or  excuse.

In  United  States  v  Belgard,  894  F2d  1092,  1100  (9th  Cir.,
1990),  the  Court  explained:

Belgard  also  attacks  the  formulation  of  the
Sentencing  Guidelines  in  the  area  of  probation
because  they  curtail  the  authority  of the  district
judges  to  individualize  sentences.  That  attack
cannot  succeed.

First,  the  Constitution  does  not  require  indi-
vidualized  sentences  in non-capital  cases.  United
States  v  White,  869  F2d  822,  825  (5th  Cir.),  cert.
__,  109  S Ct 3172,  (1989).  See  also
denied,  __  US
Mistretta,  488  US  at  __,  190  S.Ct.  at  650  (“the
scope  of  judicial  discretion  with  respect  to  a
sentence  is  subject  to  congressional  control”);
Lockett  v  Ohio,  438  US  586,  605,  98  S.Ct.  2954,
2965,  57  L.Ed.2d  973  (1978)  (“the  established
practice  of individualized  sentences  rests  not  on
constituti»nal  commands,  but  on  public  policy
enacted  into  statutes”).  Congress  has  the  power
to  enact  mandatory  and  determinate  sentencing
laws  and  to completely  divest  the courts  of their
sentencing  discretion,  and  has  exercised  this
power.  Lockett,  438  US  at  603-05,  98 S Ct at  2965.
See  also  United  States  v Grayson,  438  US  41, 45, 98
S  Ct  2610,  2613,  57  L.Ed.2d  582  (1978)  (early
practice  was  for  Congress  to  prescribe  specific
sentence).

For  .his  Court  to  try  to  weigh  Petitioner’s  individual
characteristics  to  determine  whether  his  sentence  was
proper  would  be  fruitless  given  that  there  was  no  exten-
sive  development  in  the  trial  court  of  all  mitigating  and
aggravating  factors  which  might  have  been  considered.
Such  a  review  would  entail  even  more  judicial  line-draw-
ing.

Oe

28

29

E.  Solem  v  Helm  Should  Be  Overruled

As  made  out  in  the  vigorous  dissent  in  Solem  by
former  Chief  Justice  Burger,  joined  by  Justices  White,
Rehnquist  and  O’Connor,  the  Solem  majority  launched
into  “uncharted  and  unchartable  waters.”  463  US  at  314.
As  Chief  Justice  Burger  concluded:

The  Court’s  traditional  abstention  from
reviewing  sentences  of  imprisonment  to  ensure
that  punishment  is  “proportionate”  to  the  crime
is well  founded  in  history,  in  prudential  consid-
erations,  and  in  traditions  of  comity.  Today’s
conclusion  by five  Justices  that  they  are  able  to
say  that  one  offense  has  less  “gravity”  than
another  is nothing  other  than  a bald  substitution
of  individual  subjective  moral  values  for  those
of  the  legislature.  Nor,  as  this  case  well  illus-
trates,  are  we  endowed  with  Solomonic  wisdom
that  permits  us  to  draw  principled  distinctions
between  sentences  of  different  length  for  a
chronic  “repeater”  who  has  demonstrated  that
he  will  not  abide  by  the  law.  463  US  314.

The  Solem  proportionality  test  has  been  criticized  by
many  as  nonobjective  and  readily  manipulable.'?  The
gravity  of  the  offense  compared  to  the  harshness  of  the
penalty  begs the question  of proportionality  as  it suggests
no  truly  objective  factors.'4  The  Solem  formulation  does
not  take  into  account  crimes  which  may  not  be  violent
but  which  society  in  general  believes  harmful  such  as
fraud  or  drug  crimes.'15  Applying  the  Solem  standard  in

13  See:  Note,  Cruel  and  Unusual  Punishment  and  Sodomy
LR

Statutes:  The  Breakdown  Of the  Solem  v  Helm  Test,  56  U Chi
367,  377  and  commentators  cited  at  377,  n.  58.

14  56  U  Chi  L R at  377.
15  Id.  at  378-379.

the  case  at  bar  reveals  the  inadequacy  of  the  criteria  for
weighing  the  gravity  of  an  offense  provided  in  Solem.

Part  of  the  reason  Helm’s  sentence  was  overturned
whereas  Rummel’s  was  not  may  have  been  that  Helm’s
sentence  shocked  the  conscience.  He  appeared  to  be  an
alcoholic  and  could  not  even  fully  recall  his  bad-check
offense  for  purposes  of  providing  a  factual  basis  for  his
guilty  plea.  Helm  pleaded  guilty  to  the  habitual  charge
over  his  attorney’s  objection  and  then  waived  his  right  to
a  presentence  investigation  and  received  immediate  sen-
tencing.  The  sentencing  judge  appeared  confused  as  to
whether  Helm  would  be  eligible  for  parole.'®  Petitioner
Harmelin  concedes  his  crime  was  much  more  serious.

Although  in  the  years  since  the  Court’s  ruling  in
Solem  there  have  been  few  reversals  of  sentences  on  the
basis  of  the  Solem  standard,  the  point  is  that  numerous
minor  sentences  had  to  be  reviewed  on  appeal  because
Solem  invited  such  analysis.  See for example:  United  States  v
Sullivan,  895  F2d  1030  (5th  Cir.,  1990);  United  States  v
Benefield,  889  F2d  1061,  1063-1065  (11th  Cir.,  1989);  United
States  v  Savage,  888  F2d  528  (7th  Cir.,  1989)  reh den  894  F2d
1495  (1989);  State  v  Elbert,  125  NH  1;  480  A2d  854,  862
(1984)  (Souter,  J.).

As  noted  in  Cabana  v  Bullock,  474  US  376,  386  (1986)
(plurality),  the  type  of  Eighth  Amendment  propor-
tionality  inquiry  undertaken  in  Solem  involves  case-by-
case,  totality-of-the-circumstances  decision-making.  Jus-
tice  Scalia  has  noted  such a test  for  the  appellate  judge
relegates  him  or  her  to  the  less  exalted  role  of  finder  of

16  69  lowa  L  R  supra  at  777-778.

Ee

31

CONCLUSION

For  the  foregoing  reasons,  this  Court  should  affirm
the  judgment  of the  Michigan  Court  of Appeals  and  leave
intact  MCL  333.7403.

fact  as  opposed  to  determiner  of  law.  This  in  turn  facili-
tates  judicial  arbitrariness. 1”

Given  the  scourge  of illegal  drugs,  the  Michigan  Leg-
islature’s  answer  to the problem  was  not  disproportionate
to  the  gravity  of the  crime.  See:  United  State  v  Bernal,  884
F2d  1518,  1520-1521  (ist  Cir.,  1989);  United  States  v  Mar-
torano,  866  F2d  62,  69-70  (3rd  Cir.,  1989)  cert  den  __  US
__;  110  S Ct  1128  (1990);  United  States  v  Aiello,  864  F2d
257,  265  (2nd  Cir.,  1988);  United  States  v  Stewart,  820  F2d
1107,  1108  (9th  Cir.,  1987)  (Kennedy,  J.) cert  den  484  US
867  (1987).  In  that  regard,  it is the  legislature,  composed
of  elected  representatives  of  the  people  from  a  diverse
cross-section  of the  jurisdiction,  that  is best  equipped  to
draw  moral  lines  and  measure  public  sentiment.  It is very
difficult  to  compare  crimes  that  may  implicate  different
sucietal  interests.  To  not  allow  deference  to  the  Michigan
Legislature’s  determination  of the  gr@Vity  of drug  crimes
and  the  severity  of  the  punishment  is  to  trample  on  the
fundamental  concepts  of  federalism.'®
