SUMMARY  OF ARGUMENT

This  case  involves  a  simple  principle  of  law  that  is
nonetheless  fundamental  to  any  system  of  justice:
the
punishment  must  fit  the  crime.
In  the  first  instance,  of
course,  that  judgment  rests  with  the  legislature.  But,  like
all  legislative  judgments,  it  is  subject  to  constitutional
review.

Thus,  this  Court  has  consistently  recognized  that  the
Eighth  Amendment’s  ban  on  "cruel  and  unusual"  punish-
ment  incorporates  the  notion  of  proportionality.
In  the
death  penalty  context,  the  relevance  of  proportionality
analysis  is  now  broadly  accepted,  even  if its  application
in  particular  cases  is  still  hotly  disputed.
See,  e.8.,
Enmund  v.  Florida,  458  U.S.  782  (1982);  Coker  v.  Geor-
gia,  433  U.S.  584  (1977).
Outside  the  death  penalty
context,  however,  this  Court  has  engaged  in  a  vigorous
debate  about  the  propriety  of  proportionality  review.
Yet,  even  those  members  of  the  Court  who  approach
such  review  with  the  greatest  skepticism  have  acknowl-
edged  that  some  sentences  are  so  out  of  line  with  pre-
vailing  standards  that  they  cannot  be  reconciled  with

* See  Appendix  1 to  the  Petition  for  Certiorari.
*  See  Appendix  4 to  the  Petition  for  Certiorari.

Id.  at  4-5.

''  See  Appendix  5 to  the  Petition  for  Certiorari.

3

Eighth  Amendment  principles.  Rummel  v.  Estelle,  445
U.S.  263,  274  n.11  (1980).

The
It  is  difficult  to  reach  any  other  conclusion.
principle  of  proportionate  sentencing  dates  back  at  least
as  far  as  the  Magna  Carta  and,  while  evidence  of  the
framers’  intent  on  this  issue  is  relatively  scarce,  there  is
certainly  no  clear  indication  in  the  constitutional  debates
that  the  framers  meant  to  depart  from  this  historicaily
endorsed  view  when  they  adopted  the  Eighth  Amend-
ment.  Cf. Rutan  v.  Republican  Party  of Illinois,  110  S.Ct.
2729,  _,  58  U.S.L.W.  4872,  4881  (June  21,  1990)
(Scalia,  J., dissenting).

The  text  of  the  Eighth  Amendment  also  supporis  a
role  for  ihe  courts  in  reviewing  legislative  sentences.
The  reference  to  “cruel  and  unusual"  punishments  is
admittedly  vague,  but  the  ban  on  "excessive  fines"  is
Nothing  in  either  logic  or  history
clear  and  specific.
supports  a  distinction  between  “excessive  fines"  and  ex-
cessive  imprisonment.  Those  who  argue  that  the  former
is a  matter  of constitutional  concern  but  the  latter  is not
must,  at  the  very  least,  bear  the  burden  of justifying  such
a  counterintuitive
interpretation  of  the  constitutional
language.  That  burden  cannot  be  met.

The  real  issue,  therefore,  is  how  to  judge  when  a
sentence  becomes  “excessive”  under  the  Eighth  Amend-
In  resolving  that  question,  this  Court  is not  writ-
ment.
ing on  a  clean  slate.  Less  than  a  decade  ago,  the  major-
ity  in  Solem  v.  Helm,  463  U.S.  277  (1983),  adopted  a
three-part  test  that  weighs  the  gravity  of the  offense,  the
sentence  imposed  by  the  same  jurisdiction  for  other
crimes,  and  the  sentence  imposed  by  other  jurisdictions
for  the  same  crime.

That  test  does  not  unduly  interfere  with  legislative
‘To  the  contrary,  deference  to  legislative
prerogatives.
judgments  is  built  into  the  Solem  test.
For  that  reason,
perhaps,  amici  have  been  unable  to  find  a  single  prison

sentence  that  has  been  reversed  in  the  federal  courts
under  the  Solem  standard.
The  sentence  in  this  case,
however,  cannot  be  sustained  if Solem  is  to  have  any
meaning  at  all.

Amici  do  not  discount  the  gravity  of  Harmelin’s
offense,  just  as  this  Court  did  not  minimize  the  serious-
ness  of rape  in Coker  v.  Georgia,  428  U.S.  at  597-98.  But
the  fact  that  an  offense  is  serious  does  not  mean  that
any  and  all  punishments  are  constitutionally  appropriate.
‘n particular,  this  Court  has  frequently  recognized  a
bright  line  distinction  between  murder  and  all  other
crimes.  /d.  at  598.  Yet,  the  only  other  crime  that  Mich-
igan  punishes  by life  imprisonment  without  the  possibili-
ty of parole  is first  degree  murder.  Second  degree  mur-
der  and  kidnapping,  among  many  others,  are  subject  to
less  severe  sentences.

~

The  punishment  Harmelin  received  in  this  case  is
also” grossly  disproportionate  to  what  he  would  have
received  in  virtually  every  other  state  in  the  nation.
Here,  as  in Solem,  Michigan  can  point  to  only  one  other
jurisdiction  that  would  have  imposed  a  similar  sentence
on  the  facts  of this  case.  The  differences  are  dramatic  in
many  instances.
Under  federal
law,  for  example,
Harmelin  could  not  have  been  sentenced  to  more  than
one  year  in jail for  possession  of 672.5  grams  of cocaine.
The  Constitution  does  not  require  Michigan  to
endorse  the  federal  government’s  approach  to  this  prob-
lem.  At  the  same  time,  it does  not  permit  Michigan,  or
any  other  state,  to  subject  its  citizens  to  "cruel  and  unus-
ual"  punishment.  By any  objective  measure,  that  consti-
tutional  line  has  been  crossed  in this  case.

ARGUMENT
I.  THE  DECISIONS  OF  THIS  COURT  FIRMLY
ESTABLISH  THAT  THE  POWER  OF  THE  LEGIS-
LATURE  TO  SET  CRIMINAL  SENTENCES  IS
AND  MUST  BE  SUBJECT  TO  EIGHTH  AMEND-
MENT  REVIEW

The  legal  issues  raised  by this  case  are  indistinguish-
able  from  those  decided  by this  Court  in  Solem  v.  Helm,
463  U.S.  277.
Unless  Solem  is  now  overruled,  its legal
principles  are  therefore  controlling.  Those  principles
First,  the  Eighth  Amendment's
can  be  simply  stated.
ban  on  "cruel  and  unusual"  punishment  incorporates  a
notion  of  proportionality.
Thus,  any  criminal  sentence
that  is  disproportionate  to  the  offense  violates  constitu-
tional  standards.
Second,  it  is  the  responsibility  of  the
courts  to  enforce  the  relevant  constitutional  standard  by
considering:
(a)  the  gravity  of  the  offense;  (b)  the sen-
tence  imposed  by the  same  jurisdiction  for other crimes;
and  (c)  the  sentence  imposed  by  other  jurisdictions  for
the  same  crime.  /d.  at  290-92.

This  standard  does  not  interfere  with  legislative
prerogatives  except  insofar  as  it  ensures  that  those  pre-
rogatives  are  exercised  in  conformity  with  the  Consti-
tution.
Under  Marbury  v.  Madison,  5  U.S.  137  (1803),
the  courts  would  be  shirking  their  duty  if they  did any-
thing  less.  Amici  recognize  that  the  decision  in Solem
was  not  without  controversy.
Nevertheless,  it  is  consis-
tent  with  a  body  of case  law  that  dates  back  to  the  early
days  of  the  twentieth  century.
It accurately  reflects  both
the  constitutional  text  and  constitutional  history.
And,
despite  the  fears  of  its  detractors,  it  has  not  undermined
the
validity
sentencing
schemes  around  the  country.

of  legislative

constitutional

Although  rarely  invoked,  Solem  operates  as an  im-
portant  safety  valve  that  can  and  should  be  activated  in

extreme  cases.
believe  that  this  is another.

Solem  itself  was  one  such  case;  amici

A.

Solem’s  Requirement  Of  Proportionate
Sentencing  Is  Consistent  With  Nearly  A
Century  Of Eighth  Amendment  Law
The  defendant  in Solem  was  convicted  of uttering  a
"no  account"  check  for  $100  and,  without  enhancement,
would  have  faced  a  maximum  sentence  of  five  years  in
prison  and  a  $5000  fine.  463  U.S.  at  281.  Because  of a
prior  criminal  record,”  however,  he  was  treated  as  a
recidivist  under  South  Dakota  law  and  sentenced  to  life
imprisonment  without  parole."

In  overturning  that  sentence,  this  Court  relied  on  a
series  of  decisions,  beginning  with  Weems  v.  United
States,  217  U.S.  349  (1910).  The  Weems  Court  deemed
it  "a  precept  of justice  that  [the]  punishment  for  crime
should  be  graduated  and  proportioned  to  [the]  offense.”
Id.  at  367."  Applying  that  principle  to  the  facts  before
it,  the  Court  then  held  that  a  sentence  of  fifteen  years
hard  labor  in  chains  was  disproportionate  to  the  offense
of  falsifying  public  documents.  Part  of  the  Court’s  con-
cern  was  undoubtedly  based  on  the  method  of  punish-
ment,  which  bordered  on  the  torturous.  But  part  of  the

*  His  prior  criminal  record  consisted  of six  convictions  for  what  this
Court  characterized  as  "nonviolent  felonies,”  including  third  degree
burglary,  obtaining  moncy  under  false  pretenses,  and  driving  while  in-
toxicated.  463  U.S.  at  280.
'’  Like  the  defendant  in  this  case,  the  defendant  in  Solem  was  theo-
rctically  cligible  for  a  pardon  by the  governor.  463  U.S.  at  282.
The  principle  that  grossly  disproportionate  sentences  violate  the
Eighth  Amendment  was  first  articulated  by Justice  Ficld,  who  dis-
sented  in  O'Neill  v.  Vermont,  144  U.S.  323  (1892),  on  the  ground  that
the  Eighth  Amendment  barred  “all  punishments  which  by their  exces-
sive  length  or  severity  are  greatly  disproportioned  to  the  offences
charged.”  /d.  at  339-40.

~4

Court’s  concern  also  involved  the  length  of  punishment.
As  the  Court  noted  in  Weems,  a  fifteen  year  sentence
was  “cruel  in  its  excess  of  imprisonment."
217  U.S.  at
377.

Fifty  years  later,  the  Court  reached  a  similar  conclu-
sion  on  very  different  facts  in Robinson  v.  California,  370
U.S.  660  (1962).  The  defendant  in  Robinson  was  sen-
tenced  to  ninety  days  in  jail  for  being  a  drug  addict.”
The  Court  stressed,  however,  that  judgments  about  pro-
portionality  cannot  be  made  in  a  vacuum.  As  the  Court
explained:

(I]Jmprisonment  for  ninety  days  is
not,  in  the  abstract,  a  punishment
which  is  either  cruel  or  unusual.
But  the  question  cannot  be  con-
sidered  in  the  abstract.  Even  one
day  in  prison  would  be  cruel  and
unusual
the
punishment
"crime"  of having  a  common  cold.

for

Id.  at  667.

Following  Robinson,  the  question  of  proportionality
has  most  often  arisen  in  a  death  penalty  context.
In
Coker  v.  Georgia,  433  U.S.  at  592,  the  Court  held  that
the  "sentence  of death  is grossly  disproportionate  and  ex-
cessive  punishment  for  the  crime  of  rape."  Likewise,  in
Enmund  v.  Florida,  458  U.S.  782,  the  Court  held  that  the
crime  of felony  murder  cannot  be  punished  by death  un-
less  the  state  proves  beyond  a  reasonable  doubt  that  the

‘The  holding  in  Robinson
grounds.
Court’s  Eighth  Amendment  jurisprudence.  Solem,  463  U.S.  at  287.

defendant  intended  the  murder  as  well  as  the  predicate
felony.”

The  notion  of  proportionality  review  is  thus  well
entrenched  in  this  Court’s  Eighth  Amendment  jurispru-
dence.  Rummel  v.  Estelle,  445  U.S.  263,  represents  the
one  significant  exception  in the  past  eighty years.”  Rum-
mel,  of  course,  has  since  been  superseded  by  Solem.
Even  Rummel,  however,  did  not  reject  the  notion  of
proportionality  entirely.  To  the  contrary,  the  majority  in
Rummel  conceded  that  "a proportionality  principle  would
-

The  hypothetical  offered  by  the  Rummel  majority
was  obviously  intended  as  an  "extreme  example."
/d.
Nevertheless,  it  substantially  qualifies  the  otherwise
broad  language  of the  majority  opinion.  Once  the  con-
cession  is  made  that  some  prison  sentences  would  be
constitutionally  excessive,  it  is  no  longer  possible  to
maintain  that  "the  length  of  the  sentence  actually  im-
posed  is purely  a  matter  of  legislative  prerogative."  445
U.S.  at  274  (emphasis  added).

Furthermore,  the  Rummel  Court’s  reference  to  an
"extreme  example"  is, at  bottom,  not  all  that  much  differ-
ent  from  this  Court’s  consistent  admonition  in Solem  and

‘©  Numerous  opinions  of  this  Court  between  Robinson  and  Enmund
referred  to  the  fact  that  disproportionate  punishment  violates  the
Eighth  Amendment.
See,  e.g.,  Hutto  v.  Finney,  437  U.S.  678,  685
(1978);  Ingraham  v.  Wright,  430  U.S.  651,  667  (1977);  Gregg v.  Georgia,
428  US.  153,  171-72  (1976)(opinion  of Stewart,  Powell  and  Stevens,
JJ.);  Hutto  v.  Davis,  454  U.S.  370,  374  (1982)(per  curiam).  Some  of
those  cases  have  involved  the  death  penalty;  others  have  not.

In  Graham  v.  West  Virginia,  224  U.S.  616  (1912),  the  Court  rejected
a  proportionality  challenge  in  a  single  conclusory  sentence  that  most
plausibly  rested  on  the  fact  that  the  Eighth  Amendment  had  not  yet
been  applied  to  the  states.

elsewhere  that  prison  sentences  should  not  be  over-

turned  unless  they  are  "grossly"  disproportionate.
one  suggests  otherwise.  The  critical  question,  therefore,
is  what  criteria  to  use  in  determining  when  the  legisla-
ture  has  overstepped  its  constitutional  bounds.  Rummel
Solem  establishes  its  previously
proposes  no  criteria.
In  our  view,  that  test  is  con-
described  three  part  test.
sistent  with  history  and  the  constitutional  text.
It  also
provides  a  workable  standard  that  judges  can  and  have
applied  in  an  objective  fashion.

B.

The  Decision  In  Solem  Faithfully  Re-
flects  The  Constitutional  Text  And  Its
Relevant  History

The  Eighth  Amendment  provides:

"Excessive  bail
shall  not  be  required,  nor  excessive  fines  imposed,  nor
cruel  and  unusual  punishments  inflicted."  Obviously,  the
reference  to  “cruel  and  unusual"  punishment  is  less  spe-
cific  than  the  reference  to  “excessive  bail"  and  "excessive
fines."  But  despite  the  different  phraseology,  this  Court
has  had  no  difficulty  concluding  that  the  Amendment,  as
a  whole,  was  designed  to  impose  "parallel  limitations”  on
bails,  fines,  and  other  punishments.
See  Ingraham  v.
The  logic  of  that  position  is
Wright,  430  U.S.  at  664.
unassailable.  As  this  Court  noted  in Solem:

It  would  be  anomalous  indeed  if
the  lesser  punishment  of  a  fine
and  the  greater  punishment  of
death  were  both  subject  to  pro-
portionality  analysis,  but  the  inter-
mediate
of  prison
were  not.

punishment

463  US.  at  289.

At  a  minimum,  the  burden  should  be  placed  on
those  who  advocate  such  a  counterintuitive  interpretation
of  the  constitutional  text  to  establish  its  historical  basis.

In  fact,  the  history  surveyed  in  Solem  and  elsewhere
demonstrates  that  the  notion  of  proportionality  was
"deeply  rooted  and  frequently  repeated"  in  the  common
law  by 1791.  463  U.S.  at  284.

This  principle  stems  at  least  as  far  back  as  the  Mag-
na  Carta,  which  established  the  right  against  excessive
“amercements"  or  fines  in  1215.
Jd."  As  prison  sen-
tences  became  more  common  in  later  years,  the  English
courts  were  equally  insistent  that  "imprisonment  ought
always  to  be  according  to  the  quality  of  the  offence."
Hodges  v.  Humkin,  80  Eng.Rep.  1015,  1016  (KB  1615).
In  1689,  the  English  Bill  of Rights  adopted  the  reference
to  “cruel  and  unusual"  punishment  that  was  repeated  ver-
batim  by the  framers  of  the  Eighth  Amendment.  Only
three  months  later,  that  language  was  interpreted  by the
House  of  Lords,  which  declared  that  a  "fine  of  thirty
thousand  pounds,  imposed  by the  court  of  King’s  Bench
upon  the  earl  of  Devon  was  excessive  and  exorbitant,
against  magna  charta,  the  common  right  of  the  subject,
and  the  law  of the  land."  Earl  of Devon’s  Case,  11  State
Tr.  133,  136  (1689),  quoted  in Solem,  463  U.S.  at  285."

'*  Chapter  20 of the  Magna  Carta  provided  that  "a  free  man  shall  not
be  amerced  for a trivial  offense,  except  in  accordance  with  the  degree
of the  offense  ...  ."  Solem,  463  U.S.  at  284  n.9.  See  also  Granucci,
“Nor  Cruel  and  Unusual  Punishments  Inflicted’:  The  Original  Mean-
ing,” 57 Cal.  Law  Rev.  839,  844  (1969).

In  addition,  the  Enlightenment  philosophers  read  by  the  framers
argued  in  favor  of  proportionate  sentencing,  including  Montesquicu
and  Beccaria.
See  Schwartz  and  Wishingard,  "The  Eighth  Amend-
ment,  Beccaria,  and  the  Enlightenment:  An  Historical  Justification
for  the  Weems  v.  U.S.  Excessive  Punishment  Doctrine,"  24  Buffalo
Law  Rev.  783,  811  (1975).
The  influence  of  Montesquieu  and
Beccaria  on  the  framers  is  generally  discussed  in  B.  Bailyn,  The
Ideological  Origins  of the  American  Revolution  (1967).
See  also
Uliman  v.  United  States,  330  U.S.  442,  450  (1956)(Douglas,  J.
dissenting)("the  Italian  jurist  Beccaria  and  his  French  and  English
followers  influenced  American  thought  in  the  critical  years  following
our  Revolution”).

10

1]

The  principle  of  proportionality  was  also  reflected
in  the  colonial  laws,  which  served  as  the  source  of  many
constitutional  provisions.  The  Maryland  Charter  of  1632,
for  example,  authorized  penalties  if "the  Quality  of  the
offence  require  it."  See  Perry,  Sources  of Our  Liberties
107  (1959).
The  Massachusetts  Body  of  Liberties  of
1641  allowed  whipping  only  if the  "crime  be  very  shame-
full.".
Jd.  And  the  Charter  of  Rhode  Island  (1663)  ex-
plicitly  extended  proportionality  to  prison  sentences,
requiring  "the  imposing  of  lawfull  and  reasonable  ffynes,
mulcts,  and  imprisonments  ...."  /d.  at  173.

Following  independence,  numerous  state  constitu-
tions  adopted  a  similar  view.  The  Pennsylvania  Consti-
tution  of  1776  called  for  a  revision  of  the  penal  system
to  make  “punishments  in  some  cases  less  sanguinary  and
in  general  more  proportionate  to  the  crime."
Pa.Const.
§38  (1776).  The  South  Carolina  Constitution  also  advo-
cated  reform  to  make  punishments  "more  proportionate
to  the  crime."
And  the  New
S.C.Const.  §XL  (1776).
Hampshire  Constitution  spelled  out  at  length  the  ration-
ale  for  proportionate  punishment:

All  penalties  ought  to  be  propor-
tioned  to  the  nature  of  the  of-
No  wise  legisiature  will
fense.
affix  the  same  punishment  to  the
crimes  of  theft,  forgery,  and  the
like,  which  they  do  to  those  of
murder  and  treason;  where  the
same  undistinguishing  severity  is
exerted  against  all  offenses,  the
people  are  led  to  forget  the  real
distinction
in  the  crimes  them-
selves,  and  to  commit  the  most
flagrant  with  as  little  compunction
as  they  do  those  of  the  lightest
eae

N.H.Const.,  Bill  of Rights,  §18  (1783).”

There  is  no  reason  to  believe  that  the  framers  of
the  Eighth  Amendment  intended  to  break  from  this  his-
torical  tradition  when  they adopted  the  constitutional  bar
on  "cruel  and  unusual"  punishments.”

.

®  See generally  Schwartz  and  Wisingard,  supra  n.19,  at  819-26.

*  The  dissenters  in Solem  argued  that  the  “cruel  and  unusual”  punish-
ment  clause  referred  only  to  “prohibiting  the  kind  of  torture  meted
out  during  the  reign  of the  Stuarts.”  463  U.S.  at  312.  Proponents  of
this  view  have  principally  relied  on  three  statements  by the  framers.
See,  e.g.,  Granucci  at  840-42.  In  the  Massachusetts  Ratifying  Conven-
tion  in June  1788,  Abraham  Holmes  protested  that  "there  is no  consti-
tutional  check  of  punishments  but  that  racks  and  gibbets  may  be
amongst  the  most  mild  instruments  of their  discipline.”  2 J.  Elliot,
The  Debates  In  the  Several  State  Conventions  on  the  Adoption  of the
Federal  Constitution  111  (2d ed.  1881).  At  the  Virginia  Ratifying  Con-
“What  has  distin-
vention  in  June  1788,  Patrick  Henry  declared:
That  they  would  not  admit  of  tortures,  or
guished  our  ancestors?
crucl  and  barbarous  punishments.”  3 J.  Elliot,  supra,  at  447-48.
In
the  first  Congress  in  1789,  Mr.  Livermore  objected  to  the  Eighth
Amendment  on  the  grounds  that  “it  is sometimes  necessary  to  hang  a
man,  villains  often  deserve  whipping,  and  perhaps  having  their  cars
cut  off  ...."  1 Annals  of Cong.  782-83  (1789).

As  Justice  Brennan  noted  in  his  concurring  opinion  in  Furman  vy.
Georgia,  408  U.S.  238,  260  (1972),"[i]t  does  not  follow  [from  these
three  statements]  that  the  framers  were  exclusively  concerned  with
prohibiting  torturous  punishments,”  only  that  “torturous  punishments”
were  among  their  concerns.
In  any  event,  three  isolated  statements
scarcely  diminish  from  the  general  thrust  of the  historical  evidence.

12

13

Court’s  ruling  in  Solem,  not  one  prison  sentence  has
been  reversed  in  the  federal  courts  on  the  basis  of  the
Solem  standard.”

Thus,  the  federal  courts  have  not  “bald[ly]  substi-
tut[ed]"  their  own  “subjective  moral  values  for those  of
/d.  at  314  (Burger,  CJ.,  dissenting).
the  legislature."
Nor  have  the  courts  interpreted  Solem  as  a  general  li-
cense  to  second  guess  the  sentencing  judgment  of elected
legislatures.  Rather,  the  federal  courts  have  demonstrat-
ed  time  and  again  that  they  are  “capable  of  applying
Eighth  Amendment  law  to  disproportionate  noncapital
sentences  with  a  high  degree  of  sensitivity  to  principles
of federalism  and  state  autonomy."  Rummel,  445  U.S.  at
306  (Powell,  J., dissenting).

In  short,  Solem  established  an  Eighth  Amendment
standard  that  reflects  constitutional  values  and  that
works  in  practice.  There  is no  need  either  to  reexamine
There  is,  unfortunately,  a  need  to
or  to  question  it.
apply  it.

Il.  PETITIONER’S  SENTENCE  TO  LIFE  IMPRISON-
MENT  WITHOUT  PAROLE  SHOULD  NOT  BE
UPHELD  UNDER  THE  SOLEM  STANDARDS
Petitioner  was  convicted  for  possession  of  672.5
grams  of  cocaine.  Under  Michigan  law,  the  trial  judge
had  no  choice  but  to  sentence  him  to  life  in prison.  This
sentence  did  not  depend  on a finding  that  petitioner  en-
gaged  in  the  manufacture,  sale,  or  distribution  of narcot-
Petitioner  was  not  even  charged  with  such  crimes.
ics.

*  This  conclusion  is based  upon  review  of all  published  federal  court
opinions  mentioning  Solem  (116  appellate  and  35  district  court  deci-
sions).  Amici  have  been  able  to  find  only  one  case  in  which  Solem
was  cited  by a  district  court  to  overturn  a  criminal  sentence  and  that
decision  was  later  reversed  on  appeal.  See  Sutton  v.  State  of Md., 681
F.Supp.  291  (D.Md.  1988),  rev'd  en  banc,  886  F.2d  708  (4th  Cir.  1989).

i

14

It did  not  depend  on a finding  that  petitioner  is a  recidi-
vist,  and  no  such  finding  was  made.  Nor  did  it contem-
plate  petitioner’s  early  release  on  parole.  To  the  contra-
ry,  parole  is prohibited  under  these  circumstances.

The  severity  of  petitioner’s  sentence,  therefore,  is
not  a  matter  for  serious  dispute.
In  particular,  the  ab-
sence  of  even  the  possibility  of  parole  means  that  the
petitioner  here,  like  the  defendant  in  Solem,  can  expect
to  "spend  the  rest  of  his  life  in  the  state  penitentiary."
463  U.S.  at  297.”  And  while  the  petitioner  here,  like  the
defendant  in  Solem,  can  seek  commutation  of  his  sen-
tence,

[t]he  possibility  of commutation  is
nothing  more  than  hope  for  "an
ad  hoc  exercise  of  clemency."
It
is  little  different  from  the  possi-
bility  of  executive  clemency  that
exists  in  every  case  in  which  a
defendant  challenges  his  sentence
under  the  Eighth  Amendment.
Recognition  of  such  a  bare  possi-
bility  would  make  judicial  review
under
Eighth  Amendment
the
meaningless.

Id.  at  303.

The  fact  that  petitioner  was  not  sentenced  as  a  re-
cidivist  is  also  relevant  in  weighing  the  punishment  he
received.  As  this  Court  explained  in Rummel,  the  state’s
interest  in  punishing  a  recidivist  is  different  than  its
interest  in  punishing a first  time  offender:

*  By contrast,  the  defendant  in  Rummel  was  cligible  for  parole  after
serving  twelve  years  of his  life  sentence,  a  fact  that  this  Court  empha-
sized  in Solem.  463  U.S.  at  302.

15

[A]  recidivist  [under  Texas  law]
must  twice  demonstrate  that  con-
viction  and  actual  imprisonment
do  not  deter  him  from  returning
to  crime  once
he  is  released

been  both  graphically  informed  of
the  consequences  of  lawlessness
and  given  an  opportunity  to  re-

a  societal  decision  that  when  such

Amici  do  not  suggest  that  petitioner’s  crime  is  in
any  way  comparable  to  the  petty  thefts  at  issue  in  Rum-
mel  and  Solem.  It  is  not.
Petitioner  was  convicted  of  a
serious  offense.  Nevertheless,  the  special  concerns  asso-
ciated  with  recidivism  cannot  be  used  to  justify  the  pun-
ishment  imposed  in  this  case.
Absent  the  enhancing
factor  of  recidivism,  petitioner’s  sentence  is  grossly  dis-
proportionate  both  to  the  range  of sentences  imposed  by
Michigan  for  other  serious  crimes  and  to  the  sentence
that  petitioner  would  have  received  if convicted  of  the
Same  crime  in virtually  every  other  jurisdiction.

A.  Michigan’s  Sentencing  Structure

Life  imprisonment  without  parole  is the  most  severe
sentence  that  any  defendant  can  receive  in  the  state  of

16

Michigan  for  any  crime.”
In  petitioner’s  case,  it was  a
mandatory  sentence.”  Only  two  other  crimes  in  Michi-
gan  carry  a  similar  penalty:
first  degree  murder,”  and
the  manufacture  or  sale  of  more  than  650  grams  of  a
Schedule  1 or  2 drug.”  By contrast,  second  degree  mur-
der,  kidnapping,  and  hostage-taking  by prisoners  carry  a
minimum  sentence  of  “life  or  any  term  of years.”  As-
sault  with  intent  to  maim  is  punishable  by ten  years  in
prison.”  Assault  with  a  dangerous  weapon  is punishable
by four  years  in prison.”

Amici  fully  appreciate  the  difficulty  of  placing  a
moral  and  penal  value  on  different  crimes.  At  the  same
time,  the  Court’s  Eighth  Amendment  cases  have  consis-
tently  recognized  a  bright  line  between  premeditated
murder  and  all  other  offenses.  For  example,  in  Coker  v.
Georgia,  433  U.S.  584,  the  Court  held  that  rape  could
not  be  punished  by the  death  penalty.  The  Court’s  hold-
ing was  clearly  not  intended  to  "discount  the  seriousness
of  rape,"  id.  at  597,  just  as  amici  do  not  discount  the
seriousness  of petitioner’s  conviction  in  this  case.  Never-

*  Michigan  does  not  have  a  death  penalty.

*  See  n.2, supra.

*  Mich.Comp.  Laws  Ann.  § 750.316.

””  Mich.Comp.  Laws  Ann.  § 333.7401.
*  Mich.Comp.  Laws  Ann.  § §750.31,  750.349,  750.349a,  The  Michigan
courts  have  interpreted  the  phrase  “life  or  any  term  of years”  to  mean
a  mandatory  minimum  of  one  year  and  one  day  imprisonment.
People  v.  West,  317  N.W.2d  261  (1982);  People  v.  Harper,  269  N.W.2d
470  (1979).
Furthermore,  Michigan  law  provides  that  a  prisoner
"under  sentence  for  life  or  for  a  term  of years”  is eligible  for  parole
after  ten  years.  Mich.Comp.  Laws  Ann.  §791.234(4).

*  Mich.Comp.  Laws  Ann.  § 750.86.

*  Mich.Comp.  Laws  Ann.  § 750.82.

17

theless,  the  Court  insisted  that  a  distinction  between
rape  and  murder  was  appropriate:

Rape  is  without  doubt  deserving
of  serious  punishment,  but  in
terms  of  moral  depravity  and  the
injury  to  the  person  and  the  pub-
lic,  it  does  not  compare  with
murder,  which  does  involve  the
unjustified  taking  of human  life.

Id.  at  598.

A  similar  rationale  applies  to  the  facts  of  this  case.
Indeed,  any  other  result  would  be  inconsistent  with  the
very  premise  of  proportionate  sentencing  and,  conse-
quently,  inconsistent  with  the  demands  of  the  Eighth
Amendment.”

B.  The  Practice  In  Other  Jurisdictions

For  the  Court’s  convenience,  amici  have  compiled  a
list  of  the  maximum  sentence  that  petitioner  could  have
received  had  he  been  tried  for  the  same  crime  by  any
other  state.  See  Appendix  la-Sa.”  That  list  makes  clear
that  Michigan’s  mandatory  sentence  of life  imprisonment
It  is,  more-
without  parole  is the  harshest  in  the  nation.

"There  is  another  significant  disproportionality  inherent  in  Michi-
gan’s  sentencing  scheme.  Michigan  has  chosen  to  punish  simple  pos-
session  of  narcotics  as  severely  as  it  punishes  their  manufacture  or
This  produces  two  complementary  results,
sale  (above  50  grams).
neither  of which  can  be justified  in logic  or  law.  It eliminates  any  dis-
incentive  against  drug sales  while  relieving  the  state  of the  necessity  of
proving  an  intent  to  sell.

*  It  is  important  to  note  that  the  Appendix  lists  the  maximum
In  Michigan,  of  course,  the
possible  sentence  for  each  jurisdiction.
maximum  sentence  is  also  the  mandatory  sentence.
The  mandatory
nature  of  Michigna’s  sentencing  scheme  simply  ignores  "the  need  for
individualized  sentencing  decisions”  that  the  Court  has  referred  to  in
its  Eighth  Amendment  opinions.  See  Solem,  463  U.S.  at  290  n.17.

over,  the  harshest  by a  considerable  margin.  Only  Ala-
bama  imposes  a  similar  punishment.  In  more  than  half
the  states,  petitioner  could  not  have  been  sentenced  to
more  than  ten  years  in jail  and  in  at  least  two  states  his
maximum  sentence  would  have  been  six  months.  Thus,
as  in  Solem,  the  defendant  in  this  case  "could  not  have
received  such  a  severe  sentence  in  48  of  the  50  States."
463  US.  at  299.

The  comparison  with  federal  law  is even  more  illu-
minating.
Had  petitioner  been  tried  for  possession  of
672.5  grains  of  cocaine  in  federal  court,  he  could  not
have  been  sentenced  to  more  than  one  year  in  jail,  21
U.S.C.  §844(a),  and  even  that  sentence  is  discretionary.
As  a  first  offender,  petitioner  could  also  have  been
placed  on  probation  and,  upon  successful  completion  of
his  probationary  term,  all  charges  against  him  would
have  been  dropped.  21  U.S.C.  §844(b).*

In  short,  petitioner  would  probably  not  be  in  jail
today  had  he  been  prosecuted  by  the  federal  govern-
ment.  Because  he  was  prosecuted  by the  state  of Michi-
gan,  he  will  never  get  out  of jail for  the  remainder  of his
life.
These  are  not  simply  differences  in  degree;  they
are  differences  in  kind.
It  is  one  thing  to  say  that  the
states  can  act  as  laboratories  in addressing  difficult  social
issues.
It  is another  thing  to  say  that  one  or  two  states
can  impose  criminal  punishments  that  are  so  out  of line
with  the  national  consensus.
Such  radical  disparities
cannot  and  should  not  be  upheld  under  the  Eighth
Amendment.

*  Under  the  federal  sentencing  guidelines,  a  first  offender  found
guilty  of the  more  serious  crime  of possession  with  intent  to  distribute
672.5  grams  of cocaine,  20  U.S.C.  §841(b)(1)(B),  would  still  face  only
63-78  months  in jail.

19

CONCLUSION

For  the  reasons  stated  herein,  the  decision  below

should  be  reversed.

No.  89-7272

In The
Supreme  Court  of the United  States
October  Term,  1990

RONALD  HARMELIN,

Petitioner,

v.

STATE  OF  MICHIGAN,

Respondent.

ON WRIT  OF CERTIORARI
TO THE  SUPREME  COURT
OF  MICHIGAN

BRIEF  AMICUS  CURIAE  OF
THE  NATIONAL  DISTRICT
ATTORNEYS  ASSOCIATION,
IN  SUPPORT  OF THE  RESPONDENT.

This  brief  is filed  pursuant  to  the  Rules  of the  United
States  Supreme  Court.  Consent  to  file  has  been  granted
by Carla  J. Johnson,  Esq.,  Counsel  for the  Petitioner,  and
Hon.  Richard  Thompson,  Prosecuting  Attorney,  Oakland
County,  Michigan,  Counsel  for  Respondent.  A  Joint
Consent  of the  parties  has  been  filed  with  the  Clerk  of
this  Court.

\ ——__—_

2

INTEREST  OF AMICUS  CURIAE

The  National  District  Attorneys  Association,  Inc.
(NDAA),  is  a  nonprofit  corporation  and  the  sole
national  organization  representing  state  and  local
prosecuting  attorneys  in America.  Since  its founding  in
1950,  NDAA’s  programs  of  education,  training,
publications,  and  amicus  curiae  activity  have  carried  out
its  guiding  purpose  of  reforming  the  criminal  justice
system  for the  benefit  of all of our  citizens.

In  this  case  NDAA  speaks  as  the  representative  of
locally  elected  prosecuting  attorneys  in  America.  Our
over  6,500  members  are  drawn  from  the  body  of elected
and  appointed  prosecutors  from  every  state  in  the
United  States.  Among  the  programs  operated  by NDAA
for  local  prosecutors  is the  National  Drug  Prosecution
Center,  Alexandria,  Virginia,  which  seeks  to  improve  the
prosecution  function  in  drug  cases.  The  statute  in
question,  and  its application  in this  case,  is viewed  by the
Nation’s  prosecutors  as  indispensable  to  the  effective
prosecution  of drug traffickers.

3

ARGUMENT

A  MANDATORY  SENTENCE  OF  LIFE  IMPRISON-
MENT  FOR  CONVICTION  OF  POSSESSION  OF
MORE  THAN  650  GRAMS  OF  COCAINE  DOES  NOT
VIOLATE  THE  CRUEL  AND  UNUSUAL  PUNISH-
MENT  CLAUSE  OF  THE  EIGHTH  AMEND-
MENT.

In this  case  the  Michigan  Court  of Appeals  held,  inter
alia,  that  a mandatory  sentence  of life  imprisonment  for
a  conviction  of possession  of more  than  650  grams  of
cocaine  did  not  violate  the  Cruel  and  Unusual
Punishment  Clause  of the  Eighth  Amendment.  People  v.
Harmelin,  176  Mich.  App.  525,  440  N.W.2d  75  (Mich.
App.  1989), leave  to appeal den.,  434  Mich.  863  (1990).

Amicus  will  not  duplicate  the  case  law  analysis
presented  by the  Respondent  in this  case,  although  we
agree  with  it. Instead,  we  concentrate  primarily  on  issues
of policy  that  concern  local  prosecutors  and  we  speak
|
from  a national  perspective.

Although  amicus  believes  that  this  Court’s  decision  in
Solem  v.  Helm,  463  U.S.  277,  103  S. Ct.  3001  (1983),  as
requiring  a  proportionality  review  for  legislatively
determined  terms  of imprisonment,  was  wrongly  decided,
it should  be clear  in this  case  that  the  sentence  imposed
does  not  offend  the  rule  adopted  in  Solem.  Solem
identified  three  factors  for  reviewing  courts  to  consider
in  passing  on  the  proportionality  issue  in terms  of the
Eighth  Amendment:

(1) The  gravity  of the  offense  and  the  harshness  of the

punishment;

(2) The  sentences  imposed  on  other  defendants  in the

same  jurisdiction;  and

(3) The  sentence  imposed  for  commission  of the  same

crime  in other jurisdictions.

To  begin  with,  this  Court  has  made  clear  in Mistretta
v.  United  States,  _U.S.___,  109  S. Ct.  647  (1989),  that
in non-capital  cases  a proportionality  review  need  not
focus  on  the  particular  offender  (his  background,
criminal  record,  prospects  for  rehabilitation,  etc.),  but
need  only  address  the  appropriateness  of the  legislative
choice  in  establishing  a  penalty  (and  the  fact  that  the
choice  has  been  made  by the  legislature,  with  reduced
discretion  in the judiciary,  does  not  violate  separation  of
powers  principles).  Even  if the  facts  concerning  the
particular  offender  were  relevant  to  a  proportionality
analysis  in  the  instant  case,  however,  the  Petitioner
would  be an  entirely  appropriate  subject  for  the  sentence
received.  The  record  shows  that  he  not  only possessed
over  650  grams  of  cocaine,  but  that  some  of  it  was
packaged  for  resale,  he  possessed  additional  narcotics
and  paraphernalia,  and  had  a  beeper  with  coded
instructions  to  service  his  narcotics  clientele.  He  was,  in
short,  the  typical  modern  drug  dealer,  well-equipped  to
ply  his  trade.  The  collective  experience  of  our
membership  with  the  havoc  wreaked  upon  Society  by
drug  traffickers  argues  against  any  contention  that  the
sentence
a
inappropriate
imposed  was
constitutional  sense  for this particular  defendant.

in

as

We  believe  that  the  proportionality  review  required
under  Solem  was  effectively  performed  by the  Sixth
Circuit  Court  of Appeals  in Young  v.  Miller,  883  F.2d
1276  (6th Cir.  1989),  when  it rejected  a similar  argument
made  by a defendant  convicted  under  a similar  statute
for possession  with  intent  to  deliver  650 grams  or  more
of  heroin.  Although  the  court  in  Young  v.  Miller
considered  the  particular  circumstances  of the  defendant
in  that  case,  its  analysis  of the  three  part  Solem  test  as

5

applicable  to  the  statute  is correct.  It concluded  that
"[h]aving  analyzed  Young’s  sentence  under  each  of the
Solem  factors,  we  conclude  that  her  sentence  is not  so
grossly  disproportionate  to  her  crimes  as  to  violate  the
Eighth  Amendment."  883  F.2d  at  1285.

Other  courts  have  agreed  that  similar  and  related
mandatory  penalty  statutes  for  drug  possessors  and
traffickers  were  appropriate  and  constitutional  legislative
choices,  and  were  properly  applied  to  the  defendants  in
those  cases.  See,  e.g.,  United  States  v.  Brown,  859  F.2d  974
(D.C.  Cir.  1988);  United  States  v.  Cook,  859  F.2d  777  (9th
Cir.  1988);  United  States  v.  Zavala-Serra,  853  F.2d  1512
(9th  Cir.  1988);  United  States  v> Murillo-Guzman,  845
F.2d  314  (11th  Cir.  1988).  Perhaps  the  matter  was  put
best  by the  Fifth  Circuit  Court  of Appeals  in Terrabonne
v.  Butler,  848  F.2d  500  (Sth Cir.  1988),  when  the  court,  in
considering  a similar  statute,  noted:

Except  in rare  cases,  the  murderer’s  red  hand
falls  on  one  victim  only,  however  grim  the
blow;  but  the  foul  hand  of the  drug  dealer
blights  life  after  life  and,  like  the  vampire  of
fable,  creates  others  in its owner’s  evil  image--
others  who  create  others  still,  across  our  land
and  down  our  generations,  sparing  not  even
the unborn.
848  F.2d  at 504.

Amicus  submits  that  the  legislative  choice  made  by
the  State  of Michigan  and  applied  in this  case  is not  only
constitutionally  sound  but good public policy as  well.  It is
a  choice  geared  to  adequately  meeting  the  narcotics
trafficking  problem  of  the  1990’s,  and  a  choice  that
prosecutors  who  are  in the  front  lines  of dealing  with  this
problem  support.  Many  states  have  adopted  various
forms  of mandatory  and  discretionary  life  term  penalties

6

for  sale,  trafficking,  and  possession  of specified  large
quantities  of narcotics.  These  statutes  are  collected  in
the  Appendix  to  this  brief.  Amicus  curiae  expects  that  as
the  success  of these  statutes  in effectively  dealing  with
the  narcotics  problem  becomes  apparent,  many  more
states  will  adopt  similar  statutes.  We  respectfully  urge
this  Court  to  give  a  clear  signal  to  the  state  legislatures
that  this  needed  response  to  the  scourge  of  drug
trafficking  is constitutionally  permissible.

of  over

650  grams

of  cocaine

For  those  who  argue  that  a  life  sentence  for
is
possession
constitutionally  disproportionate  to  the  crime,  we  would
answer  that  prosecutors  are  well  aware  of  the
significance  of possession  of that  amount  of narcotics
and  its  devastating  impact  upon  Society.  As  noted  by
Donald  Reisig,  Director,  State  of Michigan  Office  of
Drug  Agencies:  "I think  we  have  to  articulate  what  650
grams  is.  It’s  a  pound  and  a  half,  roughly.  That’s  not  a
small  quantity.  [...]  It’s  not  20  tons  in  a  warehouse.  I
understand  that.  But  a pound  and a half  of cocaine  can
do  immeasurable  damage  to  a  society."  Castine,  "Drug
Czar  Says  Law  Fits  the  Crime,"  The  Free  Press  (Detroit,
MI,  March  12,  1990,  p. A4).

As  prosecutors  with  a  national  perspective,  amicus
curiae  have  seen  the effects  of similar  large  quantities  of
cocaine  and  related  narcotics  upon  our  communities,
particularly  our  youth.  We  respectfully  submit  that  the
effect  of  such  a  quantity  of  narcotics  is  easily  more
devastating  than  a  gun  in the  hand  of a  murderer.  The
legislative  choice  made  by the  State  of Michigan,  and  its
application  in  this  case,  does  not  offend  the  con-
stitutional  mandate  of the  Eighth  Amendment.

7

CONCLUSION

Amicus  curiae  submits  that  the judgment  of the  court
below  should  be  affirmed  on  the  basis  of the  law  and
sound  judicial  policy.

Respectfully  submitted,

RICHARD  P.  IEYOUB,  ESQ.

APPENDIX

Life  Imprisonment  for Drug Offenses  --
A Selection  of State  Statutes.

The  statutory  authority for imposition  of life sentences  is
not  uncommon.  For  example,  Alabama,  Louisiana,
Michigan,  and  Oklahoma  impose  mandatory  life sentences
for particular  drug offenses.  (See  attached  list A).  Several
other  states  give courts  discretion  to  impose  life sentences  in
specified  related  circumstances.  (See attached  list B).

A
Mandatory  Life  Imprisonment
for
Specified  Drug Offenses

Alabama

ALA.  CODE  13A-12-201  to  13A-12-270

(Supp.  1989).

13A-12-231
(1)(d)  "Any  person  who  knowingly  sells,
manufactures,  delivers,  or  brings  into  this  state,
or  who  is knowingly  in  actual  or  constructive
possession  of"  1,000  pounds  or  more  of cannabis
"shall  be  sentenced  to  a  mandatory  term  of
imprisonment  of life without  parole."

(2)(d)
"Any  person  who  knowingly  sells,
manufactures,  delivers,  or  brings  into  this  state,
or  who  is in actual  or  constructive  possession  of"
10  kilos  or  more  of "cocaine  or  of any  mixture
containing  cocaine"  [...] "shall  be  sentenced  to  a
mandatory  term  of imprisonment  of life without
parole."

(3)(d)  "Any  person  who  knowingly  sells,
manufactures,  delivers,  or  brings  into  this  state,

App.-2

or  who  is knowingly  in  actual  or  constructive
possession  of"  56  grams  or  more  of  "any
morphine,  opium,  or  any  salt,  isomer,  or  salt  of
an  isomer  thereof,  including  heroin"  [...] "shall  be
sentenced  to  a mandatory  term  of imprisonment
of life without  parole."

(4)(d)  “Any  person  who  knowingly  sells,
manufactures,  delivers,  or  brings  into  this  state,
or  who  is knowingly  in  actual  or  constructive
possession  of"  100,000  capsules  or  more  of
"methaqualone"  [...]  "shall  be  sentenced  to  a
mandatory  term  of imprisonment  of life without
parole."

(5)(d)  “Any  person  who  knowingly  sells,
manufactures,  delivers,  or  brings  into  this  state,
or  who  is knowingly  in  actual  or  constructive
possession  of"  10,000  pills  or  capsules  of
hydromorphane  "shall  be  sentenced  to  a
mandatory  term  of imprisonment  of life without
parole."

(6)(d)  "Any  person  who  knowingly  sells,
manufactures,  delivers,  or  brings  into  this  state,
or  who  is knowingly  in  actual  or  constructive

methylenediaxy  amphetamine  or  of any  mixture
containing  3,4  -methylenediaxy  amphetamine"
[...] "shall  be  sentenced  to  a mandatory  term  of

case  of  the  Respondent.

OF  THE  AMICUS
INTEREST

Amicus  is  the  Prosecuting  Attorneys

of  Association  of  Michigan,  to  which  all

elected  Prosecuting  Attorneys  in  the

state  are  members.

Charged  with  the

responsibility  of  litigating  all  criminal

violations,

including  drug  offenses,

in

their  respective  counties,  the  outcome  of

the  current  litigation  is  a  matter  of

great  interest  to  all  prosecuting

attorneys,  and  thereby  the  Association.

The  historical  background  informing

the  meaning  of  the  phrase  "cruel  and

unusual  punishment"  has  been  explored  in

depth  by  various  members  of  the  Court,

and  will  not  be  belabored  here.

Rather,

the  beginning  of  the  inquiry  will  be

Rummel  v  Estelle,  445  US  263,  63  L  Ed  2d

382,  100  S  Ct  1133  (1980),  the  first  case

to  consider  the  question  of  whether  the

length  of  a  term  of  imprisonment  alone

can  be  violative  of  the  Eight  Amendment

as  "cruel  and  unusual."

In  Rummel  the

defendant  was  sentenced  to  life  in  prison

as  an  habitual  offender  upon  his

conviction  of  obtaining  $120.75  by  false

pretenses,  having  been  previously

convicted  of  fraudulent  use  of  a  credit

card  to  obtain  $80  worth  of  goods  or

services,

a  felony,

and  also  passing  a

forged  check  in  the  amount  of

©  @«

$28.36,

also  a  felony.

Rummel's  life

sentence  did  not  preclude  parole.

The

Third  Circuit,  sitting  en  banc,  affirmed

the  denial  of  habeas  relief  sought  on

Eighth  Amendment  grounds,  relying  largely

on  the  fact  that  Rummel  would  be  eligible

for  parole  within  12  years  of

confinement.

This  Covrt  affirmed.

The  Court  observed  that  death

penalty  cases  are  different  in  kind  from

all  other  sentences,  and  that  thus  death

penalty  Eighth  Amendment  jurisprudence  is

of  limited  utility  in  analyzing  an  Eighth

Amendment  imprisonment  claim.

The  Court

also  noted  that  it  was  critical  that  the

"Court's  Eighth  Amendment  judgments

should  neither  be  nor  appear  to  be  nerely

the  subjective  views  of  individual

Justices."

445  US  at  275.

Although

undertaking  what  might  be  called  a

limited  proportionality  review,  the  Court

The  question  involved  in

Stanford  was  "whether  the  imposition

was  left  to  whether  capital  punishment  in

the  circumstances  of  the  case  was

contrary  to  “evolving  standards  of

decency  that  mark  the  progress  of  a

maturing  society."

106  L  Ed  2d  at

317-318.

But  how  is  such  an  inguiry  to

be  carried  out?

What  is  the  measure?

What  informs  the  judgment?

What  tools

-  15  =-

-  16  =

does  a  court  have  to  make  it?

What

enables  a  court  to  overrule  society's

expression  of  its  "standard  of  decency,"

communicated  through  statute,  imposing  a

different  standard,  which  is  also

supposed  to  be  society's  standard  and  not

the  court's,  especially  given  that

"'tthose  institutions  which  the

Constitution  is  supposed  to  limit'

include  the  Court  itself"?  106  L  Ed  2d  at

324.

The  Court  in  Stanford  canvassed  the

law  of  other  jurisdictions  (in  the  United

States,

it  being  American  society  which

is  the  concern  of  the  Constitution)  and

even  purely  scientific  evidence  as  an

"available  weapon"  in  the  argument,

for

the  role  of  the  Court,  held  the

plurality,  is  not  to  create  an  evolving

standard  of  decency  (that  is,  not  to  be

an  agent  of  evolution  in  one  direction  or

the  other),  but  to  discover  or  identify

it--not  what  it  should  be,  but  what  it

is.

The  plurality  disputed  the

contention  of  the  dissent  that  it  was

simply  leaving  the  meaning  of  the  Eighth

Amendment  to  the  political  majority.

Rather,

said  the  plurality,  though  the

Court  had  cut  loose  from  history  in  its

Eighth  Amendment  jurisprudence,  that

action  did  not  leave  the  Court

punishment.

872

F  2d  889

(CA  9,  1989).

But  Solem

makes  no  allowances  for  standards  of

decency  requiring  harsher  treatment  of

certain  offenders,  assuming  that  a

"mature"  society  is  one  which  treats

offenders  less  severely  than  it  had

(apparently  when  immature)  in  the  past.

-  23  -

The  societal  ratchet  has  also  turned
in  a  manner  similar  to  that  of  drunken
driving  with  regard  to  narcotics,  and  it
may  be  that  Michigan  is  one  of  the
leaders  in  this  regard  (a  proportionality
review  will  be  conducted  infra).
Amicus
submits  that  the  State  has  a  right  to  be
a  leader,
and  that  a  State  legislature
may  play  a  role  in  the  Shaping  of
contemporary  standards  of  decency
throughout  the  Nation.

Though  Michigan's
legislature  may  have  set  a  harsh  penalty,
the  penalty  is  certainly  not  one  against
which  "society  has  set  its  face";  indeed,
Society,  in  the  Sense  of  other
jurisdictions,  is  moving  in  the  same
direction  (Michigan  is  truly  a  leader,
and  not  a  deviant,  in  this  regard).
The
differences  among  jurisdictions  turn
principally  upon  the  amount  of  substance
which  triggers  the  most  severe
penalties--the  federal  jurisdiction,  in

-  24  -

fact,  is  moving  toward  a  possible  death
penalty  for  certain  drug  offenses

(see

brief  of  Respondent).

(1910),

30  S  Ct  544

Where  the  only  issue  is  the  length
of  punishment,  and  not  the  mode  (see
Weems  v  United  States,  217  US  349,  54  L
Ed  793,

and  death
penalty  cases)  this  Court  ought  to
abandon  proportionality  review,  as  our
Constitution  "'is  made  for  people  of
fundamentally  differing  views....'",  no
federal  standard  of  appropriateness  being
required  (or  even  permitted).
Here
Petitioner  cannot  assert  that  his
sentence  constitutes  one  of  "those  modes
Or  acts  of  punishment  that  had  been
considered  cruel  and  unusual  at  the  time
that  the  Bill  of  Rights  was  adopted."
Stanford,  supra.

And  while  in  the
capital  sentencing  area  this  Court  has
cast  loose  from  history,  and  perhaps

-  25  =

-  26  =

irretrievably  so,  there  is  no  need  for
this  Court  to  continue,  on  scant
precedent,  to  do  so  with  regard  to
sentences  of  imprisonment,  particularly
given  that  standards  cannot  be  drawn
which  do  not,

in  the  end,

turn  on  the

preferences  of  a  majority  of  the  Court.
If  the  test  is  "evolving  standards  of
decency,"  then  the  Court  is  ill-equipped,

particularly  as  compared  to  a
legislature,  to  make  the  assessment  of
the  seriousness  of  a  crime  and  the
harshness  of  the  punishment,  particularly
armed  with  a  ratchet  which  turns  only  one
way.  Solem v_ Helm  should  be  abandoned,

and  the  sentence  here  affirmed,  with  no
proportionality  analysis  undertaken,  and
federal  review  of  state  sentences  of
imprisonment  on  the  grounds  of
"“excessiveness"  should  cease.

This

contemplates  that  which  cannot  occur.

In  his  work  Democracy
and  Distrust
(Harvard  University  Press:  1980),
Professor  John  Hart  Ely  pondered  the
constitutionality  of  a  hypothetical
statute  prohibiting  gall  bladder
operations  except  to  save  the  person's
life.
His  answer  was  that  the  statute

could  not  pass,

and  that  if  it  did  it
would  be  repealed  and  the  representatives
who  passed  it  possibly  impeached.

To  the
claim  that  perhaps  that  wouldn't  happen
either,  the  representatives  and  the
public  all  "just  acting  crazy,"  and  there

being  no  possibility  of  reasoning  with

them,  Professor  Hart  responded:

You  know  what  you're  telling  me?
that  you  don't  believe  in
democracy.
With  neuroses  like
yours,  I  sure  as  hell  would  be
worrying  about  problems  a  lot
less  rarified  than  substantive
versus  participational  review.
Like  whether  I'm  going  to  read
in  tomorrow's  paper  that  my  dog
is  under  consideration  for
Secretary  of  Agriculture.  Ely,
at  182.

:

Professor  Ely  concluded  that  "It  is  an

entirely  legitimate  response  to  the  gall
bladder  law  to  note  that  it  couldn't  pass

and  refuse  to  play  any  further."
In
fact,  he  continued,  "it  can  only  deform

enacted,  since  constitutional  law

appropriately  exists  for  the  situations
where  representative  government  cannot  be
trusted,  not  those  where  we  know  it  can."

Ely,  at  183  (emphasis  added).

This

language  is  precisely  appropriate  for  the

Quoting  from  the

original  panel  decision  the  court

observed  that:

Except  in  rare  cases,  the
murderer's  red  hand  falls  on  one

victim  only,  however
grim  the
blow;  but  the  foul  hand
of  the
drug  dealer  blights  life  after
life  and,  like  the  vampire  of
fable,  creates  others  in  its
owner's  evil  image--others  who
create  others  still,  across  our
land  and  down  our  generations,
sparing  not  even  the  unborn.  848
F  2d  at  504.

The  court  also  recognized  the  value

judgments  involved  in  "comparing  crimes,"

value  judgments  that  courts  are

ill-suited  to  make:

Nor  can  we  say,  attempting  as
best  we  can  to  compare  the  apple
of  aggravated  ki  napping,  the
orange  of  heroin  dealing  and  the
pear  of  aggravated  arson  with
each  other,  that  Terrebonne  ‘has
been  treated  in  the  same  manner
as,
than,
criminals  who  have  committed  far
more  serious  crimes.'
And  the
reason  we  cannot  do  so  is  that
it  is  far  from  clear  to  us  that
the  other  crimes  that  Louisiana
punishes  similarly  are  ‘far  more
serious.'  848  F  2d  at  504.

D.  Conclusion

In  summary,  then,  amicus  submits  the

following:

1)Solem

v_ Helm  should  be  overruled.

Comparing  other  jurisdictions  also,

the

Outside  the  capital  punishment  area

court  concluded  that  though  severe  the

sentence  was  not  "bizarre  or  outlandish,"

and  concluded  that  the  sentence  did  not

violate  the  Eighth  Amendment.

Amicus

submits  that  the  isolation  of  this  Court

also  does  not  allow  it  to  “touch  the

earthy"  with  regard  to  the  severity  of

the  crime  involved  here,

and  that  the

Court  should  not  undertake  to  do  so,  for

it  cannot  without  assuming  the  function

of  a  legislature.

proportionality  review  of  sentences

for  “excessiveness"  should  cease.

"Evolving  standards  of  decency  of  an

evolving  society"  are  best  expressed

through  the  elected  representatives

of  that  society  rather  than  the

judicial  branch  of  Government,

particularly  in  those  circumstances

where  life-tenured  federal  judges

are  otherwise  required  to

second-guess  the  decisions  of  State

legislators  as  to  the  standards  of

decency  that  are  evolving  with

regard  to  particular  offenses,

and

particularly  if  it  is  recognized

-  37 -

»  32 «©

that  standards  may  evolve  both  ways

"“excessiveness"  the  Michigan  statute

(that  is,  may  become  more  severe  for

passes  as  the  expression  of  a

certain  offenses).

2)If  the  Court  undertakes  a

proportionality  review,  then  under  a

"mature  society"  of  a  "decent"

standard.

This  review  is  undertaken

not  by  looking  at  the  individual

characteristics  of  the  offender

proportionality  review  for

(outside  tne  capital  sentencing  area

individualized  sentencing  is  not

required  by  the  Eighth  Amendment;  if

a  review  of  the  individual

characteristics  of  the  offender  were

undertaken,  it  would  provide

Petitioner  no  succor),  but  at  the

severity  of  the  offense  and  the

severity  of  the  punishment.

Given

the  massive  problem  of  drugs,

and

the  tremendous  harm  caused  by

amounts  exceeding  650  grams,

the

punishment  here  is  in  no  way

"bizaare"  or  "“outlandish."

,

in  fact,  most  appropriate.

-  39  -

CONCLUSION

Wherefore,  amicus  respectfully

requests  that  the  judgment  of  the

Michigan  Court of Appeals  be  affirmed.

Respectfully  submitted,

ROBERT  WEISS
President,
Prosecuting  Attorneys  Association
of  Michigan

JOHN  D.  O'HAIR
Prosecuting  Attorney
County  of  Wayne.

INTEREST  OF  THE  STATE  OF  MICHIGAN

This  case  presents  the  question  whether  a

Michigan  statute  mandating  a  life  sentence

without  parole  for  possssion  of  a  controlled

substance  in  the  amount  of  650  grams  or  more

imposes  cruel  and  unusual  punishment  under

the  Eighth  Amendment.

The  State  of  Michigan

has  a  compelling  interest  in  combating  drug

trafficking  in  order  to  reduce  drug  related

crime,  drug  addiction  and  related  problems.

One  form  the  State  Legislature  has  chosen  to

attack  these  problems  is  to  enact  statutory

provisions  requiring  convicted  drug  offenders

to  be  sentenced  to  mandatory  prison  terms  or

mandatory  minimum  terms.

The  mandatory  life

sentence  without

parole

for  possession  of

cocaine  in  an  amount  involving  650  or  more

grams  is  the  sentence  at  issue  in  this  case.

Mich.  Comp.  Laws  333.7403(2)(a)(i).

of=

_=

PENALTIES

MANDATORY
NECESSARILY
REJECT  THE  CONCEPT  OF  INDIVIDUALIZED
SENTENCING  WHICH  FORMS  THE
HISTORIC
PREMISE

INTERESTS  OF  AMICI  CURIAE

The  Washington  Legal  Foundation  (WLF)  is  a
nonprofit  law  and  policy  center  with  more  than  120.000
members  and  supporters  nationwide.  WLF  engages  in
litigation  and  administrative  proceedings  affecting
th
broad  public  interest,  and  has  a  particular  interest  in i
areas  of  criminal  justice,  drug  abuse  and  crime  victims’
rights.
In  that  regard,  WLF  has  Participated  as  a  party

a

In
and  Enmund  v.  Florida,  458  U.S.  782  (1982).
addition,  WLF  filed  a  sentencing  memorandum  with  the
District  Court  in  the  espionage  case

years  upon  the  defendant  such  that  he  would  serve  a  de
facto  sentence  of  life  without  parole.  The  term  of  360
years  with  no  parole  for  60  years  was  upheld  on  appeal

:  Be  for  Law  and  Order  (CLO)  is  a  grass-roots

CLO  supports  measures  in the

California  legislature  designed  to  strengthen  the  criminal

Families  and  Friends  of  Missing  Persons  and  Vio-
lent  Crime  Victims  is  a  national  organization  based  ix
Seattle,  Washington,  that  provides  assistance  to  the
families  of  kidnapped  children  and  victims  of  other
It  is  particularly  involved  with  efforts  to  pro-

tect  our  nation’s  youth  from  becoming  involved  in

;
guard  neighborhoods  from  drug  dealers.  .
The  Maryland  Coalition  Against  Crime  (MCAC)  is
based  in  Baltimore,  Maryland,  and  works  for  enactment
of  legislation  that  promotes  tougher  law  enforcement
measures  and  the  rights  of  crime  victims.  MCAC  is
currently  supporting  the  adoption  of  a  "Victims’  Rights
Amendment"  to  the  Maryland  Constitution.

‘

3

The  Parents  Association  to  Neutralize  Drug  and
Alcohol  Abuse  (PANDAA)  is  a  national  organization
devoted  to  eliminating  drug  use  among  young  people.
PANDAA  works  toward  this  end  by  sponsoring  anti-
drug  classes  and  seminars,  by  distributing  informational
materials  and  by  helping  families  with  drug  abuse  prob-
lems  in  finding  effective  treatment.

The  Stephanie  Roper  Committee,  Inc.  is  a  nonprofit
organization  based  in  Upper  Marlboro,  Maryland.
It
was  founded  in  1982  by  Roberta  Roper,  the  mother  of
Stephanie,  who  was  kidnapped,  raped,  tortured  and
brutally  murdered.
The  Committee  is  a  victims’  rights
advocacy  organization  which  has  supported  and  pro-
posed  legislation  responsive  to  the  needs  of  crime  vic-
tims.
The  Committee  has  previously  appeared  in  this
Court  as  amicus  curiae  in  Pennsylvania  y.  Davenport,
110  S.  Ct.  2126  (1990).

the

Amici  are

all  deeply  concemed  about

vast
human  destruction  caused  by  illegal  drugs,  and  support
stern  measures  designed  to  punish  drug  traffickers.
Amici  feel  that  criminals  of  petitioner’s  ilk  represent  one
of  the  most  serious  threats  to  American  society,  and
that  Michigan’s  mandatory  punishment  of  life  without
parole  for  large-scale  drug  dealing  represents  the  mea-
sured  and  proper  judgment  of  the  people  of  that  state.
This  brief  is  being  filed  with  the  consent  of  both
Parties.

STATEMENT  OF  THE  CASE

On  October  12,  1986  Ronald  Harmelin  was  arrested
in  Oak  Park,  Michigan  for  possession  of  illegal  drugs,
following  a  traffic  stop.
At  the  time  of  his  arrest,  he
was  a  one-man  drug  market.
From  his  person,  police
consficated
four
brass
cocaine
straws,  one  cocaine  spoon,  three
vials  containing  a
white  powder  of  suspected  cocaine,  twelve  Percodan

marijuana  cigarettes,

4

5

tablets,  twenty-five  tablets  of  Phendimetrazine  Tartrate,
a  Morotola  beeper,  a  .38  caliber  revolver,  and  nearly
During  an  inventory  search  of  his  car,
$600  in  cash.
police  later  found  a  bag  containing  672.5  grams  (about
one  and  a  half  pounds)  of  nearly  pure  cocaine,  $2,900
in  cash,  a  coded  address  book,  and  a  number  of
personal  toiletries.  Michigan  Department  of Corrections,
Presentence  Investigation  Report  on  Ronald  Harmelin,
April  27,  1987.

Petitioner  was  convicted  of  possession  of  over  650

by  the  Michigan  Court  of  Appeals  on  April  18,  1989,
and  leave  to  file  an  appeal  with  the  Michigan  Supreme
This  Court

cruel  and  unusual  punishment.

SUMMARY  OF  THE  ARGUMENT

The  fundamental  issue  in  this  case  is  whether  or
not  state  legislatures  have  the  power  to  prescribe  severe
Petitioner  is
penalties  for  serious  violations  of  the  law.
a  convicted  drug  trafficker  who  shares  a personal  Te-
sponsibility  for  the  thousands  of  deaths  in  this  nation
that  are  directly  linked  with  illegal  drugs. —  By  urging
reversal  of  the  sentence  in  his  case,  petitioner  would
have  this  Court  adopt  a  new,  extra-constitutional  rule
that  only  judges  --  and  not  legislatures  --  may  deter-

mine  appropriate  sentences  upon  persons  convicted  of
extremely  serious  crimes.

(e.g.,

offender

characteristics

In  our  view,  no  proportionality

analysis  under
Solem  v.  Helm,  463  U.S.  277  (1983)  is  required  at  all,
extended  or  otherwise,  for  patently  serious  offenses
involving  violence,  drugs,  and  similar  crimes,  regardless
of  the
of  the
first
offender),  and  regardless  of  what  other  punishments
may  or  could  have  been  meted  out  in  the  jurisdiction  in
question  or  in  other  jurisdictions  for  similar  crimes.
Rather,  such  analysis  is  constitutionally  required,  if  at
all,  only  where  the  crime  committed  is  a  minor,  non-
violent
crime
of  that
committed  in  Solem.
Otherwise,  lower  courts  would  be
faced
both
determinate  and  discretionary,  imposed  for  all  serious
crimes  by  resourceful  counsel  who,  like  counsel  in  the
instant  case,  present  statistical  comparisons  and  novel
hypotheticals
to  show  some  disparity
in  sentencing
practices.  The  result  of  such  judicial  exercise  would  be
the  risk  that  sentencing  decisions  will  be  reduced  to
determining  the  lowest  common  denominator  rather  than
allowing  the  states  the  flexibility  to  deal  severely  with
serious  criminal  behavior.

I.  PETITIONER’S  SFNTENCE  IS  NOT  SO  DIS-

In  Solem  vy.  Helm,

of  a

that  Helm’s  sentence

parole
imposed  on  a  person  who  was  convicted  for  merely
uttering  a  "no  account"  check  for  $100.
Since  the
defendant  in  that  case  had  six  prior  felony  convictions,
his  severe  sentence  was  authorized  by  a  recidivist
statute  of  South  Dakota.
Although  this  Court  had  pre-
viously  sustained  a  life  sentence  with  the  possibility  of
parole  for  a  similar  minor  non-violent  crime  committed
by  a  recidivist  in  Rummel  vy.  Estelle,  445  U.S.  263,  100
S.  Ct.  1133  (1980),  the  Solem  Court  nevertheless  struck
down  the  life  sentence  without  the  possibility  for  parole,
concluding
“significantly
disproportionate
therefore
prohibited  by the  Eighth  Amendment.”  463  U.S.  at  303
(emphasis  added).
In  so  doing,  the  Solem  Court  stated  that  it  was
guided  by  the  following  objective  factors:  1)  the  gravity
of  the  offense  and  the  harshness  of  the  penalty,  2)  a
comparison  of  the  sentences  imposed  on  other  criminals
in  the  same  jurisdiction,  and  3)  a  comparison  of  the
sentences  imposed  for  the  same  crime  in  other  Jurisidic-
tions.
Jd.  at  291.  Amici  will  focus  their  Solem  analysis
on  the  first  criterion,  especially  the  gravity  of  the
offense  prong,  which  in  our  view  so  far  outweighs  the
other  factors  as  to  make  this  Court’s  resolution  of  this
case  a  relatively  easy  matter.

Ronald  Harmelin  was  a  large-scale  drug  trafficker
who  delivered  narcotics  down  to  mid-level  dealers.“  He
has  had  no  lawful  source  of  income  for  many  years.
The  $15,000  bill
He  does  not  file  income  tax  returns.
for  his  trial  attorney  was  paid  in  cash  by an  “associate.”
The  pound  and  a  half  of  undiluted  cocaine  in  his
possession  at  the  time  of  arrest  was  far  more  than  a
normal  individual  could  consume,  and  had a street  value
His  self-serving
of  between  $60,000  and  $100,000.
claim  is  that  he  was  a  “mule  of  transport,”  and  not  a
drug  dealer.’  Even  if this  Court  takes  his  claim  at  face
value,  petitioner  was  at  the  very  least  a  courier  of
death.  Viewing  this  case  in  the  light  most  favorable  to
the  government,’  this  Court  can  easily  conclude  that
Petitioner  is  not  some  poor  illegal  immigrant  used  as  an

>  In  his  brief,  petitioner  has  admitted  that  his  crime  was  more
than  simply  one  of  possession  of  narcotics.  By  claiming  that  his
role  was  one  of  a  courier,  Pet.  Brief  at  4,  17,  he  necessarily
concedes  that  he  possessed  cocaine  with  intent  to  distribute  to
Petitioner's  self-professed  intention  to  participate  in  the
others.
Thus,
trafficking  of  cocaine  makes  his  crime  even  more  serious.
while  he  was  not  charged  with  distribution  under  Michigan  law,  it
is  clear  that  his  actual  crime  would  nevertheless  deserve  such  a
punishment.  See  Young  v.  Miller,  883  F.2d  1276  (6th  Cir.  1989).

June  1990.

March  2,  1988.

"GAO  Cites  Cost  of Drug  Use  in  US.,"  The  Washington  Post,

robbed  for  the  money  to  buy  dope,  from  the  teenagers
mowed  down  in  drive-by  shootings.  to  the  women  who
engage  in  prostitution  as  a  means  to  support  their  drug
habit.

Petitioner’s  crime  was  not  directed  solely  against
one  person,  but  rather  it  was  directed  against  all  the
people  of  Michigan,  who  every  day  live  in  the  ominous
Shadow  of  illegal  drugs.
Drug  dealing  --  the  crime
petitioner  is  guilty  of  --  is  a  crime  against  humanity.
Former  Associate  Justice  Powell  summed  it  up  well
when  he  said  that  “A  professional  seller  of  addictive
drugs  may  inflict  greater  bodily  harm  upon  members  of
society  than  the  person  who  commits  a  single  assault."
Rummel  vy.  Estelle,  445  U.S.  263,  295,  n.  12  (1980)
(Powell,  J.,  dissenting).

There  is  a  clear  nexus  between  illegal  drugs  and
other  forms  of  violent  criminal  behavior.
There  is  an
indisputable  connection  between  the  business  of  drug
dealers  and  the  anguish  of  children  born  addicted  to
cocaine.  There  is  a  direct  correlation  between  petitioner
and  the  suffering  of  innocents.
For  the  victims  of
illegal  drugs,  Michigan  is  simply  doing  justice  from
both  a  retributive  as  well  as  deterrent  perspective  by
keeping  Ronald  Harmelin  behind  bars  for  the  rest  of  his
life.

B.  Severity  of  Petitioner’s  Punishment.

Petitioner  makes  a  highly  emotional  argument  that
his  sentence  of  life  without  parole  really  "means  life
without  parole"  and  that  because  of  the  punishment
meted  out  on  him,  his  life  has  been  "shattered  beyond
all  hope."  Pet.  Brief  at  5, 8.

Petitioner  would  like  this  Court  to  believe  that  his
sentence  means  that  he  will  die  in  prison.
While  peti-
tioner  may  not  be  paroled  in  the  same  manner  as  other
Michigan  prisoners,  the  governor  of  Michigan  retains

16

the  power  to  commute  his  sentence  to  one  that  includes
Michigan’s  drug  lifer  law  is  only  twelve  years

In  other  states  that  long  ago  adopted  life  without

Nebraska  and  Massachusetts,  persons  sentenced  to  “life
without  parole"  routinely  have  their  sentences  commuted
to  life  with  possibility  of  parole  after  lengthy  periods  of

Petitioner  also  asserts  that  he  has  been  deprived  of
all  hope,  and  that  his  life  has  been  destroyed  by  the
sentence  imposed  upon  him.  Amici  are  not  sympathetic.
Nowhere  in  the  Constitution  is  this  Court  given  the
warrant  to  reduce  a  sentence  because  the  justices  may
feel  sorry  for  a  convicted  criminal.  Any  sympathy  this
Court  may  feel  would  be  better  directed  toward  the
innocent  victims  whose  lives  have  been  destroyed  by
illegal  drugs.

If  life  without  the  possibility  of  parole  is  indeed  a
qualitively  different  aspect  of  the  punishment  which
makes  this  sentence  more  severe  than  all  others  except
the  death  penalty,  then  courts  would  also  have  to  place
de facto  life  sentences  in  that  category  as  well.  A  60-

C.  Severity  of  the  Punishment  in  Other  Juris-

dictions.

In  his  listing  of  other  states’  maximum  sentences
for  possession  of  650  grams  of  cocaine,  petitioner  often
compares  apples  with  oranges.
Michigan’s  drug  lifer
law  is designed  to  punish  not  solely  the  possession  of
cocaine,  but  also  possession  with  intent  to  distribute.

*  Petitioner  has  admitted  in  his  own  brief  that  he  di d not  merel

and  transfer  the  drugs  to  others.  See  note  5,  supra.  Thus,  the

This  argument  is  utterly  without  merit.
Neither  the
Constitution  nor  the  decisions  of  this  Court  require  the
tailoring  of  non-capital  sentences  to  the  individual  de-
fendant.

:

Petitioner  spends  a  large  portion  of  his  brief  trying
to  establish  the  curious  notion  that  a  sentence  of  life  in
prison  without  parole  --  with  the  people  of  Michigan
paying  for  his  room,  board,  medical  care,  and  recrea-
tional  facilities
.5  equivalent  to  a  sentence  of  death
by  electrocution.
Pet.  Brief  at  21-27.
Having  made
this  argument,  petitioner  would  then  have  this  court
fashion
that  would

non-capital

death  sentences.

a  new,  extra-constitutional

of  tough

imposition

the

-

This  Court  has  applied  the  requirement  of  individ-
ualized  sentencing  only  in  the  context  of  death  penalty
cases.
See  e.g.  Gregg  v.  Georgia,  428  U.S.  153  (1976):
Woodson  v.  North  Carolina,  428  U.S.  280  (1976):
Roberts  (Stanislaus)  v.  Louisiana,  428  U.S.  325  (1976);
Lockett  v.  Ohio,  438  U.S.  586  (1978).

Chief  Justice  Burger’s  opinion  in  Lockett  is  espe-

cially  instructive  on  this  point:

[Ljegislatures  remain  free  to  decide  how
much  discretion  in  sentencing  should  be  re-
posed  in  the  judge  or  jury  in  noncapital  cases...
We  recognize  that,  in  noncapital  cases,  the
established  practice  of  individualized  sentences
rests  not  on  constitutional  commands,  but  on
public  policy  enacted  into  statutes.

Id.  at  604-05.

Adhering  to  this  Court’s  decision  in  Solem,  lower

made  by non-capital  defendants  who  demand  individual-

for  capital  defendants.  The  United  States  Court  of  Ap-.
peals  for  the  Second  Circuit,  for  example,  recently  re-

attacked  the  non-individualized  nature  of  his  ‘sentence,
as mandated  by  the  Sentencing  Guidelines.

the  circumstances  of  his  crime.

OF AMICUS  CURIAE
INTEREST

Amicus  curiae  asks  this  Court  to  review

its  decision  in  Solem v.  Helm,  463  U.S.  277

(1983),  because  that  decision  has  resulted

in  flagrant  overreaching  by  state  and

federal  courts  in  redefining  crimes  and

reestablishing  punishments  therefor;  it  has

allowed  courts  to  act  as  superlegislatures;

and  it  has  wreaked  havoc  upon  law  enforcement

efforts,  which  must  thereafter  be

second-guessed  by  courts  on  the  charge  of

cruel  and  unusual  punishment.

The  decision

has  created  conflicts  among  the  various

courts  of  the  United  States,  because  the

purportedly  objective  criteria  set  forth  in

that  decision  actually  are  subjective  and

result  in  abrogating  legislative  intent  when

sentences  solely  of  imprisonment  are

reviewed  on  Eighth  Amendment  grounds.

The

Arizona  Supreme  Court  has  recently

misapplied  Solem v.  Helm  by  overturning  a

sentence  of  40  years’  imprisonment  for  two

a

counts  of  sexual  conduct  with  a  minor,

ole

a Oh

finding  that  sentence  to  be

unconstitutionally  cruel  and  unusual.

State

Ve  S TORS...  AEEBs.  acne

Tee  F.26,  692

(1990).

Amicus  curiae  has  asked  this  Court

to  review  that  decision  in  a  petition  for

certiorari  filed  September  13,  1990.

OF  ARGUMENT
SUMMARY

Michigan's  statute  required  that  the

sentencer  sentence  petitioner  to  a  mandatory

life  sentence  without  the  possibility  of

parol:  for  possessing  in  excess  of  650  grams

of  coc’ine.

This  Court  has  held  that,  in

s

reviewing  the  constitutionality  of  a

sentence,  objective  ertiucts  must  be

*

considered;  however,  this  "objective

criteria”  is  not  objective,  and  their  use

results  in  conflicting  eighth  amendment

analyses.

Mandated  consistency  of  sentences

for  like  crimes  destroys  all  notions  of

federalism  inherent  in  the  United  States

Constitution,  and  permits  courts  to

redetermine  sentences  determined  appropriate

by  the  constituents  of  the  states  enacting

legislation.

aie

Amicus  submits  that  this  Court  should

overrule  Solem v.  Helm  and  allow  states  to

ne a —-
ene

hie ends

determine  what  acts  should  be  criminal  and

what  punishments  are  appropriate  for  the

commission  of  those  acts,  without  resorting

to  a  national  standard,  and  without  imposing

techniques  of  comparison  that  destroy  all

notions  of  federalism.

Except  in  those  rare

instances  contemplated  by  the  eighth

amendment,  courts  should  not  undertake

proportionality  review  of  sentences  solely

of  imprisonment.

IN  MAKING  A  BREAK  FROM  TRADITIONAL
EIGHTH  AMENDMENT!  ANALYSIS  BY
EXAMINING  SENTENCES  OF  IMPRISONMENT,
AS  WELL  AS  THE  MANNER  IN  WHICH
SENTENCES  ARE  CARRIED  OUT,  SOLEM  v.
HELM  SET  FORTH  AN  UNWORKABLE  TEST  TO
DETERMINE  WHETHER  A  GIVEN  SENTENCE  IS
PROPORTIONAL  TO  A  GIVEN  CRIME;  THIS
HOLDING  CREATED  A  JUDICIAL
ABERRATION,  WHICH  THE  DISSENT  FORESAW
WOULD  RESULT  IN  CONSTITUTIONAL  CHAOS,
A  RESULT  THAT  HAS  COME  TO  PASS.

EVOLUTION
THAT  OCCURRED  BETWEEN
RUMMEL  v.  ESTELLE  AND  SOLEM  v.  HELM.

In  Rummel  v.  Estelle,  445  U.S.  263

(1980),  this  Court  specifically  rejected

the  invitation  to  extend  eighth  amendment

analysis  to  challenges  solely  of  sentences

of  imprisonment,  finding:

Given  the  unique  nature  of  the
punishments  considered  in  Weems  [v.
United  States]  and  in  the  death
penalty  cases,  one  could  argue
without  fear  of  contradiction  by  any
decision  of  this  Court  that  for

1.

The  Eighth  Amendment  to  the
United  States  Constitution  provides:

Excessive  bail  shall  not  be
required,  nor  excessive  fines
imposed,  nor  cruel  and  unusual
punishments  inflicted.

atin

crimes  concededly  classified
andclassifiable  as  felonies,  that
is,  as  punishable  by  significant
terms  of  imprisonment  in  a  state
penitentiary,  the  length  of  the
sentence  actually  imposed  is  purely
a  matter  of  legislative  prerogative.

s
aan

Rummel,  445  U.S.  at  274.

This  Court  found

it  difficult,  if  not  impossible,  to

differentiate  between  one  term  of  years  for

en ee ae ee ee ee re

a  specific  crime  and  another  term  of  years,

and  refused  to  engage  in  that  “line-drawing”

that  is  peculiarly  the  province  of  the

legislatures.

Jd.  at  275.

Rummel

specifically  asked  the  Court  to  apply

“objective”  criteria  to  determine  that  his

sentence  was  out  of  sync  with  the  majority

of  states  whose  sentencing  provisions  were

markedly  different  from  the  recidivist

statute  under  which  he  was  sentenced  to

life  imprisonment.

The  majority  of  this

Court  rejected  these  objective  criteria,

Saying:

Me se
Anicttcel

Absent  a  constitutionally  imposed
uniformity  inimical  to  traditional
notions  of  federalism,  some  State
will  always  bear  the  distinction
of  treating  particular  *ffenders
more  severely  than  any  other  State.

-

Id.  at  283.

Three  years  later,  this  Court  issued  its

opinion  in  Solem v.  Helm,  463  U.S.  277

(1983),  in  which  it  appeared  to  embrace  the

objective  criteria  it  had  recently  rejected

in  Rummel,  without  overruling  that  decision.

In  Solem,  this  Court  set  forth  an  “objective”

test  to  determine  whether  a  given  sentence

ee ee re

was  proportional  to  a  given  crime.

Factors

that  a  court  should  consider  include:

(1)

Looking  at  the  gravity  of
the  offense  and  the  harshness  of
the  penalty;

(2)

Comparing  the  sentences

imposed  on  other  criminals  in  the
same  jurisdiction;  and

(3)

Comparing  the  sentences
imposed  for  the  same  crimes  in
other  jurisdictions.

Id.  at  290-91.

The  Court  completely

unraveled  its  prior  precedent  and  opened  the

doors  to  proportionality  challenges  such  as

the  one  before  this  Court.

CHIEF  JUSTICE  BURGER’S  PROPHECY
HAS  BECOME  REALITY.

|

The  controlling  law  governing
this  case  is  crystal  clear,  but
today  the  Court  blithely  discards
any  concept  of  stare  decisis,
trespasses  gravely  on  the
authority  of  the  states,  and
distorts  the  concept  of
proportionality  of  punishment  by
tearing  it  from  its  moorings  in
Capital  cases.

proportional,  undermine  the  authority

all  sentences  of  imprisonment  to

appellate  scrutiny.

speculative  at  best.
