There  is

no  meanirg.ul  way  to  measure  the  weight

to  be  given  to  certain  offenses,  or  the

legislative  intent  behind  the  enactment

of  certain  penalties.

The  Chief  Justice

feared  that  the  majority's  decision  would

flood  appellate  cofirts  with  cases

challenging  sentences  on  eighth  amendment

grounds  and  “administer  the  coup  de  grace

to  the  courts  of  appeals  as  we  know  them.”

Solem v.  Helm,  463  U.S.  at  316.

As  the

statistics  reported  below  demonstrate,

the  Chief  Justice's  fears  have  indeed

become  a  conkdéy,  as  the  appellate  courts

have  been  flooded  with  challenges  to  the

proportionality  of  sentences,  following

the  Solem v.  Helm  decision.”

However,  actual  comparisons  among

states  are  impossible,  given  the

Numerous

factors  play  upon  what  any  given

defendant  may  receive  as  a  sentence  for  a

Similar  crime.

These  variables  and

permutations  complicate  and  obscure  any

meaningful  analysis,  and  demonstrate  the

apparent  that  various  state  and  federal
courts  interpret  the  mandate  of  Solem
differently.
Some  interpret  the  case  to
require  review  only  of  sentences  of  life
imprisonment  without  the  possibility  of
parole  (Maddox
v.  State,  502  So.  2d  790
(Ala.  1986);  McLester
So.  2d  870,  873-75  n.2  (Ala.  Crim.  App.
1984);  Webb v.  State,  539  So.  2d  343
(Ala.  Crim.  App.  1987);  Donahoo v.
State,  505  So.  2d  1067  (Ala.  Crim.  App.
1986).

while  others  seem  to  put  each  sentence

lack  of  objectivity  inherent  in  the  tests

outlined  by  the  Solem  Court.

C.  COURTS  HAVE PLACED  DIFFERENT

INTERPRETATIONS
UPON  THE  DOCTRINE

REQUIRED  THEREUNDER  IMPOSSIBLE.

As  mentioned  earlier,  there  is  a  vast

uncertainty  among  state  and  federal

pit

courts  about  the  meaning  of  the  tests  set

forth  in  Solem 7.  Helm,  and  the  cases  to

which  they  apply.

In  Marrero v.

Wainwright,  823  F.2d  1468,  1470  (llth

Cir.  1987),  the  Eleventh  Circuit  Court  of

Appeals  rejected  the  argument  propounded

by  the  government  that  only  those  cases

involving  ineligibility  for  parole  were

subject  to  review  under  Solem.

In  contrast  to  the  Marrero  decision,

the  Alabama  courts  and  the  Fourth  Circuit

Court  of  Appeals  have  held  that  the  Solem

v.  Helm  analysis  is  to  be  applied  only  in

cases  involving  sentences  of  life  without

the  possibility  of  parole.

See

footnote  2,  supra.

=)

In  the  case  before  this  Court,  the

Michigan  Court  of  Appeals  found  it

unnecessary  even  to  address  the

applicability  of  Solem  v.  Helm,  or  to  put

the  facts  of  this  case  through  the  rigors

of  Solem's  analysis.

In  Maryland,  when  applying  the  second

prong  of  the  Solem v.  Helm  test,  the

court  compares  defendants  who  have

committed  crimes  within  the  same  category

of  offenses.  See  State  v.  Davis,  310  Md.

611,  530  A.2d  1223  (Md.  1987).

The

Maryland  court  looks  at  particular

categories  of  crimes,  and  the  punishments

set  forth  therefor.

If  all  defendants

who  commit  a  particular  class  of  crime

are  sentenced  similarly,  the  inquiry  ends

under  this  prong  of  the  Solem  test.

The

Ninth  Circuit  Court  of  Appeals  has

interpreted  Solem  and  upheld  sentences

challenged  under  the  second  prong,

because  all  offenders  who  committed

Crimes  while  released  from  confinement

abBn

v.  Bramlett,
were  treated  alike.  Cocio

872  F.2d  889  (9th  Cir.  1989).

By

contrast,  the  Arizona  Supreme  Court  held

that  a  court  must  look  at  punishments  for

any  and  all  crimes  that  can  be  committed

within  a  jurisdiction  to  meet  the  Solem

test.  State

v.  Bartlett,  792  P.2d  692,

at  699  (Ariz.  1990).

Under  the  third  prong  of  Solem,  both

Maryland  and  the  fourth  circuit  subscribe

to  the  interpretation  that  Solem  requires

a  comparison  of  what  sentences  could  be

imposed  in  other  jurisdictions,  not

merely  what  sentences  actually  have  been

imposed.  State

v. Davis;  United  States

v. Rhodes;  Teeter

v,  State,  499  A.2d  503

(Md.  App.  1985).

By  contrast,  the

Arizona  court  stated  it  must  look  at

other  sentences  actually  imposed,  to  meet

a

the  Solem  test.

Bartlett,  792  P.2d  at

700-03.

In  Bartlett,  the  Arizona  Supreme  Court

overturned  a  sentence  of  40  years’

~~

imprisonment  for  two  counts  of  sexual

conduct  with  a  minor,  after  applying  the

"objective"  criteria  of  Solem.

In

analyzing  those  factors,  the  court  redrew

lines  set  by  the  legislature  in

determining  the  severity  of  the  offense;

chose  a  non-related  group  of  offenders  in

Arizona  to  compare  sentences  of  varying

severity;  and  limited  its  inquiry  under

the  third  prong  of  the  test  to  cover  only

sentences  actually  imposed  in  other

jurisdictions  for  identical  crimes,  where

factors  of  mandatoriness,  parole

eligibility,  age  of  victims,  repeat

offender  classifications,  and  identity  of

elements  all  coalesced.

State  v.

Bartlett,  792  P.2d  692.

It  then

formulated  a  remedy  that  has  resulted  in

the  defendant  being  sentenced  to  less

than  the  statutory  minimum  for  the  crimes

he  committed.

Even  though  this  Court  has  said  that

successful  challenges  to  the

-14-

proportionality  of  particular  sentences

are  “exceedingly  rare,”  courts  are

nevertheless  succumbing  to  challenges  of

sentences  solely  of  imprisonment,  without

giving  the  deference  required  by  this

Court  and  the  United  States  Constitution

to  the  legislatures  who  define  crimes  and

the  punishments  therefor.

The  result  is  that  courts  are  now

allowed  to  redefine  crimes  and  in  essence

determine  whether  the  conduct  proscribed

by  one  state  also  may  be  proscribed  by

others.

Even  if  a  court  finds  that

certain  conduct  may  be  proscribed  by  one

state  while  it  is  lawful  in  another,

under  a  Solem v.  Helm  challenge,  che

sentence  may  be  found  unconstitutional,

because  there  are  only  a  few  other  states

that  prohibit  the  conduct.

See,  e.g.,

Bowers

v.  Hardwick,  478  U.S.  186  (1986)

(Georgia's  prohibition  of  consensual

sodomy  does  not  violate  rights  of  privacy

and  sodomy  may  be  made  criminal).

Thus,

-15-

while  the  conduct  may  be  lawfully

prohibited,  under  a  Solem v.  Helm

analysis,  it  may  not  be  constitutionally

punished,  because  it  may  fail  under

Solem's  third  prong.

Petitioner  misdirects  this  Court  when

en ee ee ee

it  asks  it  to  compare  death  penalty

analysis  to  the  analysis  in  cases  such  as

his.

It  is  instructive,  however,  to  note

that,  in  death  penalty  cases,  this  Court

has  repeatedly  held  that  a  proportionality

review  (such  as  Solem  mandated)  is

unnecessary.  McClesky

v.  Kemp,  481  U.S.

279  (1987);  Pulley

v.  Harris,  465  U.S.  37

(1984).

It  seems  odd  that,  in  cases  where

the  ultimate  punishment  is  imposed,

proportionality  review  is  not  required;

however,  in  cases  not  involving  the  death

penalty,  but  subject  to  great  variations

in  punishments  for  differently  defined

crimes,  such  analysis  is  required.

olGe

It  is  time  for  this  Court  to  review  its

decision  in  Solem,  because  of  the  effect

that  decision  is  having  on  law

enforcement,  and  because  of  the  judicial

activism  that  it  is  fostering.

As  a

result  of  the  opinion,  great  conflict  now

exists  across  the  country  among  courts

regarding  the  appropriate  cases  to  which

to  apply  Solem v.  Helm,  and  in  cases  in

which  that  doctrine  is  "applied,"

inconsistent  results  are  occurring.

In

its  present  framework,  Solem v,  Helm  is

unworkable,  and  either  should  be  modified

Or  overruled.

CONCLUSION

Courts  are  misinterpreting  and

misapplying  the  doctrine  set  forth  in

Solem v.  Helm.

They  are  using  that

opinion  as  a  vehicle  through  which  to

change  judicially  unpopular  laws  and

mandatory  sentences  required  thereunder.

Because  the  criteria  are  really

subjective,  judges  have  no  guidance  in

= =

applying  them  in  given  situations.

Thus,

amicus  asks  that  this  Court  overrule

Solem v.  Helm,  or  modify  it  to  afford

litigants  needed  guidance  in  doing

meaningful  analyses  under  the  eighth

amendment.

Amicus  curiae  asks  this  Court  to  affirm

the  judgment  of  the  Michigan  Court  of

Appeals.

Respectfully  submitted,

ROBERT  K.  CORBIN
Attorney  General
